Dislike and Divine Will: Intellectual vs. Legal Dislike
Sanusi distinguishes intellectual and legal dislike and proves the impossibility of the former.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why intellectual dislike (absence of will) is impossible for Allah, while legal dislike does not contradict His creative act.

Core Idea
Allah's will is necessary; intellectual dislike cannot be, but legal dislike is compatible with creation.

This lesson unpacks a key distinction in Islamic theology: the difference between intellectual dislike (absence of will) and legal dislike (a ruling). The passage from Sanusi clarifies that Allah cannot create something while disliking it intellectually, but He can and does create legally disliked actions, all by His will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Sanusi first states the impossibility then makes a distinction.
The distinction between intellectual and legal dislike is central.
2
Identify the two types of dislike
Find where Sanusi defines intellectual dislike (absence of will) and legal dislike (a ruling). Note why the first is impossible and the second is not.
3
Understand the command-will relationship
Examine the examples of Abu Bakr and Abu Jahl. Sanusi shows that command and will do not always go together. Trace how this supports the compatibility of legal dislike with creation.
Ahl al-Sunnah affirm general and specific relationship between command and will.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Consider the misconception that Allah cannot create anything He dislikes. The passage shows this is only true for intellectual dislike.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إيجاد شيء من العالم مع كراهته لوجوده فهو محال، لأن الكراهة ضد الإرادة والإرادة واجبة الله تعالى والكراهة مستحيلة على الله والمراد بها هي الكراهة العقلية التي هي عدم الإرادة. وأما الكراهة الشرعية التي هي إحدى الأحكام الخمسة فلا تنافي الإيجاد وهي - أي المكروهات على هذا المعنى - واقعة بإرادة الله تعالى لأنه لو لم يردها لما وقعت، إذ لا يكون في ملكه تعالى ما لا يريد ولم يأمر بها إذ لا ملازمة بين الأمر والإرادة عند أهل السنة، بل بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه فيتواردان في إيمان أبي بكر مثلاً فإنه مأمور به ومراد الله، وتنفرد الإرادة في كفر أبي جهل وغيره من الكفار وينفرد الأمر في الإيمان منهم، فهم مأمورون بالإيمان ولم يُرِدِ الله تعالى وقوعه منهم لعلمه بعدم إرادتهم لذلك. ويستحيل على الله تعالى الذهول وهو غيبوبة الشيء بعد العلم به، ويستحيل على الله تعالى الغفلة حالة إيجاده، والغفلة هي عدم العلم بالشيء مطلقا، أي إنه يستحيل على الله أن يوجد شيئا من العالم مع الغفلة أو الذهول. وقيل الذهول أعم من الغفلة لأن الغفلة زوال الشيء من المدركة مع بقائه في الحافظة، والذهول زواله من المدركة مطلقاً، وعلى هذا فالسهو مرادف للغفلة، كما يؤخذ من القاموس حيث قال غفل عنه تركه وسها عنه، وأما النسيان فهو أخص من الذهول لأنه زوال الشيء من الحافظة والمدركة معاً. ووجه منافاة كل من الذهول والغفلة للإرادة أنهما منافيان للعلم، وكل ما كان منافياً للعلم كان منافياً للإرادة، فهما منافيان للإرادة بواسطة منافاتهما للعلم.
As for bringing into existence something from the world with His dislike for its existence, it is impossible because dislike is the opposite of will (al-irādah), and will is necessary for Allah, and dislike is impossible for Allah. What is meant by it here is intellectual dislike (al-karāhah al-‘aqliyyah), which is the absence of will. As for legal dislike (al-karāhah al-shar‘iyyah), which is one of the five rulings, it does not contradict bringing into existence. That is, the disliked acts (al-makrūhāt) in this sense occur by the will of Allah, because if He did not will them, they would not occur, since nothing occurs in His dominion that He does not will. And He did not command them, because there is no necessary correlation between command and will according to Ahl al-Sunnah; rather between them is general and specific in some respects. So they coincide in the faith of Abū Bakr, for example, for it is commanded and willed by Allah. Will is unique in the disbelief of Abū Jahl and other disbelievers, and command is unique in the faith from them, for they are commanded with faith and Allah did not will its occurrence from them because of His knowledge of their lack of will for it. And forgetfulness (al-dhuhūl) is impossible for Allah, which is the disappearance of a thing after knowledge of it. And negligence (al-ghaflah) is impossible for Allah during His act of bringing into existence, and negligence is the absence of knowledge of a thing absolutely. That is, it is impossible for Allah to bring into existence anything from the world with negligence or forgetfulness. And it is said: forgetfulness is more general than negligence because negligence is the departure of a thing from the perception while it remains in memory, and forgetfulness is its departure from the perception absolutely. On this, inadvertence (al-sahw) is synonymous with negligence, as taken from al-Qāmūs: 'he was heedless of it: he left it and was inadvertent about it.' As for forgetfulness (al-nisyān), it is more specific than forgetfulness (al-dhuhūl) because it is the departure of a thing from both memory and perception together. And the aspect of the contradiction of each of forgetfulness and negligence to will is that they contradict knowledge, and everything that contradicts knowledge contradicts will, so they contradict will through their contradiction of knowledge.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and proof. It begins by stating that creating anything while Allah dislikes its existence is impossible, because dislike (karāhah) is the opposite of will (irādah), and will is necessary for Allah, so dislike cannot be attributed to Him. It clarifies that the dislike intended here is 'intellectual dislike' (al-karāhah al-‘aqliyyah), which simply means the absence of will. Then it distinguishes this from 'legal dislike' (al-karāhah al-shar‘iyyah), which is one of the five legal rulings (obligatory, recommended, prohibited, discouraged, permissible). Legal dislike does not prevent Allah from creating that thing; for example, He creates the actions of sinful people even though He legally dislikes them. The text explains that such things occur by His will, because nothing occurs in His dominion without His will. However, He does not command them, and there is no necessary link between command and will according to Ahl al-Sunnah. The relationship is one of general and specific in some respects: they coincide in the case of the believer's faith (commanded and willed), will alone applies to the disbeliever's disbelief (not commanded but willed), and command alone applies to the disbeliever's faith (commanded but not willed because Allah knows they will not choose it). Next, the passage proves the impossibility of forgetfulness (dhuhūl) and negligence (ghaflah) for Allah. Forgetfulness is the disappearance of something after having known it. Negligence is absolute absence of knowledge. Both are impossible for Allah because they contradict knowledge, and knowledge is necessary for Allah. Since they contradict knowledge, they also contradict will (because will requires knowledge). The passage then provides lexical distinctions: forgetfulness (dhuhūl) is more general than negligence (ghaflah) – negligence is when something leaves the active perception but remains in memory, while forgetfulness is when it leaves perception entirely. Inadvertence (sahw) is synonymous with negligence. Forgetfulness (nisyān) is more specific than dhuhūl, as it involves loss from both memory and perception. All forms contradict knowledge, hence contradict will.

Key Terms
الكراهة (al-karāhah)
الإرادة (al-irādah)
الكراهة العقلية (al-karāhah al-‘aqliyyah)
الكراهة الشرعية (al-karāhah al-shar‘iyyah)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that Allah's legal 'dislike' of an action prevents Him from creating it.
Correction: Passage distinguishes intellectual dislike (absence of will, impossible) from legal dislike (a ruling, compatible with creation). Legally disliked actions still occur by Allah's will.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it impossible for Allah to create something with 'dislike'?
A) Because dislike contradicts His mercy.
B) Because the dislike intended here is intellectual, which is absence of will, and will is necessary for Allah.
C) Because legal dislike prevents creation entirely.
D) Because Allah never creates anything that is disliked by Him in any sense.

Key Takeaway
Allah's will encompasses all creation; His legal dislike does not negate His willing those actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience dislike for something that has happened in your life, do you recognize that Allah's will is behind it? How does that change your response to that event?
?
Think of an action you know Allah legally dislikes but which still occurs in the world. Does knowing that He wills its existence (for wise reasons) affect how you view His sovereignty and justice? How can this trust reshape your heart when you see evil?
?
In what specific area of your life have you been resisting Allah's decree because you 'dislike' it? How can the truth that Allah's will is perfect and all-encompassing lead you to surrender that area to Him today?

The Impossibility of Forgetfulness and Negligence for Allah
Sanusi proves the impossibility of dhuhul and ghaflah and distinguishes related terms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why forgetfulness (dhuhul) and negligence (ghaflah) are impossible for Allah, and how they relate to knowledge and will.

Core Idea
Allah’s knowledge is perfect; forgetfulness and negligence contradict that, making them impossible.

This lesson addresses two attributes that cannot apply to Allah: forgetfulness (dhuhul) and negligence (ghaflah). Sanusi proves their impossibility by linking them to knowledge, and provides precise lexical distinctions between related terms like sahw and nisyan.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the definitions of dhuhul and ghaflah, and the final proof linking them to knowledge and will.
Focus on the logical chain: dhuhul/ghaflah contradict knowledge → knowledge is necessary → therefore impossible.
2
Identify the definitions and proof
Find the definitions of dhuhul (disappearance after knowledge) and ghaflah (absolute lack of knowledge). Understand why each is impossible for Allah.
3
Examine the lexical distinctions
Note how Sanusi distinguishes dhuhul, ghaflah, sahw, and nisyan. Understand the hierarchy: dhuhul is more general than ghaflah; sahw is synonymous with ghaflah; nisyan is more specific than dhuhul.
These distinctions help precisely define what is impossible for Allah.
4
Reflect on the relationship to will
The passage concludes that dhuhul and ghaflah contradict will because they contradict knowledge. Trace this reasoning.
This connects back to the broader topic of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
Why do forgetfulness (dhuhul) and negligence (ghaflah) contradict the divine will?
A) Because they directly oppose Allah's mercy.
B) Because they contradict knowledge, and will depends on knowledge.
C) Because they are temporary states.
D) Because they are not mentioned in the Quran.

Key Takeaway
Allah's knowledge is perfect and perpetual; any form of forgetfulness or negligence is impossible and would contradict His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that Allah never forgets even a single atom of your deeds. Then why do you still commit sins in secret, as if He is unaware? What would change in your daily actions if you lived with the certainty that His knowledge is perfect and perpetual?
?
If Allah's will depends on perfect knowledge, then His commands to you are never given in negligence. When you delay or ignore an obligation, are you not acting as if Allah might have 'forgotten' that command? How can you train yourself to respond with immediate obedience?
?
The passage says forgetfulness (dhuhūl) is the disappearance of something after knowing it. Your mind often forgets Allah and His greatness. But He never forgets you. How does this asymmetry make you feel, and what will you do today to make His remembrance more constant in your heart?

Proof of the Impossibility of Incapability in God
Sanusi proves the impossibility of divine incapability through a logical dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that proves incapacity cannot be attributed to God, and how each branch leads to an absurdity.

Core Idea
Divine incapacity is impossible because it leads to contradictions in every scenario.

Sanusi now demonstrates that the attribute of incapacity (al-ʿajz) cannot be ascribed to God. He constructs a rigorous dilemma: if incapacity were possible, it would have to be either eternal or temporal, and both options lead to impossible consequences. This proof reinforces God's absolute power (qudra) as a necessary attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof passage
Read the Arabic passage slowly, noting the logical structure. Then read the literal translation to grasp the argument.
The passage begins with 'عنه لأنه لو اتصف...' and presents a dilemma.
2
Identify the two branches
Notice that incapacity is split into two possibilities: eternal (qadīm) or originated (ḥādith). Each branch is examined separately.
This is a classic kalam dilemma (taqsīm) that covers all possibilities.
3
Trace the eternal branch
If incapacity were eternal, it could not cease, so God would always be incapable—meaning He lacks power. But the world exists, requiring a powerful creator, so this branch is false.
The argument uses observation (mushāhada) as evidence.
4
Trace the originated branch
If incapacity is temporal, either it coexists with power (impossible: opposites) or it exists without power (then its temporal origination implies the subject's temporality, negating God's eternity and thus the world's existence). Both sub-branches lead to impossibility.
This branch also concludes with the necessity of the world's negation, which is false.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Since all possibilities lead to absurdity, the original assumption (that incapacity could be attributed to God) is false. Hence, God must be absolutely powerful.
This is an indirect proof (burhān bi-l-khalf) showing the necessity of divine power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عنه لأنه لو اتصف تعالى بالعجز لا يخلو إما أن يكون قديماً أو حادثاً، فإن كان قديما لزم أن لا يعدم لاستحالة عدم القديم. فلا يوصف المولى تبارك وتعالى بالقدرة لاستحالة جمع الضدين. وإذا لم يتصف بالقدرة فينتفي العالم، وهذا محال بالمشاهدة والعيان وإن كان حادثا فإن اتصف به مع القدرة لزم الجمع بين الضدين وإن اتصف به مع عدمها لزم حدوثها لأن كل من يطرأ عليه العدم حادث ، ويلزم من حدوث الصفة حدوث الموصوف فينتفي العالم ونفى العالم محال بالمشاهدة.
From him: because if He, exalted, were described with incapacity, it must be either eternal or temporal. If it were eternal, it would necessitate that it does not cease, because it is impossible for the eternal to cease. Thus the Lord, blessed and exalted, would not be described with power, because it is impossible to combine two opposites. And if He were not described with power, then the world would be negated, and this is impossible by observation and sight. And if it were temporal, then if He were described with it along with power, combining the two opposites would be necessary; and if He were described with it without power, then its temporal origination would be necessary, because everything that incurs non-existence is temporal, and from the temporal origination of the attribute follows the temporal origination of the subject, so the world would be negated, and negating the world is impossible by observation.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a proof by dilemma against the possibility of divine incapability. The author begins by assuming, for the sake of argument, that God could be described with incapacity (العجز). He then splits the case: either this incapacity is eternal (قديم) or originated (حادث). If it is eternal, then it cannot cease to exist (because the eternal cannot be annihilated). Therefore, God would forever be incapable, and thus would not be described with power (القدرة). But if He lacks power, the world could not exist (since it requires a powerful agent), yet the world does exist – this is observable. Therefore, the eternal hypothesis leads to a contradiction. If the incapacity is temporal, then either it coexists with power or it exists without power. If it coexists with power, then two opposites (incapacity and power) would be combined in one subject, which is impossible. If it occurs without power, then its occurrence implies that it came into being after not existing, so it is temporal. This temporality of the attribute entails the temporality of the subject (God), because the attribute cannot be temporal if the subject is eternal. But if God is temporal, then He cannot be the creator of the world (since the creator must be pre-existent), so again the world would be negated – which is false. Thus all branches lead to a contradiction, proving that incapacity cannot be attributed to God.

Key Terms
العجز
القدرة
القديم
الحادث
الجمع بين الضدين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's inability to do things like injustice is a form of incapacity (ʿajz), so the proof would be false.
Correction: The proof targets literal incapacity (lack of power), not voluntary refrain. The attribute of power (qudra) includes the ability to act or refrain; incapacity is its absence. The proof shows that absolute incapacity cannot exist in God, but voluntary refraining does not contradict power.

Checkpoint
What was the first logical possibility considered for incapacity if it were attributed to God?
A) It is eternal (قديم)
B) It is originated (حادث)
C) It is both
D) It is neither

Key Takeaway
Incapacity cannot be attributed to God because any possible scenario—eternal or temporal—leads to an absurdity, thereby establishing the necessity of divine power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were even slightly powerless, how would that affect your confidence in His promise to forgive your deepest sins? Think of one sin you hide—does the certainty of God's absolute power change how you approach Him about it? What will you do differently today?
?
The proof shows that every scenario of divine incapacity is absurd. Yet in your daily life, do you sometimes act as if God is unable to handle your problems? Identify one situation where you have doubted God's power, and commit to reciting this logical proof to yourself whenever that doubt creeps in.
?
This lesson uses the principle that opposites cannot combine. In your own life, are there contradictory beliefs or behaviors you hold that betray a lack of trust in God's power? For instance, claiming God is all-powerful but worrying as if He is not. What is one concrete step you can take today to align your actions with the reality that God is absolutely capable?

The Impossibility of Creating with Rational Dislike
Sanusi establishes the impossibility of creation with rational dislike and defines rational dislike.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible for God to create something while having rational dislike for it, and what rational dislike means.

Core Idea
Rational dislike is simply the absence of will; it cannot be attributed to God.

Sanusi begins by addressing a potential question: can God create something He dislikes? He answers with an emphatic 'impossible' and explains that the kind of dislike in question is rational dislike, which is nothing other than the absence of will. Since God's will is necessary and comprehensive, rational dislike cannot apply to Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the key term 'rational dislike' and how the author defines it.
2
Identify the core claim
The passage states that creating with dislike is impossible. Look for the reason given: dislike is contrary to will. Understand why this makes it impossible for God.
3
Define rational dislike
The passage defines rational dislike as the absence of will. Reflect on why this cannot apply to God, given that His will is necessary.
Contrast this with legal dislike, which is covered in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إيجاد شيء من العالم مع كراهته لوجوده فهو محال ، لأن الكراهة ضد الإرادة والإرادة واجبة الله تعالى والكراهة مستحيلة على الله والمراد بها هي الكراهة العقلية التي هي عدم الإرادة. وأما الكراهة الشرعية التي هي إحدى الأحكام الخمسة فلا تنافي الإيجاد وهي - أي المكروهات على هذا المعنى - واقعة بإرادة الله تعالى لأنه لو لم يردها لما وقعت ، إذ لا يكون في ملكه تعالى ما لا يريد ولم يأمر بها إذ لا ملازمة بين الأمر والإرادة عند أهل السنة، بل بينهما عموم وخصوص من وجه فيتواردان في إيمان أبي بكر مثلاً فإنه مأمور به ومراد الله ، وتنفرد الإرادة في كفر أبي جهل وغيره من الكفار وينفرد الأمر في الإيمان منهم، فهم مأمورون بالإيمان ولم يُرِدِ الله تعالى وقوعه منهم لعلمه بعدم إرادتهم لذلك.
As for bringing about something from the world while He dislikes its existence, it is impossible, because dislike is contrary to will, and will is necessary for God, and dislike is impossible for God. What is meant by it is rational dislike, which is the absence of will. As for legal dislike, which is one of the five rulings, it does not conflict with bringing into existence. And they – i.e., the disliked things in this sense – occur by God's will, because if He did not will them they would not occur, since nothing occurs in His dominion that He does not will. And He did not command them, because there is no necessary connection between command and will according to the Ahl al-Sunna. Rather, there is a general-and-specific relationship between them in one respect. They coincide in the faith of Abu Bakr, for example, for it is commanded and willed by God. Will alone applies to the disbelief of Abu Jahl and other disbelievers, and command alone applies to their faith. Thus they are commanded to believe, but God did not will its occurrence from them, because He knew that they do not will it.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection: if God dislikes something (كراهة), how can He create it? The author distinguishes two meanings of 'dislike'. Rational dislike (الكراهة العقلية) is simply the absence of will – this is impossible for God because He must will everything that exists. Legal dislike (الكراهة الشرعية), on the other hand, is a legal judgment that something is prohibited or disliked in religion; this does not imply that God does not will its existence. For example, disbelief is legally disliked, but God wills its existence from certain individuals. The author then explains the relationship between God's command (الأمر) and His will (الإرادة) according to Ahl al-Sunna: they are not necessarily tied. God may command something without willing it (e.g., faith from Abu Jahl), and He may will something without commanding it (e.g., disbelief). They coincide in some cases (e.g., faith of Abu Bakr). This preserves God's justice and wisdom while affirming His universal will.

Key Terms
الكراهة العقلية
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that God can dislike something in the same way humans do, leading to the idea that He might create something He dislikes.
Correction: Rational dislike is not a sentiment but the absence of will. Since God's will is necessary and active in all creation, rational dislike cannot be attributed to Him.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'rational dislike' mean?
A) It is the same as legal dislike
B) It is the absence of will
C) It is a feeling of hatred
D) It is a legal ruling

Key Takeaway
God cannot have rational dislike because it is the absence of will, and His will is necessary and all-encompassing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's will covering all things, including your own sins and failures, do you secretly assume that God dislikes them in a way that conflicts with His willing them? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what would it mean for you to truly believe that God's will is one and without any deficiency?
?
You often feel that God must be 'displeased' with certain events in your life. But if rational dislike is simply absence of will, and God's will is never absent, then what is the correct way to understand God's relation to hardship or suffering? Are you willing to accept that God's will fully encompasses even what you find painful, without any 'dislike' on His part?
?
This lesson defines rational dislike as the absence of will. If you have ever doubted God's goodness because of evil in the world, did you implicitly assume that God 'dislikes' evil in a rational sense? How might redefining dislike as mere absence of will reshape your trust in God's complete sovereignty and wisdom? What concrete step can you take today to stop projecting human hesitation onto God?

Legal Dislike and the Relationship Between Command and Will
Sanusi explains legal dislike and the nuanced relationship between command and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how legal dislike (one of the five rulings) is compatible with God's will, and how command and will are related according to Ahl al-Sunna.

Core Idea
God may command what He does not will, and will what He does not command.

After clarifying rational dislike, Sanusi turns to legal dislike, which is a category of divine law. He explains that legal dislike does not prevent God from willing the existence of something. This leads to a deeper point: command and will are not necessarily linked. The Ahl al-Sunna maintain a nuanced relationship between them, which is illustrated with examples of the believers and disbelievers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on legal dislike
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'وأما الكراهة الشرعية' to the end. Focus on how legal dislike is defined and why it does not conflict with God's will.
2
Understand the compatibility
The author states that legal dislike does not conflict with bringing something into existence. Note that legal manhurs (disliked things) occur by God's will. Ask yourself why this must be the case.
Contrast with rational dislike which is impossible.
3
Grasp the command-will relationship
The author explains that there is no necessary connection between command and will. They have a general-and-specific relationship. Identify the three cases mentioned: both coinciding, will alone, command alone.
This is a key theological point of Ahl al-Sunna.
4
Reflect on the examples
The passage uses Abu Bakr and Abu Jahl as examples. Understand how each case illustrates the relationship. Consider why God commands disbelief from disbelievers without willing it.

Impossibility of Forgetfulness and Heedlessness in God
Sanusi defines and rejects two types of unawareness in God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why heedlessness (الدَّهول) and inattention (الغفلة) are impossible for Allah, and how these terms are distinguished from related concepts like forgetfulness (السهو) and forgetting (النسيان).

Core Idea
Divine knowledge leaves no room for even a moment of unawareness.

After establishing that ignorance is impossible for God, the author now addresses two specific forms of lacking knowledge: heedlessness and inattention. These may seem subtle, but they have precise definitions in Islamic theology. This lesson unpacks these terms and shows why they cannot be attributed to the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall meaning. Notice the two main terms being defined: الذهول and الغفلة.
The footnote contains important additional distinctions.
2
Identify the key definitions
Find how the passage defines heedlessness (الذهول) and inattention (الغفلة). Write down their definitions in your own words.
Heedlessness: disappearance of something after knowledge. Inattention: absolute lack of knowledge.
3
Understand the fine distinctions in the footnote
The footnote further distinguishes between these terms and relates them to forgetfulness (السهو) and forgetting (النسيان). Focus on the role of perception (المدركة) and memory (الحافظة).
Ghafla: item leaves perception but stays in memory. Dhuhl: leaves perception entirely.
4
Grasp the argument for impossibility
The passage concludes by stating that both are incompatible with will because they are incompatible with knowledge. How does this follow? Connect it to the earlier lesson on knowledge and will.
Will depends on knowledge, so anything negating knowledge also negates will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيل على الله تعالى الذهول وهو غيبوبة الشيء بعد العلم به، ويستحيل على الله تعالى الغفلة حالة إيجاده ، والغفلة هي عدم العلم بالشيء مطلقا، أي إنه يستحيل على الله أن يوجد شيئا من العالم مع الغفلة أو الذهول ). (1) (۱) وقيل الذهول أعم من الغفلة لأن الغفلة زوال الشيء من المدركة مع بقائه في الحافظة ، والذهول زواله من المدركة مطلقاً، وعلى هذا فالسهو مرادف للغفلة ، كما يؤخذ من القاموس حيث قال غفل عنه تركه وسها عنه أهـ وأما النسيان فهو أخص من الذهول لأنه زوال الشيء من الحافظة والمدركة معاً. ووجه منافاة كل من الذهول والغفلة للإرادة أنهما منافيان للعلم، وكل ما كان منافياً للعلم كان منافياً للإرادة، فهما منافيان للإرادة بواسطة منافاتهما للعلم.(ج)
And it is impossible for God, exalted, to have heedlessness (الذهول), which is the disappearance of something after knowledge of it, and it is impossible for God, exalted, to have inattention (الغفلة) at the moment of creating. And inattention is the absolute lack of knowledge of something. That is, it is impossible for God to bring into existence anything from the world while in a state of inattention or heedlessness. (1) And it is said that heedlessness is more general than inattention, because inattention is the removal of something from the perception while it remains in the memory, while heedlessness is its removal from perception absolutely. Based on this, forgetfulness (السهو) is synonymous with inattention, as taken from al-Qamus: 'He was inattentive to it: he left it and forgot it.' As for forgetting (النسيان), it is more specific than heedlessness, because it is the removal of something from both memory and perception together. The reason for the incompatibility of both heedlessness and inattention with will is that they are incompatible with knowledge, and everything that is incompatible with knowledge is incompatible with will. So both are incompatible with will by virtue of their incompatibility with knowledge.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage continues the series of things impossible for God. It defines two related terms: الذهول (heedlessness/forgetfulness) and الغفلة (inattention). Both involve a lack of knowledge, which would be impossible for God who is all-knowing. The footnote (likely from the editor) further distinguishes them: ghafla is when something leaves the conscious perception but remains in memory; dhuhl is when it leaves perception entirely. Forgetting (السهو) is synonymous with ghafla, while نسيان (forgetting) is more specific – it is when something leaves both memory and perception. The key point: both are impossible for God because they negate knowledge, and since will depends on knowledge, they would also negate will. God's creating cannot involve any lack of awareness.

Key Terms
الذهول
الغفلة
السهو
النسيان
المدركة
الحافظة
العلم
الإرادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that God can 'forget' sins or overlook them, as a metaphorical expression.
Correction: Forgetfulness implies ignorance and lack of knowledge, which is impossible for God. Even metaphorical forgetfulness is not allowed because it attributes deficiency to Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between heedlessness (الذهول) and inattention (الغفلة)?
A) Heedlessness is more general than inattention
B) They are synonyms
C) Inattention is more general than heedlessness
D) There is no relation

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of heedlessness and inattention in God is rooted in His perfect knowledge, which is essential for His will and action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you sin secretly, do you act as if God is momentarily unaware of that sin? How does this lesson confront that assumption? What must you change in your heart today?

Impossibility of Ignorance on God
Sanusi defines ignorance, its two types, and states that it is impossible for God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of ignorance, its two types (compound and simple), and why it is impossible for Allah.

Core Idea
Ignorance is the absence of knowledge; both types are impossible for Allah.

Sanusi now turns to a key attribute of Allah: knowledge. To understand that Allah knows all, we must first understand what ignorance is and why it cannot apply to Him. This passage defines ignorance precisely, breaks it into two categories, and then extends the impossibility to all states that resemble ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own definitions come through.
Notice that Sanusi defines ignorance before describing its types.
2
Identify the two types of ignorance
Look for 'الجهل المركب' and 'الجهل البسيط'. Understand how they differ. Compound ignorance involves being unaware of one's ignorance, while simple ignorance is knowing that you don't know.
3
Reflect on impossibility for Allah
The passage states both types are impossible for Allah. Consider why: if Allah is all-knowing, He cannot have any lack of knowledge, nor can He be mistaken about His own knowledge.
4
Scan the related impossible states
The passage lists doubt, conjecture, illusion, absent-mindedness, inattention, forgetfulness, and limitations on knowledge. These all imply ignorance and are therefore impossible for Allah.
These are additional clarifications, not a separate proof.
5
Understand the two notes
The notes explain that 'the known' includes all three categories (necessary, impossible, possible), and that the opposition between knowledge and compound vs. simple ignorance differs in type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيل الجهل عليه تعالى : وهو ضد العلم والجهل ،قسمان مركّب وبسيط : فحقيقة المركب تصور الشيء على خلاف ما هو به ، وهو أن يجهل الشيء ويجهل جهله به ، فهو مركب من جهلين. والبسيط أن يجهل الشيء ويعلم أنه جاهل به وحقيقته عدم العلم بالشيء فيما شأنه أن يعلم. هذان القسمان مستحيلان على الله تعالى. ويستحيل عليه أيضا في معنى الجهل ، كالشك والظن والوهم والذهول والغفلة والنسيان وكون علمه ضرورياً أو نظرياً أو بديهياً أو معرفة ، لأن المعرفة ما تقدمها جهل على قول بعض العلماء، أو كونه يقينياً لأن اليقين ما ثبت بالدليل ، أو كونه نظرياً أو كسبياً. ملاحظة : المعلوم يشمل الواجب والمستحيل والجائز. ملاحظة : التقابل بين العلم والجهل المركب تقابل بين الضدين، وبين العلم والجهل البسيط من باب عدم العلم والملكة. وحيث ثبت العلم انتفت حقيقة الجهل والملكة فالعلم وجودي.
And ignorance (al-jahl) is impossible for Him, exalted is He. It is the opposite of knowledge. Ignorance is of two types: compound (murakkab) and simple (basit). The reality of the compound is conceiving of something other than what it is, and it is that one is ignorant of the thing and ignorant of his ignorance of it, so it is composed of two ignorances. The simple is that one is ignorant of the thing and knows that he is ignorant of it, and its reality is the absence of knowledge of something that is capable of being known. These two types are impossible for Allah Almighty. Also impossible for Him are things that are in the meaning of ignorance, such as doubt (shakk), conjecture (zann), illusion (wahm), absent-mindedness (dhuhul), inattention (ghafla), forgetfulness (nasyan), and that His knowledge be necessary (daruri) or theoretical (nazari) or intuitive (badihi) or acquired (ma'rifa), because 'ma'rifa' is preceded by ignorance according to some scholars, or that it be certain (yaqini) because certainty is established by proof, or that it be theoretical or acquisitive. Note: The known (al-ma'lum) includes the necessary, the impossible, and the possible. Note: The opposition between knowledge and compound ignorance is opposition of contraries, and between knowledge and simple ignorance is of the type of absence (of knowledge) and capacity (malaka). Where knowledge is established, the reality of ignorance and capacity are negated, so knowledge is existent.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage defines ignorance (jahl) and its impossibility for God. Ignorance is the opposite of knowledge. It is divided into two types: compound ignorance and simple ignorance. Compound ignorance is when a person has a false conception of something and is unaware of his own ignorance; it consists of two ignorances. Simple ignorance is when a person is ignorant of something but knows he is ignorant; it is simply the absence of knowledge of something that could be known. Both are impossible for Allah. Furthermore, any state similar to ignorance is also impossible, such as doubt, conjecture, illusion, absent-mindedness, inattention, and forgetfulness. Also impossible is that God's knowledge be necessary (acquired without effort), theoretical, intuitive, or acquired, because these involve limitations. The two notes clarify that the known encompasses all three categories (necessary, impossible, possible), and that the opposition between knowledge and the two types of ignorance differs: with compound ignorance it is a true opposition of contraries, while with simple ignorance it is the opposition of absence and capacity. Since knowledge is an attribute of God, it is an existent attribute (wujudi).

Key Terms
الجهل
الجهل المركب
الجهل البسيط
الشك
الظن
الوهم
الغفلة
النسيان
المعرفة
اليقين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think ignorance is simply lack of knowledge and that it is all the same.
Correction: The passage shows ignorance has two types (compound and simple) and that many states (doubt, forgetfulness, etc.) are also in the meaning of ignorance.

Checkpoint
What is the reality of simple ignorance (al-jahl al-basit)?
A) Conceiving something contrary to what it is
B) Lack of knowledge while knowing one is ignorant
C) Combination of two ignorances
D) Doubt about a matter

Key Takeaway
Ignorance in any form, compound or simple, is impossible for Allah because He is all-knowing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have moments of doubt about Allah’s promises or wisdom. How does knowing that doubt is a form of ignorance, and that Allah is free from all ignorance, challenge you to trust His knowledge completely? What specific area of your life do you need to surrender to His perfect knowledge today?
?
When you forget your own faults or the lessons you've learned, you experience simple ignorance. Allah never forgets. How does this truth comfort you regarding your own shortcomings? In what way will you respond to His perfect awareness of your state right now?
?
The passage says compound ignorance means believing something false while being unaware of your ignorance. Have you ever been certain about something regarding your faith only to later discover you were mistaken? What does Allah's freedom from even simple ignorance teach you about the need to constantly seek true knowledge? What step will you take this week to replace a comfortable assumption with verified knowledge?

Impossibility of Death and Deafness on God
Sanusi states the impossibility of death and deafness on God and explains their meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why death and deafness are impossible for God, and the two theological positions on the reality of these attributes.

Core Idea
God is eternally living and all-hearing; death and deafness cannot apply to Him.

Having established that life and hearing are necessary attributes of God, Sanusi now shows that their opposites—death and deafness—are absolutely impossible. He also clarifies what these negations entail and introduces a key theological debate on the nature of death and deafness as either existent accidents or mere absences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Notice that Sanusi first addresses death, then deafness. Pay attention to the editorial footnotes that contrast the Sunni and Mu'tazilite views.
The passage for deafness is incomplete; it cuts off mid-sentence.
2
Identify the two impossibilities and their definitions
Find where Sanusi states that death and deafness are impossible. Note how he defines each as the opposite of a divine attribute (life and hearing) and expands on what is included in that impossibility.
3
Compare the two theological schools
The editor's notes explain the difference between the Sunni view (death/deafness are existent accidents) and the Mu'tazilite view (they are mere absences). Reflect on how this affects the understanding of God's perfection.
This comparison highlights why the Sunni position maintains that God is free from all imperfections, including even the absence of perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيل عليه تعالى الموت : وهو ضد الحياة ) ، وكذلك يستحيل عليه ما في معنى الموت، مثل أن تكون حياته تعالى بروح أو نفس أو بأكل أو شرب أو نوم أو سنة أو غير ذلك من أوصاف الحوادث. ملاحظة : هناك مذهبان في حقيقة الموت الأول يقول الموت عرض يعقب الحياة فالموت على هذا المذهب وجودي ، والثاني يقول الموت هو عدم الحياة فيما شأنه الحياة، والموت على هذا المذهب عدمي. (1) الموت هو أمر وجودي عند أهل السنة، وأما عند المعتزلة فهو عدم الحياة عما شأنه أن يكون حياً، والتقابل بينه وبين الحياة من تقابل الضدين على الأول ، ومن تقابل العدم والملكة على الثاني. (ج). ويستحيل عليه تعالى الصمم : ضد السمع، وحقيقة الصمم عدم السمع بالكلية بوجود آفة تمنع من ذاك () ، وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى ما في معنى الصمم ككونه يسمع من [؟] (2) هو أمر وجودي يضاد السمع عند أهل السنة، وأما عند المعتزلة فهو عدم السمع عما من شأنه أن يكون سميعا . (ج)
And death (al-mawt) is impossible for Him, exalted is He, which is the opposite of life. Likewise impossible for Him are what are in the meaning of death, such as that His life, exalted is He, be by a spirit (ruh) or soul (nafs) or by eating or drinking or sleep or slumber or other attributes of temporal beings. Note: There are two schools regarding the reality of death: the first says death is an accident that follows life, so death according to this school is existent; the second says death is the absence of life in that which is capable of life, and death according to this school is non-existent. (1) Death is an existent thing according to the Ahl al-Sunnah, while according to the Mu'tazila it is the absence of life from what is capable of being alive, and the opposition between it and life is opposition of contraries on the first view, and of absence and capacity on the second. (ed.). And deafness (al-samam) is impossible for Him, exalted is He, which is the opposite of hearing. The reality of deafness is total lack of hearing due to the existence of an affliction that prevents it. Likewise impossible for Him are what are in the meaning of deafness, such as that He hears from [؟] (2) It is an existent thing that opposes hearing according to the Ahl al-Sunnah, while according to the Mu'tazila it is the absence of hearing from what is capable of being hearer. (ed.)
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage addresses the impossibility of death and deafness for God. Death is defined as the opposite of life. Since God is eternally living, death is impossible. Also impossible are any attributes that imply death, such as having a spirit, soul, needing food or drink, sleep, slumber, etc. A note explains two theological positions on the reality of death: the first holds that death is an existent accident that follows life (the Sunni view), the second holds that death is the mere absence of life (the Mu'tazilite view). The editor's footnote adds that death is existent for Ahl al-Sunnah and nonexistent for Mu'tazila, with corresponding relationships of opposition. The second impossibility is deafness, which is the opposite of hearing. Deafness is total lack of hearing due to an affliction. Also impossible are any attributes similar to deafness, such as hearing from a particular direction—though the text is incomplete. The editor's footnote again contrasts the Sunni view (deafness as an existent entity) with the Mu'tazilite view (absence of hearing). The passage establishes that God is free from all imperfections and temporal attributes.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Death is simply non-existence and therefore not a real attribute that can be negated from God.
Correction: According to Ahl al-Sunnah, death is an existent accident, so negating it from God is a real negation of a possible imperfection, not a mere tautology.

Checkpoint

A) Death is the absence of life
B) Death is an existent accident following life
C) Death is a state of unconsciousness
D) Death is the separation of soul and body

Key Takeaway
Death and deafness are impossible for God because they contradict His perfect life and hearing; moreover, they are understood as positive attributes of imperfection in Sunni theology.

Why Death is Impossible for Allah
Declares death impossible for Allah and explains its meaning and related opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why death is impossible for Allah and how two different definitions of death both lead to the same conclusion of impossibility.

Core Idea
Death is impossible for Allah because it would reduce His life to contingent attributes.

This lesson focuses on the attribute of life and its perfection. The author declares that death, the opposite of life, is impossible for Allah, and extends this to any qualifications that would make divine life resemble created life. A note on two theological schools about the nature of death further clarifies that regardless of how death is defined, it cannot apply to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's statements.
Notice the straightforward declaration at the beginning.
2
Identify the main ruling and its extension
The passage first states death is impossible, then extends to anything in the meaning of death such as life by spirit, soul, eating, etc. Understand that these are contingent descriptions that cannot apply to Allah.
3
Understand the two schools on the reality of death
The passage notes two schools: one says death is an existential accident following life, the other says it is the absence of life. Reflect on how both schools agree death is impossible for Allah.
This shows the thoroughness of the argument.
4
Reflect on the significance for divine transcendence
Consider why this ruling is foundational: Allah's life is perfect and independent, not subject to any imperfection or dependence on created means.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيل عليه تعالى الموت: وهو ضد الحياة، وكذلك يستحيل عليه ما في معنى الموت، مثل أن تكون حياته تعالى بروح أو نفس أو بأكل أو شرب أو نوم أو سنة أو غير ذلك من أوصاف الحوادث. ملاحظة: هناك مذهبان في حقيقة الموت الأول يقول الموت عرض يعقب الحياة فالموت على هذا المذهب وجودي، والثاني يقول الموت هو عدم الحياة فيما شأنه الحياة، والموت على هذا المذهب عدمي.
And death is impossible for Him, exalted: and it is the opposite of life. Likewise impossible for Him is what is in the meaning of death, such that His life, exalted, be by a spirit or soul or by eating or drinking or sleep or slumber or other than that of the descriptions of contingents. Note: There are two schools concerning the reality of death: the first says death is an accident that follows life, so death according to this school is existential; the second says death is the absence of life in that which should be living, and death according to this school is non-existential.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal statement on the impossibility of death (الموت) for Allah. It begins by directly ruling death impossible, defining it as the opposite (ضد) of life. It then extends that impossibility to anything that implies death or resembles it, such as Allah's life being dependent on a spirit (روح), soul (نفس), eating, drinking, sleep, slumber (سنة), or any other characteristics of contingent beings (أوصاف الحوادث). Such restrictions would reduce divine life to the level of created life, which is inadmissible. The passage then adds a note on two scholarly schools regarding the reality of death itself: the first holds that death is an accident (عرض) that follows life, thus it is an existent (وجودي) attribute; the second holds that death is the mere absence of life in a subject that would normally be alive, thus it is non-existent (عدمي). Regardless of which school is accepted, death is impossible for Allah because He is necessarily living.

Misconception: Some might think death is merely the absence of life, so it might be possible for Allah since it is not an existing thing.
Correction: Even if death is defined as absence of life, it is still impossible for Allah because He is necessarily living without any interruption or imperfection.

Key Takeaway
Allah's life is perfect and independent of any contingent characteristics, making death impossible.

?

Impossibility of Deafness, Blindness, and Muteness in Allah
Lists three sensory-verbal impossibilities and concludes with a general rule.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why deafness, blindness, and muteness are impossible for Allah, and how these impossibilities extend to any limited or physical modalities of hearing, sight, and speech.

Core Idea
Allah's attributes are perfect and unlimited; any deficiency or physical mode is impossible.

This passage from Sanusi's creed outlines three sensory-verbal impossibilities for Allah: deafness, blindness, and muteness. Each is defined as the complete absence of a perfection due to a defect, which is impossible for the Perfect Being. The author then extends the impossibility to any limited or physical form of these attributes, affirming that Allah's hearing, sight, and speech encompass all existents without restriction. Finally, a general principle is stated: the opposites of all essential attributes are impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the full Arabic once to get a sense of the argument.
2
Identify the three impossibilities
Notice the three items: deafness, blindness, muteness. Each is defined and then extended.
The pattern is similar for each.
3
Understand the extensions
For each, the author negates not only the direct opposite but also any limited mode (e.g., hearing with ears).
4
Grasp the general rule
The last sentence states that opposites of essential attributes are clearly impossible.
This connects to the previous lesson on opposites of meanings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويستحيل عليه تعالى الصمم: ضد السمع، وحقيقة الصمم عدم السمع بالكلية بوجود آفة تمنع من ذلك، وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى ما في معنى الصمم ككونه يسمع الأصوات دون الذوات وكون سمعه بآذان أو صماخ أو غير ذلك، بل سمعه تعالى متعلق بكل موجود. ويستحيل عليه تعالى العمى ضد البصر وحقيقة العمى عدم البصر بالكلية بوجود آفة تمنع من ذلك. وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى ما في معناه من المقلة والأجفان والحدقة وغير ذلك. ويستحيل عليه تعالى البكم: وهو ضد الكلام وحقيقة البكم عدم الكلام بالكلية بوجود آفة تمنعه من ذلك، وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى ما في معناه بأن يكون كلامه بحروف وأصوات وغير ذلك من الصفات الحادثة. وأما استحالة أضداد ومنافيات الصفات المعنوية فهي واضحة ويزداد جلاؤها ظهورا بعد العلم باستحالة أضداد المعاني كما سبق.
And deafness is impossible for Him, exalted: it is the opposite of hearing, and the reality of deafness is the complete absence of hearing due to the existence of a defect that prevents that. Likewise impossible for Him, exalted, is what is in the meaning of deafness, such as that He hears sounds without substances, and that His hearing be by ears or ear canals or other than that; rather His hearing, exalted, is related to every existent. And blindness is impossible for Him, exalted: it is the opposite of sight, and the reality of blindness is the complete absence of sight due to the existence of a defect that prevents that. Likewise impossible for Him, exalted, is what is in its meaning, such as eyeball, eyelids, pupil, and other than that. And muteness is impossible for Him, exalted: it is the opposite of speech, and the reality of muteness is the complete absence of speech due to the existence of a defect that prevents that. Likewise impossible for Him, exalted, is what is in its meaning, such that His speech be by letters and sounds and other than that of contingent attributes. As for the impossibility of the opposites and contradictions of the essential attributes, it is clear, and its clarity increases after knowing the impossibility of the opposites of the meanings as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive statement of three sensory and verbal impossibilities for Allah: deafness (الصمم), blindness (العمى), and muteness (البكم), followed by a general rule about the impossibility of opposites of essential attributes. Each is handled similarly: first the term is defined as the opposite of the divine attribute (hearing, sight, speech), then its reality is given as the complete absence of that faculty due to a defect (آفة), thus it is a deficiency necessarily impossible for Allah. The passage then extends the impossibility to anything that implies such limitations: for hearing, that Allah would hear sounds but not substances, or hear with ears or ear canals; rather His hearing encompasses all existents (متعلق بكل موجود). For sight, that He would have eyeballs, eyelids, pupils, etc. For speech, that His speech be composed of letters and sounds—these are all contingent traits. Finally, a concluding remark: the impossibility of opposites and contradictions (أضداد ومنافيات) of the essential attributes (الصفات المعنوية) is obvious, and becomes even clearer after understanding the previous discussion about the impossibility of opposites of the meanings (أضداد المعاني) that was covered earlier. This serves as a summary and reinforcement of the broader principle.

Correction: The passage also negates any limited or physical modes of perception and speech, such as hearing with ears or seeing with eyes.

Key Takeaway

The Universal Scope of Divine Hearing and Sight
Sanusi states the universality of divine hearing and sight, gives a rational proof, and addresses disagreements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for why God's hearing and sight encompass all existents, and how this differs from created senses.

Core Idea
God's hearing and sight are unlimited because existence itself is their ground.

In this passage, Imam Sanusi addresses a fundamental attribute of God: His hearing and sight. While we affirm these attributes from revelation, reason shows they must be universal—otherwise they would be contingent and limited like our senses. This lesson unpacks Sanusi's rational argument and clarifies common misconceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Let Sanusi's own words establish the framework before you analyze the argument.
Notice the contrast between divine and created senses.
2
Understand the difference from human senses
Observe how Sanusi distinguishes divine hearing/sight from human hearing (limited to sounds) and sight (limited to bodies, colors, spaces). This sets up the need for a proof of universality.
Human senses are restricted; why must God's be unrestricted?
3
Follow the rational proof
Trace the proof: existence grounds divine perception. If it were limited to some existents, a specifier would be needed, implying contingency—impossible for God. Therefore, it must encompass all existents.
This proof relies on the concept of 'specifier' (mukhaṣṣiṣ) familiar in kalām.
4
Reflect on the conclusion and cautions
Note that the attributes themselves are from revelation, but their universal scope is from reason. Then consider the warnings: not to limit 'audible' and 'visible' to sounds and colors, and to ponder before objecting.
Sanusi's response to opponents shows the subtlety of this issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام السنوسي في شرح صغرى الصغرى: وقولنا في السمع والبصر المتعلقان بجميع الموجودات، أي ينكشف لسمعه تعالى وبصره جميع الموجودات قديمة كانت أو حادثة، وليس كسمع المخلوق الذي يختص عادة تعلقه بالأصوات ولا كبصر المخلوق الذي إنما يتعلق عادة بالأجسام والألوان والأكوان، وبرهان عموم التعلق لسمعه تعالى أن مصحح تعلقهما إنما هو الوجود، فلو تعلقا ببعض الموجودات دون بعض لافتقرا إلى مخصص، فيكونان حادثين، وقيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى مستحيل، والحاصل أن ثبوت هاتين الصفتين أخذ من الشرع وتعلقهما بجميع الموجودات أخذ من الدليل العقلي. اهـ، يقصد الإمام السنوسي بالدليل العقلي أن هذه المسألة تحتاج إلى مزيد نظر وبسط، ولا يكفي فيها الاعتماد على ما يبدو لأول النظر سواء في العقليات أو النقليات وخالفه بعض العلماء في كون هاتين الصفتين تتعلقان بالموجودات، وقال بل تتعلقان بالمسموعات والمبصرات، ورد عليه السنوسي رداً مطولاً، وبينهما مراسلات معروفة، وهي مسألة دقيقة. وعلى كل حال فلا يجوز التوهم بأن المقصود بالمسموعات مجرد الأصوات، لأن المعتمد عند أكثر أهل السنة سماع كلام الله النفسي وأن الله تعالى يسمع كلامه النفسي، وهو ليس بصوت ولا بحرف، وكذلك لا يقال بأن المبصرات هي الألوان فقط لأن الله تعالى يرى نفسه وهو تعالى ليس بلون ولا له لون. فعلى العاقل أن يستحضر هذه اللمعات فإنها تقرب كلام الإمام السنوسي إلى عقله ولا يبادر بالمخالفة، قبل الفهم. والمسألة من دقيق الكلام، ولنا فيها تحقيق لا يتسع هذا المختصر لإثباته. فتأمل. (س).
Imam Sanusi said in his commentary on 'al-Sughra al-Sughra': And our statement concerning hearing and sight: both are related to all existents, meaning that all existents, whether eternal or contingent, are disclosed to His hearing and sight. And it is not like the hearing of a creature which is usually limited in its relation to sounds, nor like the sight of a creature which is usually related only to bodies, colors, and spaces. The proof for the universality of their relation to His hearing is that the ground for their relation is only existence. So if they related to some existents without others, they would need a specific, and thus they would be contingent, and the subsistence of contingents in His essence is impossible. The conclusion is that the affirmation of these two attributes is taken from the Law, and their relation to all existents is taken from rational proof. End of quote. He means by rational proof that this matter requires further consideration and expansion, and it is not sufficient to rely on what first appears, whether in rational or transmitted matters. Some scholars disagreed with him concerning these two attributes being related to existents, saying rather they are related to audible and visible things. Sanusi responded at length, and there are well-known correspondences between them, and it is a subtle matter. In any case, it is not permissible to suppose that what is meant by 'audibles' is mere sounds, because the relied-upon view among most Ahl al-Sunna is that God's speech is heard as an eternal attribute (kalam nafsi), and Allah hears His own eternal speech, which is not a sound nor a letter. Likewise, one should not say that 'visibles' are only colors, because Allah sees Himself, and He is not a color nor does He have a color. So the intelligent person should keep these points in mind, for they bring Sanusi's words closer to his understanding, and he should not hasten to object before understanding. And the matter is from the subtlety of kalam, and we have a verification that this brief work cannot contain. So reflect. (S)
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary by the author of Tahdhib (likely Said Foudah) quoting and explaining Imam Sanusi's position on the scope of divine hearing (السمع) and sight (البصر). It begins by quoting Sanusi from his commentary on 'صغرى الصغرى', where he states that these two attributes relate to all existents ('متعلقان بجميع الموجودات'). This means that all things, whether eternal (قديمة) like God's attributes or contingent (حادثة) like creation, are disclosed to God's hearing and sight. This differs from created hearing and sight, which are typically limited to sounds or physical objects like bodies, colors, and spaces. The proof for universality is that the ground for relation is sheer existence: if they related only to some existents, they would require a specific (مخصص) to choose which ones, making them contingent (حادثين), and contingency in God is impossible. So the affirmation of the attributes themselves is from revelation (الشرع), but their universal scope is derived from rational proof (الدليل العقلي). The commentator then clarifies that Sanusi's 'rational proof' means careful reasoning, not relying on first impressions in either rational or transmitted matters. He notes that some scholars disagreed with Sanusi, arguing that these attributes relate only to audibles (مسموعات) and visibles (مبصرات). Sanusi refuted them at length, and there is a known correspondence. The commentator then warns against misunderstanding: 'audibles' does not mean mere sounds, because Ahl al-Sunna hold that God's eternal speech (كلام نفسي) is heard, and it is not sound or letter. 'Visibles' does not mean only colors, because God sees Himself and He is not colored. The passage concludes with advice to the intelligent reader to ponder these subtleties before objecting.

اللمعات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God's hearing and sight are limited like human senses, only perceiving sounds and visible objects.
Correction: The passage shows that God's hearing and sight encompass all existents because existence itself is the ground, and limitation would require a specifier implying contingency.

Checkpoint
What is the rational proof for the universal scope of God's hearing and sight?
A) The consensus of the scholars
B) The fact that existence itself is the ground for relation, otherwise limitation would require a specific and imply contingency
C) The text of the Quran
D) The need for God to know all things

Key Takeaway
God's hearing and sight are universal, proven by reason from the principle that existence itself grounds perception, and are not limited like human senses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever limit God's awareness of your personal struggles or sins, thinking they are too small or hidden for His sight? How does Sanusi's proof that God's sight relates to all existents challenge that assumption?
?
When you make dua, do you unconsciously imagine God's hearing as limited to your spoken words? The lesson says His hearing is not like that—He hears even your unspoken inner state. How does this change the sincerity and urgency of your supplication?
?
Sanusi warns against hastening to object before understanding. In your own study of Islamic creed, have you ever rejected a subtle theological point without truly grasping it? What is one area where you need to slow down and reflect before forming an opinion? Commit to a specific practice of patience in learning.

God's Hearing and Sight Encompass All Existence
Sanusi explains that God's hearing and sight are not limited to sounds/colors but relate to all existents, supported by a rational proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's hearing and sight are not limited to sounds and colors but relate to every existent, and the rational proof for this universality.

Core Idea
God's hearing and sight relate to every existent, not just sounds or colors.

Sanusi begins by clarifying the nature of God's hearing and sight, distinguishing them from human senses. He then presents a rational argument to show why these attributes must relate to all existents, a key point in Islamic theology to avoid anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Read the Arabic passage noting how Sanusi first negates limitations (sounds, ears) before asserting universality.
2
Examine the rational proof
Study the proof: the basis of relation is existence; if limited, a specifier would be needed, implying temporality, impossible for God.
This proof establishes universality from reason.
3
Consider the editorial clarification
Note that the existence of hearing/sight is from revelation, but universality is from reason. Also beware of misunderstandings about 'audibles' and 'visibles'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصوات دون الذوات وكون سمعه بآذان أو صماخ أو غير ذلك، بل سمعه تعالى متعلق بكل موجود (١) ويستحيل عليه تعالى العمى ضد البصر وحقيقة العمى عدم البصر بالكلية بوجود آفة. (٢) [؟] تمنع من ذلك . وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى ما في معناه من المقلة والأجفان والحدقة وغير ذلك. ويستحيل عليه تعالى البكم : وهو ضد الكلام وحقيقة البكم عدم الكلام بالكلية بوجود آفة تمنعه من ذاك (٣) ، وكذا يستحيل عليه تعالى ما في معناه بأن يكون كلامه بحروف وأصوات وغير ذلك من الصفات الحادثة. وأما استحالة أضداد ومنافيات الصفات المعنوية فهي واضحة ويزداد جلاؤها ظهورا بعد العلم باستحالة أضداد المعاني كما سبق. (١) قال الإمام السنوسي في شرح صغرى الصغرى : وقولنا في السمع والبصر المتعلقان بجميع الموجودات ، أي ينكشف لسمعه تعالى وبصره جميع الموجودات قديمة كانت أو حادثة، وليس كسمع المخلوق الذي يختص عادة تعلقه بالأصوات ولا كبصر المخلوق الذي إنما يتعلق عادة بالأجسام والألوان والأكوان، وبرهان عموم التعلق لسمعه تعالى أن مصحح تعلقهما إنما هو الوجود، فلو تعلقا ببعض الموجودات دون بعض لافتقرا إلى مخصص، فيكونان حادثين، وقيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى مستحيل ، والحاصل أن ثبوت هاتين الصفتين أخذ من الشرع وتعلقهما بجميع الموجودات أخذ من الدليل العقلي. اهـ ، يقصد الإمام السنوسي بالدليل العقلي أن هذه المسألة تحتاج إلى مزيد نظر وبسط ، ولا يكفي فيها الاعتماد على ما يبدو لأول النظر سواء في العقليات أو النقليات وخالفه بعض العلماء في كون هاتين الصفتين تتعلقان بالموجودات، وقال بل تتعلقان بالمسموعات والمبصرات، ورد عليه السنوسي رداً مطولاً، وبينهما مراسلات معروفة، وهي مسألة دقيقة. وعلى كل حال فلا يجوز التوهم بأن المقصود بالمسموعات مجرد الأصوات، لأن المعتمد عند أكثر أهل السنة سماع كلام الله النفسي وأن الله تعالى يسمع كلامه النفسي، وهو ليس بصوت ولا بحرف، وكذلك لا يقال بأن المبصرات هي الألوان فقط لأن الله تعالى يرى نفسه وهو تعالى ليس بلون ولا له لون. فعلى العاقل أن يستحضر هذه اللمعات فإنها تقرب كلام الإمام السنوسي إلى عقله ولا يبادر بالمخالفة، قبل الفهم. والمسألة من دقيق الكلام، ولنا فيها تحقيق لا يتسع هذا المختصر لإثباته.فتأمل.(س). (٢) هو أمر وجودي يضاد البصر عند أهل السنة وأما عند المعتزلة فهو عدم البصر عما من شأنه أن يكون بصيرا .(ج) (٣) هو أمر وجودي يضاد الكلام عند أهل السنة، وأما عند المعتزلة فهو عدم الكلام عما من شأنه أن يكون متكلما .(ج)
His hearing is not (limited to) sounds without the essences, nor is He having hearing with ears or ear canals or other such things; rather, His hearing, exalted, pertains to every existent. (1) And it is impossible for Him, exalted, to have blindness, which is the opposite of sight; the reality of blindness is the complete absence of sight due to the existence of an impediment. (2) [؟] prevents that. Likewise, it is impossible for Him, exalted, to have what is in its meaning: the eyeball, eyelids, pupil, and other such things. And it is impossible for Him, exalted, to have muteness: it is the opposite of speech, and the reality of muteness is the complete absence of speech due to the existence of an impediment that prevents that. (3) Likewise, it is impossible for Him, exalted, to have what is in its meaning: that His speech be with letters and sounds and other such temporal attributes. As for the impossibility of the opposites and contradictories of the essential attributes, it is clear, and its clarity increases after knowing the impossibility of the opposites of the meanings as previously mentioned. (1) Imam al-Sanusi said in Sharh Sughra al-Sughra: 'Our statement concerning hearing and sight: they pertain to all existents. That is, all existents, whether eternal or temporal, are disclosed to His hearing and sight. It is not like the hearing of a creature, whose relation is usually confined to sounds, nor like the sight of a creature, which is usually related only to bodies, colors, and accidents. The proof for the universality of relation for His hearing is that the corrector of their relation is only existence. So if they were related to some existents to the exclusion of others, they would need a specifier, and thus they would be temporal, and the occurrence of temporal things in His essence is impossible. The conclusion is that the affirmation of these two attributes is taken from revelation, and their relation to all existents is taken from rational proof.' End quote. Imam al-Sanusi means by rational proof that this matter requires further consideration and elaboration, and it is not sufficient to rely on what appears at first glance, whether in rational or transmitted matters. Some scholars disagreed with him concerning these two attributes pertaining to existents, and said they pertain rather to audible and visible things. Al-Sanusi refuted him at length, and there are well-known correspondences between them; it is a subtle matter. In any case, it is not permissible to suppose that 'audibles' are merely sounds, because what is relied upon by most Ahl al-Sunna is the hearing of the internal speech of God, and that God, exalted, hears His internal speech, which is neither sound nor letter. Likewise, it is not said that 'visibles' are only colors, because God sees Himself, and He is not a color nor does He have a color. So the intelligent person must bring to mind these sparks, for they bring the words of Imam al-Sanusi closer to his intellect, and he should not rush to oppose before understanding. This matter is among the subtle points of kalam, and we have a verification of it that this concise work cannot contain. So reflect. (S). (2) It is an existent thing that opposes sight according to Ahl al-Sunna; as for the Mu'tazila, it is the absence of sight from that which should be seeing. (J). (3) It is an existent thing that opposes speech according to Ahl al-Sunna; as for the Mu'tazila, it is the absence of speech from that which should be speaking. (J).
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is a continuous exposition of several divine attributes and their opposites, beginning with the nature of God's hearing and sight, then explaining the impossibility of their opposites (blindness and muteness), and finally clarifying the scope of these attributes through a long editorial note. The author first states that God's hearing is not like that of creatures: it is not limited to sounds, nor does it require ears or ear canals. Rather, God's hearing encompasses every existent thing, whether eternal or temporal. Similarly, sight encompasses all existents. The proof given in the footnote is that the only basis for the relation of hearing and sight to an object is its existence; if they related only to some things, they would require a specifier, making them temporal, which is impossible for God. Thus, the universality of relation is proven rationally, while the existence of the attributes themselves is taken from revelation. The passage then turns to the impossibility of deficiencies: blindness is the opposite of sight, defined as the complete absence of sight due to an impediment, and this is impossible for God. Likewise, muteness is the opposite of speech, defined as the complete absence of speech due to an impediment, also impossible for God. The footnote clarifies that for Ahl al-Sunna, these opposites (e.g., blindness) are positive existent things that oppose the attribute, whereas for the Mu'tazila they are mere absences. The passage also explicitly states that God's speech cannot be composed of letters or sounds, as that would be temporal. Finally, the footnote warns against misunderstanding 'audibles' and 'visibles': audibles include God's internal speech, which is not sound, and visibles include God Himself, who is not a color. The commentary thus guards against anthropomorphic interpretations.

بصر
موجودات
آفة
مخصص
حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's hearing and sight are like human senses, limited to sounds and colors.
Correction: God's hearing and sight relate to all existents, not just sounds or colors, as proven by rational necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must God's hearing and sight relate to all existents?
A) Because revelation states so explicitly.
B) Because the basis of relation is existence; otherwise a specifier would be needed, making them temporal.
C) Because God has ears and eyes like humans.
D) Because only sounds and colors are real.

Key Takeaway
God's hearing and sight are universal, relating to all existents, based on the rational proof that limitation would require a specifier and thus temporality, which is impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's hearing encompasses every thought you whisper and every word you speak, does that change how you speak when you think no one is listening? Will you commit from now on to never say anything you would be ashamed for God to hear?
?
Knowing that God sees every action—both public and secret—how does that confront your hidden deeds that you hope no one ever finds out? What is one specific habit you will change today because you know God sees it?
?
This lesson destroys the idea that God is limited like us. Are you still comfortable with a god made in your image—one who only cares about what you consider big or small? Will you let go of that false image and humble yourself before the One whose perception is unlimited?

The Impossibility of Blindness and Muteness for God
Sanusi explains that blindness and muteness are impossible for God, defines them as absences due to impediments, and notes that God's speech is not with letters/sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why blindness and muteness are impossible for God, their definitions, and the distinction between Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila on these opposites.

Core Idea
Blindness and muteness are existent impediments that cannot be attributed to God.

Having established the positive attributes, Sanusi now addresses their opposites. He defines blindness and muteness as the complete absence of sight or speech due to an impediment, and demonstrates why such imperfections are impossible for God. He also stresses that God's speech is not composed of letters or sounds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on blindness
Read the Arabic text on blindness – its definition as absence of sight due to an impediment, and notice it also includes related body parts.
2
Read the passage on muteness
Similar definition as absence of speech due to an impediment, and the negation of letters/sounds for God's speech.
3
Examine footnotes (2) and (3)
These explain the difference between Ahl al-Sunna (impediments are existent) and Mu'tazila (mere absence).
This distinction matters for understanding the nature of attributes.
4
Connect to essential attributes
The final sentence notes that impossibility of opposites of essential attributes is clear after knowing impossibility of opposites of meanings.
This ties back to earlier lessons.

His hearing is not (limited to) sounds without the essences, nor is He having hearing with ears or ear canals or other such things; rather, His hearing, exalted, pertains to every existent. Commentary
This passage is a continuous exposition of several divine attributes and their opposites, beginning with the nature of God's hearing and sight, then explaining the impossibility of their opposites (blindness and muteness), and finally clarifying the scope of these attributes through a long editorial note. The author first states that God's hearing is not like that of creatures: it is not limited to sounds, nor does it require ears or ear canals. Rather, God's hearing encompasses every existent thing, whether eternal or temporal. Similarly, sight encompasses all existents. The proof given in the footnote is that the only basis for the relation of hearing and sight to an object is its existence; if they related only to some things, they would require a specifier, making them temporal, which is impossible for God. Thus, the universality of relation is proven rationally, while the existence of the attributes themselves is taken from revelation. The passage then turns to the impossibility of deficiencies: blindness is the opposite of sight, defined as the complete absence of sight due to an impediment, and this is impossible for God. Likewise, muteness is the opposite of speech, defined as the complete absence of speech due to an impediment, also impossible for God. The footnote clarifies that for Ahl al-Sunna, these opposites (e.g., blindness) are positive existent things that oppose the attribute, whereas for the Mu'tazila they are mere absences. The passage also explicitly states that God's speech cannot be composed of letters or sounds, as that would be temporal. Finally, the footnote warns against misunderstanding 'audibles' and 'visibles': audibles include God's internal speech, which is not sound, and visibles include God Himself, who is not a color. The commentary thus guards against anthropomorphic interpretations.
