The Rational Proof for Hearing, Sight, and Speech
The author states the rational proof for hearing, sight, and speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument that God must possess the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech because their opposites are deficiencies impossible for God.

Core Idea
If God lacked these attributes, He would have their opposites, which are impossible deficiencies.

This lesson focuses on the rational proof for the necessity of God's hearing, sight, and speech. The author argues from the impossibility of deficiency in God. Let us examine the logical steps of this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the rational proof
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow of the argument. Then read the translation carefully.
Notice how the author moves from condition to conclusion.
2
Identify the logical premises
Find the key premises: (1) If God lacked these attributes, He would have their opposites. (2) Opposites are deficiencies. (3) Deficiency in God is impossible. (4) A locus must be described by a property or its opposite.
3
Understand the conclusion
See how the premises lead to the conclusion that God must possess hearing, sight, and speech. Reflect on why deficiency is impossible (it implies need).
Deficiency implies need, contradicting God's self-sufficiency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان وجوب السمع والبصر والكلام: والدليل على ثبوت هذه الصفات الكتاب والسنة والعقل، فأما الكتاب والسنة فقد مر ذكر دليل عليها منهما، وأما العقل فلأنه تعالى لو لم يتصف بهذه الصفات لزم أن يتصف بأضدادها التي هي الصمم والعمى والبكم، وهذه الأضداد هي نقائص لا تجوز نسبتها إلى المولى العظيم فالنقص عليه محال، بمعنى أنه ممتنع عقلا ونقلا، فلو لم يتصف بها لزم أن يتصف بأضدادها لأن المحل القابل للوصف لا يخلو عن الاتصاف به أو بمثله أو بضده، لكن اتصافه بأضدادها محال لأنها نقائص والنقص عليه تعالى محال لأنه يلزم عليه احتياجه إلى من يدفع عنه النقص، والاحتياج ينافي الاستغناء، ونفي الاستغناء عنه تعالى محال. (1) إن قلت إن الكتاب والسنة والإجماع إنما دلت على أنه تعالى سميع بصير متكلم، لا على أنه متصف بصفات هي السمع والبصر والكلام كما هو قول أهل السنة. فالجواب: إن أهل اللغة لا يفهمون من سميع وبصير ومتكلم إلا ذاتاً متصفة بالسمع والبصر والكلام لأن من لم يقم به وصف لا يشتق له منه اسم، فلا يقال قائم إلا لمن اتصف بالقيام ولا قاعد إلا لمن اتصف بالقعود، وهكذا، فاستدلال أهل السنة بالكتاب والسنة والإجماع إنما يعنون به الاستدلال بها مع اعتبار ما فهمه أهل اللغة لا الاستدلال بها وحدها. (غ) بتصرف يسير. (2) حاصل هذا الدليل كما يلي: هذه الأضداد نقائص والنقص عليه تعالى محال عقلا ونقلا كما مر، ينتج أن هذه الأضداد مستحيلة عليه تعالى إذن ما دام الاتصاف بأضداد هذه الصفات مستحيلاً، يلزم وجوب اتصافه تعالى بها، أي بالسمع والبصر والكلام قال البيجوري: وقد تقدم ضعف ذلك بأنه لا يلزم من كونها نقائص في الشاهد أن تكون نقائص في الغائب. أهـ، أي إننا مع تسليمنا بأن النقص مستحيل على الله تعالى عقلا ونقلا، إلا أننا لا نسلم أن عدم اتصافه تعالى بالسمع والبصر والكلام يلزم منه النقص عقلا، لأن العقل يستعيض عن السمع والبصر بالعلم، وعن الكلام بالقدرة والإرادة ولقائل أن يقول سلمنا أن العقل يمكنه الاستغناء عن السمع والبصر بالعلم، لكن لا نسلم الاستغناء عن الكلام مطلقاً بالقدرة والإرادة، لأن الذي يلزم الاستغناء عنه بذلك إنما هو الكلام الذي بحرف وصوت أما الكلام النفسي فكيف يتم بيان الاستغناء عنه؟! وهو قوي والله أعلم. (س)
Proof of the necessity of hearing, sight, and speech: The evidence for the affirmation of these attributes is the Book, the Sunna, and reason. As for the Book and the Sunna, evidence for them from those two sources has already been mentioned. As for reason, because if He, exalted, were not qualified with these attributes, He would necessarily be qualified with their opposites, which are deafness, blindness, and muteness. These opposites are deficiencies that are not permissible to attribute to the great Lord. For deficiency is impossible with respect to Him, meaning it is impossible rationally and transmittedly. So if He were not qualified with them, He would necessarily be qualified with their opposites, because a locus that is receptive to descriptions cannot be free from being described by that description or by its equivalent or by its opposite. But His being described by their opposites is impossible because they are deficiencies, and deficiency with respect to Him, exalted, is impossible because it would entail His need for someone to ward off deficiency from Him, and need contradicts self-sufficiency, and the negation of self-sufficiency with respect to Him is impossible. (1) If you say: 'The Book, the Sunna, and the consensus only indicate that He is Hearing, Seeing, Speaking, not that He is qualified with attributes that are hearing, sight, and speech as is the view of Ahl al-Sunna,' the answer: The people of language do not understand from 'Hearing,' 'Seeing,' and 'Speaking' except a self qualified with hearing, sight, and speech, because one in whom a description does not subsist cannot be derived for him a name from it. So one is not called 'standing' except for one qualified with standing, nor 'sitting' except for one qualified with sitting, and so on. So the inference of Ahl al-Sunna by the Book, the Sunna, and the consensus means inference by them with consideration of what the people of language understand, not inference by them alone. (2) The conclusion of this proof is as follows: These opposites are deficiencies, and deficiency with respect to Him is impossible rationally and transmittedly as has passed, producing that these opposites are impossible for Him. So as long as being described with the opposites of these attributes is impossible, the necessity of His being described with them follows, i.e., with hearing, sight, and speech. Al-Bayjuri said: 'The weakness of that has already been stated: that just because something is a deficiency in the witnessed realm does not necessarily mean it is a deficiency in the unseen realm.' End quote. That is, while we concede that deficiency is impossible for God rationally and transmittedly, we do not concede that the absence of His being described with hearing, sight, and speech entails deficiency rationally, because reason can substitute for hearing and sight by knowledge, and for speech by power and will. And one may say: 'We concede that reason can dispense with hearing and sight through knowledge, but we do not concede dispensing with speech absolutely through power and will, because what can be dispensed with thereby is only speech that consists of letter and sound. As for mental speech (al-kalam al-nafsi), how can dispensing with it be demonstrated?' And it is strong, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of God having the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech. The author cites scriptural and rational arguments. The rational argument is that if God did not have these attributes, He would have their opposites, which are deficiencies, and deficiency in God is impossible. A locus must be described by a property, its opposite, or an equivalent. Since opposites are impossible, the attributes must exist. Then an objection is raised: scripture only says He is Hearing, Seeing, Speaking, not that He has distinct attributes. The answer is that linguistic usage implies that these names require a subsisting attribute. Then a critique from al-Bayjuri is presented: the deficiency argument is weak because what is deficiency in the created may not be in the Creator. Reason could substitute hearing/sight with knowledge and speech with power/will. However, a rebuttal notes that substitution may work for external speech but not for internal speech (kalam nafsi). This shows the complexity of the kalam debate.

Key Terms
سمع
بصر
كلام
النقص
المحل القابل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the rational proof, why must God have hearing, sight, and speech?
A) Because scripture commands it
B) Because not having them would lead to opposites which are deficiencies
C) Because He needs them to perceive
D) Because they are accidental attributes

Key Takeaway
The rational proof for God's hearing, sight, and speech rests on the impossibility of deficiency in God and the logical necessity that a locus is described by a property or its opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that deficiency implies need. When you feel deficient in your own life, do you recognize your total dependence on God? How can you turn that feeling into a deeper reliance on Him today?
?
If you truly believed that God hears, sees, and speaks perfectly at all times, would you change any habit, thought, or private action? What is one thing you will start or stop doing this week because of this reality?
?
The argument makes it clear that God's attributes are necessary, not optional. Does your worship reflect a conviction that God is necessarily all-hearing and all-seeing, or do you sometimes act as if He is unaware? What specific step can you take to live in greater awareness of His perpetual presence?

The Linguistic Objection and Its Answer
The author presents an objection and its answer regarding the interpretation of divine names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that scripture only gives names, not attributes, and the response that linguistic usage implies attributes.

Core Idea
When God is called 'Hearing,' language requires that He possesses the attribute of hearing.

An opponent argues that scripture only calls God 'Hearing, Seeing, Speaking' without affirming distinct attributes. The author responds by appealing to language: we only call someone 'standing' if they have the attribute of standing. This lesson unpacks that rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully: it claims scripture only gives names (سميع, بصير, متكلم) not attributes (سمع, بصر, كلام).
This is a common objection from those who deny that God has distinct attributes.
2
Read the answer
The author replies that language users understand these names to imply a self qualified with the corresponding attribute. Examples of 'standing' and 'sitting' illustrate the principle.
The principle: one in whom a description does not subsist cannot be named from it.
3
Reflect on the implication
Ahl al-Sunna's inference from scripture includes this linguistic understanding, so they affirm attributes based on the names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان وجوب السمع والبصر والكلام: والدليل على ثبوت هذه الصفات الكتاب والسنة والعقل، فأما الكتاب والسنة فقد مر ذكر دليل عليها منهما، وأما العقل فلأنه تعالى لو لم يتصف بهذه الصفات لزم أن يتصف بأضدادها التي هي الصمم والعمى والبكم، وهذه الأضداد هي نقائص لا تجوز نسبتها إلى المولى العظيم فالنقص عليه محال، بمعنى أنه ممتنع عقلا ونقلا، فلو لم يتصف بها لزم أن يتصف بأضدادها لأن المحل القابل للوصف لا يخلو عن الاتصاف به أو بمثله أو بضده، لكن اتصافه بأضدادها محال لأنها نقائص والنقص عليه تعالى محال لأنه يلزم عليه احتياجه إلى من يدفع عنه النقص، والاحتياج ينافي الاستغناء، ونفي الاستغناء عنه تعالى محال. (1) إن قلت إن الكتاب والسنة والإجماع إنما دلت على أنه تعالى سميع بصير متكلم، لا على أنه متصف بصفات هي السمع والبصر والكلام كما هو قول أهل السنة. فالجواب: إن أهل اللغة لا يفهمون من سميع وبصير ومتكلم إلا ذاتاً متصفة بالسمع والبصر والكلام لأن من لم يقم به وصف لا يشتق له منه اسم، فلا يقال قائم إلا لمن اتصف بالقيام ولا قاعد إلا لمن اتصف بالقعود، وهكذا، فاستدلال أهل السنة بالكتاب والسنة والإجماع إنما يعنون به الاستدلال بها مع اعتبار ما فهمه أهل اللغة لا الاستدلال بها وحدها. (غ) بتصرف يسير. (2) حاصل هذا الدليل كما يلي: هذه الأضداد نقائص والنقص عليه تعالى محال عقلا ونقلا كما مر، ينتج أن هذه الأضداد مستحيلة عليه تعالى إذن ما دام الاتصاف بأضداد هذه الصفات مستحيلاً، يلزم وجوب اتصافه تعالى بها، أي بالسمع والبصر والكلام قال البيجوري: وقد تقدم ضعف ذلك بأنه لا يلزم من كونها نقائص في الشاهد أن تكون نقائص في الغائب. أهـ، أي إننا مع تسليمنا بأن النقص مستحيل على الله تعالى عقلا ونقلا، إلا أننا لا نسلم أن عدم اتصافه تعالى بالسمع والبصر والكلام يلزم منه النقص عقلا، لأن العقل يستعيض عن السمع والبصر بالعلم، وعن الكلام بالقدرة والإرادة ولقائل أن يقول سلمنا أن العقل يمكنه الاستغناء عن السمع والبصر بالعلم، لكن لا نسلم الاستغناء عن الكلام مطلقاً بالقدرة والإرادة، لأن الذي يلزم الاستغناء عنه بذلك إنما هو الكلام الذي بحرف وصوت أما الكلام النفسي فكيف يتم بيان الاستغناء عنه؟! وهو قوي والله أعلم. (س)
Proof of the necessity of hearing, sight, and speech: The evidence for the affirmation of these attributes is the Book, the Sunna, and reason. As for the Book and the Sunna, evidence for them from those two sources has already been mentioned. As for reason, because if He, exalted, were not qualified with these attributes, He would necessarily be qualified with their opposites, which are deafness, blindness, and muteness. These opposites are deficiencies that are not permissible to attribute to the great Lord. For deficiency is impossible with respect to Him, meaning it is impossible rationally and transmittedly. So if He were not qualified with them, He would necessarily be qualified with their opposites, because a locus that is receptive to descriptions cannot be free from being described by that description or by its equivalent or by its opposite. But His being described by their opposites is impossible because they are deficiencies, and deficiency with respect to Him, exalted, is impossible because it would entail His need for someone to ward off deficiency from Him, and need contradicts self-sufficiency, and the negation of self-sufficiency with respect to Him is impossible. (1) If you say: 'The Book, the Sunna, and the consensus only indicate that He is Hearing, Seeing, Speaking, not that He is qualified with attributes that are hearing, sight, and speech as is the view of Ahl al-Sunna,' the answer: The people of language do not understand from 'Hearing,' 'Seeing,' and 'Speaking' except a self qualified with hearing, sight, and speech, because one in whom a description does not subsist cannot be derived for him a name from it. So one is not called 'standing' except for one qualified with standing, nor 'sitting' except for one qualified with sitting, and so on. So the inference of Ahl al-Sunna by the Book, the Sunna, and the consensus means inference by them with consideration of what the people of language understand, not inference by them alone. (2) The conclusion of this proof is as follows: These opposites are deficiencies, and deficiency with respect to Him is impossible rationally and transmittedly as has passed, producing that these opposites are impossible for Him. So as long as being described with the opposites of these attributes is impossible, the necessity of His being described with them follows, i.e., with hearing, sight, and speech. Al-Bayjuri said: 'The weakness of that has already been stated: that just because something is a deficiency in the witnessed realm does not necessarily mean it is a deficiency in the unseen realm.' End quote. That is, while we concede that deficiency is impossible for God rationally and transmittedly, we do not concede that the absence of His being described with hearing, sight, and speech entails deficiency rationally, because reason can substitute for hearing and sight by knowledge, and for speech by power and will. And one may say: 'We concede that reason can dispense with hearing and sight through knowledge, but we do not concede dispensing with speech absolutely through power and will, because what can be dispensed with thereby is only speech that consists of letter and sound. As for mental speech (al-kalam al-nafsi), how can dispensing with it be demonstrated?' And it is strong, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of God having the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech. The author cites scriptural and rational arguments. The rational argument is that if God did not have these attributes, He would have their opposites, which are deficiencies, and deficiency in God is impossible. A locus must be described by a property, its opposite, or an equivalent. Since opposites are impossible, the attributes must exist. Then an objection is raised: scripture only says He is Hearing, Seeing, Speaking, not that He has distinct attributes. The answer is that linguistic usage implies that these names require a subsisting attribute. Then a critique from al-Bayjuri is presented: the deficiency argument is weak because what is deficiency in the created may not be in the Creator. Reason could substitute hearing/sight with knowledge and speech with power/will. However, a rebuttal notes that substitution may work for external speech but not for internal speech (kalam nafsi). This shows the complexity of the kalam debate.

بصير
متكلم
اشتقاق
أهل اللغة

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the answer to the objection?
A) Consensus of scholars
B) Linguistic usage
C) Reason alone
D) The Bible

Key Takeaway
The names of God in scripture imply attributes according to the rules of language.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call Allah As-Sami', do you truly live with the conviction that He hears every whisper of your heart, or has it become a mere phrase? What would change in your private worship if you internalized that this name guarantees His real attribute of hearing?
?
The linguistic rule is clear: a name implies an attribute. Are there any names of Allah you recite without pausing to consider the attribute they reveal? Pick one such name today and commit to learning its meaning deeply before your next prayer.
?
The objector claimed scripture only gives names, not attributes. Have you ever reduced any of Allah's names to empty titles, treating them as labels rather than windows to His real qualities? What step will you take this week to bridge that gap?

Critique and Rebuttal of the Rational Proof
The author presents the final logical form of the proof, a critique, and a rebuttal concerning mental speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critique that the rational proof is weak because what is a deficiency in creation may not be deficiency in the Creator, and the subsequent defense regarding mental speech.

Core Idea
The rational proof faces a critique: reason could substitute for hearing and sight with knowledge, but mental speech is not so easily replaced.

This lesson presents the concluding summary of the proof, a critique by al-Bayjuri that the deficiency argument does not necessarily apply to God, and a defense that while reason might substitute for hearing and sight, it cannot substitute for mental speech (kalam nafsi).

Guided Path
1
Follow the restatement of the proof
Read the conclusion of the proof: opposites are deficiencies, deficiency impossible, so opposites impossible, thus attributes necessary.
2
Understand al-Bayjuri's critique
Al-Bayjuri notes that what counts as a deficiency in the created realm (shahid) may not be a deficiency in the Unseen (gha'ib). He suggests reason can substitute for hearing/sight with knowledge, and for speech with power/will.
This challenges the transfer of deficiency from the witnessed to the divine realm.
3
Consider the rebuttal about mental speech
A rebuttal argues that substitution works only for external speech (letters and sounds), not for mental speech (kalam nafsi), which is an eternal attribute of God. Thus the proof stands for mental speech.
The author notes the strength of this rebuttal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson reveals that mental speech is an essential attribute of God that cannot be replaced by any other attribute. Do you truly believe that the Qur’an is God’s eternal mental speech, or do you sometimes treat it as a mere human book? This week, before you recite the Qur’an, pause and say: 'This is the attribute of the Almighty speaking to me.' Then recite with full awareness.
?
The critique in this passage suggests that reason could replace hearing and sight with knowledge. Do you ever rely more on your own reasoning than on the fact that God hears and sees every detail of your life? Commit today to remembering that His hearing and sight are direct and personal—not substitutes. Repent from moments when you acted as if He doesn't see.
?
The proof relies on the impossibility of deficiency in God. But have you ever, in times of hardship, questioned God's wisdom and thereby implicitly attributed deficiency to Him? If so, take a moment now to repent and affirm His absolute perfection and wisdom, even when you cannot see it.

Divine Attributes

Rational Proof for Hearing, Sight, and Speech
Sanusi presents the rational argument for the necessity of hearing, sight, and speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument for why God must possess the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech.

Core Idea
If God lacked these attributes, He would have their opposites—deficiencies impossible for the Self-Sufficient.

This lesson examines the rational proof that establishes the necessity of God's attributes of hearing, sight, and speech. Sanusi argues that the alternative would be deficiency, which contradicts God's perfection and self-sufficiency. The proof relies on the logical principle that a locus must either have an attribute, its like, or its opposite.

Guided Path
1
Read the rational proof passage
Read the Arabic passage from the start to where it mentions rational impossibility. Then read the literal translation. Notice the logical structure: if not X then opposite Y, Y is deficiency, deficiency impossible, so X must exist.
The passage begins with 'والدليل على ثبوت هذه الصفات' and ends with 'ونفي الاستغناء عنه تعالى محال.'
2
Identify the key logical steps
Break down the argument: (1) God must either have hearing, sight, speech or their opposites. (2) Opposites are deficiencies. (3) Deficiency in God is impossible because it implies need. (4) Need contradicts self-sufficiency. Therefore God must have the positive attributes.
The proof uses the principle of a 'locus capable of being described' (المحل القابل للوصف).
3
Reflect on the implication of need
Consider why deficiency would imply need. If God were deaf, He would need someone to make Him hear, but He is self-sufficient. This shows the rational necessity of the attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على ثبوت هذه الصفات الكتاب والسنة والعقل، فأما الكتاب والسنة فقد مر ذكر دليل عليها منهما، وأما العقل فلأنه تعالى لو لم يتصف بهذه الصفات لزم أن يتصف بأضدادها التي هي الصمم والعمى والبكم، وهذه الأضداد هي نقائص لا تجوز نسبتها إلى المولى العظيم فالنقص عليه محال، بمعنى أنه ممتنع عقلا ونقلا، فلو لم يتصف بها لزم أن يتصف بأضدادها لأن المحل القابل للوصف لا يخلو عن الاتصاف به أو بمثله أو بضده، لكن اتصافه بأضدادها محال لأنها نقائص والنقص عليه تعالى محال لأنه يلزم عليه احتياجه إلى من يدفع عنه النقص، والاحتياج ينافي الاستغناء، ونفي الاستغناء عنه تعالى محال. (۱) إن قلت إن الكتاب والسنة والإجماع إنما دلت على أنه تعالى سميع بصير متكلم، لا على أنه متصف بصفات هي السمع والبصر والكلام كما هو قول أهل السنة. فالجواب : إن أهل اللغة لا يفهمون من سميع وبصير ومتكلم إلا ذاتاً متصفة بالسمع والبصر والكلام لأن من لم يقم به وصف لا يشتق له منه اسم، فلا يقال قائم إلا لمن اتصف بالقيام ولا قاعد إلا لمن اتصف بالقعود، وهكذا، فاستدلال أهل السنة بالكتاب والسنة والإجماع إنما يعنون به الاستدلال بها مع اعتبار ما فهمه أهل اللغة لا الاستدلال بها وحدها. (غ) بتصرف يسير. ملاحظة : اعلم أن الدليل النقلي في السمع والبصر والكلام أقوى من العقلي.
The proof for the establishment of these attributes is the Book, the Sunnah, and reason. As for the Book and the Sunnah, the proof from them has already been mentioned. As for reason, if He, exalted, were not characterized by these attributes, then He would necessarily be characterized by their opposites, which are deafness, blindness, and muteness. These opposites are deficiencies that are not permissible to attribute to the Great Master, for deficiency is impossible with respect to Him, meaning it is rationally and transmittedly impossible. If He were not characterized by them, He would necessarily be characterized by their opposites, because a locus capable of being described is not free from being described by it, by its like, or by its opposite. But His being described by their opposites is impossible because they are deficiencies, and deficiency with respect to Him, exalted, is impossible because it would necessitate His need for one who would remove the deficiency from Him, and need contradicts self-sufficiency, and negating self-sufficiency with respect to Him, exalted, is impossible. (1) If you say: The Book, Sunnah, and consensus only indicate that He, exalted, is Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking, not that He is described by attributes that are hearing, sight, and speech as the Ahl al-Sunnah claim. The answer: The people of language understand from 'Hearing,' 'Seeing,' and 'Speaking' nothing but an essence characterized by hearing, sight, and speech, because from one who does not have a description, a name derived from it is not coined. One does not say 'standing' except for one who is characterized by standing, nor 'sitting' except for one who is characterized by sitting, and so on. Thus, the Ahl al-Sunnah's proof by the Book, Sunnah, and consensus is meant as proof by them while considering what the people of language understood, not proof by them alone. (Gh) with slight modification. Note: Know that the transmitted proof concerning hearing, sight, and speech is stronger than the rational one.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the rational proof for God's attributes of hearing, sight, and speech, and then addresses an objection about the nature of the proof. The author begins by stating that the proof for these attributes comes from three sources: the Qur'an, the Sunnah, and reason. The scriptural proofs have already been mentioned. The rational proof is then detailed: if God were not characterized by hearing, sight, and speech, He would necessarily be characterized by their opposites—deafness, blindness, and muteness. These opposites are deficiencies, and deficiency is impossible for God. The reasoning is that a locus capable of being described must either be described by the attribute, its like, or its opposite. Since the opposites are deficiencies and deficiency is impossible for God (as it would imply need, contradicting His self-sufficiency), God must possess the positive attributes. The author then presents an objection: the scriptural texts only refer to God as 'Hearing, Seeing, Speaking' as names, not as attributes. The response is that linguistic usage implies that a name derived from an attribute indicates that the subject is characterized by that attribute. For example, 'standing' is only said of one who actually stands. Therefore, when the Ahl al-Sunnah use scriptural proofs, they do so with the understanding of language, not merely the bare texts. The author concludes with a note that the transmitted proof (from scripture) for these attributes is stronger than the rational proof.

سمع
بصر
كلام
صفات
نقائص
احتياج
استغناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's attributes like hearing are merely metaphorical or that the names do not correspond to real attributes.
Correction: The rational proof shows that the alternative (deficiency) is impossible, so the positive attributes must be real.

Checkpoint
According to the rational proof, what would necessarily follow if God did not possess the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech?
A) He would have no attributes at all
B) He would be characterized by their opposites
C) He would still be called Hearing and Seeing by convention
D) He would be imperfect but still worthy of worship

Key Takeaway
God must possess hearing, sight, and speech because their opposites would entail deficiency, which is incompatible with His self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's self-sufficiency means He has no need for anything, why do you sometimes act as if your actions can add to or subtract from His perfection? How does this lesson confront your tendency to think you can 'impress' God?
?
The passage states that deficiency implies need. In which areas of your life are you relying on something other than God to fill a sense of lack? What would it mean for you to truly believe that God alone is sufficient?
?
If God necessarily sees and hears all, including the secrets of your heart, what specific habit or thought would you change starting today, knowing that He is fully aware and self-sufficient?

Linguistic Response to the Objection
Sanusi responds to the objection that scriptural names do not imply attributes, using linguistic derivation principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that scriptural names for God do not imply attributes and the linguistic response that refutes it.

Core Idea
Language itself shows that a name derived from an attribute implies the attribute exists in the subject.

After presenting the rational proof, Sanusi addresses a potential objection: the Qur'an and Sunnah only call God Hearing, Seeing, Speaking, but do they prove He has the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech? The response relies on linguistic norms: the very derivation of these names indicates the presence of the attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the passage starting from 'إن قلت' to the end of the response. Notice the structure: objection followed by answer.
The objection claims that the texts only give names, not attributes. The response uses linguistic derivation.
2
Understand the objection's claim
The objection says that the Book, Sunnah, and consensus only prove that God is described as Hearing, Seeing, Speaking, not that He has the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech as distinct entities.
This is a subtle point: names vs. attributes.
3
See how the linguistic argument refutes it
The response states that the people of language understand from 'Hearing,' 'Seeing,' 'Speaking' an essence characterized by those attributes. A name derived from an attribute requires the attribute to be present. For example, 'standing' is only said of one who stands.
This shows that the Ahl al-Sunnah's use of scripture is based on linguistic understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل على ثبوت هذه الصفات الكتاب والسنة والعقل، فأما الكتاب والسنة فقد مر ذكر دليل عليها منهما، وأما العقل فلأنه تعالى لو لم يتصف بهذه الصفات لزم أن يتصف بأضدادها التي هي الصمم والعمى والبكم، وهذه الأضداد هي نقائص لا تجوز نسبتها إلى المولى العظيم فالنقص عليه محال، بمعنى أنه ممتنع عقلا ونقلا، فلو لم يتصف بها لزم أن يتصف بأضدادها لأن المحل القابل للوصف لا يخلو عن الاتصاف به أو بمثله أو بضده، لكن اتصافه بأضدادها محال لأنها نقائص والنقص عليه تعالى محال لأنه يلزم عليه احتياجه إلى من يدفع عنه النقص، والاحتياج ينافي الاستغناء، ونفي الاستغناء عنه تعالى محال. (۱) إن قلت إن الكتاب والسنة والإجماع إنما دلت على أنه تعالى سميع بصير متكلم، لا على أنه متصف بصفات هي السمع والبصر والكلام كما هو قول أهل السنة. فالجواب : إن أهل اللغة لا يفهمون من سميع وبصير ومتكلم إلا ذاتاً متصفة بالسمع والبصر والكلام لأن من لم يقم به وصف لا يشتق له منه اسم، فلا يقال قائم إلا لمن اتصف بالقيام ولا قاعد إلا لمن اتصف بالقعود، وهكذا، فاستدلال أهل السنة بالكتاب والسنة والإجماع إنما يعنون به الاستدلال بها مع اعتبار ما فهمه أهل اللغة لا الاستدلال بها وحدها. (غ) بتصرف يسير. ملاحظة : اعلم أن الدليل النقلي في السمع والبصر والكلام أقوى من العقلي.
The proof for the establishment of these attributes is the Book, the Sunnah, and reason. As for the Book and the Sunnah, the proof from them has already been mentioned. As for reason, if He, exalted, were not characterized by these attributes, then He would necessarily be characterized by their opposites, which are deafness, blindness, and muteness. These opposites are deficiencies that are not permissible to attribute to the Great Master, for deficiency is impossible with respect to Him, meaning it is rationally and transmittedly impossible. If He were not characterized by them, He would necessarily be characterized by their opposites, because a locus capable of being described is not free from being described by it, by its like, or by its opposite. But His being described by their opposites is impossible because they are deficiencies, and deficiency with respect to Him, exalted, is impossible because it would necessitate His need for one who would remove the deficiency from Him, and need contradicts self-sufficiency, and negating self-sufficiency with respect to Him, exalted, is impossible. (1) If you say: The Book, Sunnah, and consensus only indicate that He, exalted, is Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking, not that He is described by attributes that are hearing, sight, and speech as the Ahl al-Sunnah claim. The answer: The people of language understand from 'Hearing,' 'Seeing,' and 'Speaking' nothing but an essence characterized by hearing, sight, and speech, because from one who does not have a description, a name derived from it is not coined. One does not say 'standing' except for one who is characterized by standing, nor 'sitting' except for one who is characterized by sitting, and so on. Thus, the Ahl al-Sunnah's proof by the Book, Sunnah, and consensus is meant as proof by them while considering what the people of language understood, not proof by them alone. (Gh) with slight modification. Note: Know that the transmitted proof concerning hearing, sight, and speech is stronger than the rational one.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage presents the rational proof for God's attributes of hearing, sight, and speech, and then addresses an objection about the nature of the proof. The author begins by stating that the proof for these attributes comes from three sources: the Qur'an, the Sunnah, and reason. The scriptural proofs have already been mentioned. The rational proof is then detailed: if God were not characterized by hearing, sight, and speech, He would necessarily be characterized by their opposites—deafness, blindness, and muteness. These opposites are deficiencies, and deficiency is impossible for God. The reasoning is that a locus capable of being described must either be described by the attribute, its like, or its opposite. Since the opposites are deficiencies and deficiency is impossible for God (as it would imply need, contradicting His self-sufficiency), God must possess the positive attributes. The author then presents an objection: the scriptural texts only refer to God as 'Hearing, Seeing, Speaking' as names, not as attributes. The response is that linguistic usage implies that a name derived from an attribute indicates that the subject is characterized by that attribute. For example, 'standing' is only said of one who actually stands. Therefore, when the Ahl al-Sunnah use scriptural proofs, they do so with the understanding of language, not merely the bare texts. The author concludes with a note that the transmitted proof (from scripture) for these attributes is stronger than the rational proof.

Misconception: Some might think that scriptural names for God are merely honorific titles without implying real attributes.
Correction: The passage clarifies that linguistic norms require that names derived from attributes indicate the presence of those attributes.

Checkpoint

B) Because the people of language understand it that way

D) Because of consensus

Key Takeaway
Scriptural names for God imply real attributes because language itself requires a name derived from an attribute to indicate that attribute in the subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Or do you merely repeat the words without letting that truth shape your awareness of His presence? Starting today, consciously pause when you say 'Al-Sami‘' and remind yourself that He hears you right now. What difference will that make in your next action?
?
The objector in this lesson separates the names from the attributes. Are there areas in your life where you separate what you claim to believe from how you actually live? Identify one specific gap—a belief you say you hold but don't act on—and commit to one concrete step today to close it.
?

Proof for Impossible Attributes via Necessary Attributes
Sanusi shows that proof for necessary attributes also proves impossibility of their opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the proof for God's necessary attributes simultaneously proves the impossibility of their opposites, based on the principle of non-contradiction.

Core Idea
Proving a positive attribute for God automatically negates its opposite.

In this lesson, Sanusi demonstrates a key logical principle in Islamic theology: proving that God possesses a necessary attribute automatically rules out its opposite. This is because contradictory attributes cannot coexist. By understanding this, you'll see how the same argument that establishes God's knowledge also decisively eliminates ignorance from His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Note the logical flow from general statement to example.
The passage is concise; pay attention to the phrase 'because the necessary is only established by negating the impossible'.
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument uses the impossibility of two opposites (knowing and not-knowing) coexisting. This is the law of non-contradiction.
Sanusi illustrates with the pair 'knowing' and 'ignorant'.
3
Apply to other attributes
The passage ends by stating the same logic applies to all attributes. Reflect on pairs like powerful/impotent, living/dead.
Consider how each positive attribute negates its opposite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان القسم الثانى وهو المستحيلات في حق الله إن براهين الواجبات هي نفسها تستلزم البرهان على المستحيلات في حق الله تعالى لأنه لا يثبت الواجب إلا بنفي المستحيل، فإنه يستحيل مثلاً أن يكون الله عالماً وغير عالم، لأنه لو جاز الجاز اجتماع النقيضين وهذا باطل إذن إما أن يكون عالماً أو جاهلاً، ولا قسم آخر ، فلما أثبتنا كونه عالماً بطل أن يكون جاهلاً. وهكذا القول في باقي الصفات.
The proof for the second category, which is the impossible things with respect to God: The proofs for the necessary things themselves necessitate the proof for the impossible things with respect to God, exalted, because the necessary is only established by negating the impossible. For example, it is impossible for God to be knowing and not knowing, because if it were possible, it would allow the conjunction of two opposites, and that is false. Therefore, He is either knowing or ignorant, with no other division. So when we established that He is knowing, it becomes false that He is ignorant. And so it is with the rest of the attributes.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage explains how proofs for God's necessary attributes simultaneously serve as proofs for the impossibility of their opposites. The author states that the proofs for the necessary attributes themselves necessitate the proof for the impossible attributes, because the necessary is only established by negating the impossible. He gives an example: it is impossible for God to be both knowing and not knowing simultaneously, as that would involve a contradiction. Therefore, God must be either knowing or ignorant, with no third option. Once we have proven that God is knowing, it follows necessarily that ignorance is impossible for Him. The same reasoning applies to all other attributes: establishing the positive attribute eliminates its opposite.

Key Terms
المستحيلات
الواجبات
النقيضين
العالم
الجاهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that proving a positive attribute does not automatically negate its opposite; they might consider it logically possible for God to have both or neither.
Correction: The passage clarifies that because two opposites cannot coexist, proving one positive attribute necessarily eliminates its opposite, leaving no third option.

Checkpoint
How does the proof for the necessary attributes also prove the impossible attributes?
A) By showing that God cannot lack any attribute
B) By demonstrating that if a positive attribute is proven, its opposite is impossible
C) By establishing that all attributes are equally necessary
D) By using transmitted proofs that explicitly list impossible attributes

Key Takeaway
The proof for a necessary attribute of God automatically proves the impossibility of its opposite due to the law of non-contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The proof uses logical necessity: if Allah is Knowing, He cannot be ignorant. Are you content to base your faith on rational certainty, or do you sometimes prefer comfortable assumptions that avoid the sharp edges of tawhid? Which attribute of Allah do you need to 'prove' today to remove a corresponding fear?

Proof of Impossibilities Derived from Necessities
Proof that establishing necessary attributes implicitly negates impossible ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how proofs of Allah's necessary attributes simultaneously prove the impossibility of their opposites.

Core Idea
Proving a necessary attribute automatically negates its impossible opposite.

This lesson explores a key logical principle in Islamic theology: establishing what Allah must be also establishes what He cannot be. The author shows that the very proof for a necessary attribute like knowledge serves as proof against its opposite, ignorance, because contradictories cannot coexist. Understanding this single argument unlocks the method for proving all attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the flow from general principle to specific example.
The passage is concise; each sentence carries logical weight.
2
Identify the logical structure
The passage presents a proof chain: the proof of necessities entails proof of impossibilities. Find the premise, the example, and the generalization.
3
Apply the reasoning
Try to apply the same reasoning to another attribute, such as power (qudra) and impotence (ajz).
This helps internalize the method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان القسم الثانى وهو المستحيلات في حق الله إن براهين الواجبات هي نفسها تستلزم البرهان على المستحيلات في حق الله تعالى لأنه لا يثبت الواجب إلا بنفي المستحيل، فإنه يستحيل مثلاً أن يكون الله عالماً وغير عالم، لأنه لو جاز الجاز اجتماع النقيضين وهذا باطل إذن إما أن يكون عالماً أو جاهلاً، ولا قسم آخر ، فلما أثبتنا كونه عالماً بطل أن يكون جاهلاً. وهكذا القول في باقي الصفات.
Proof of the second section, which is the impossibilities (al-mustaḥīlāt) regarding Allah: The proofs of the necessities (al-wājibāt) themselves entail the proof of the impossibilities regarding Allah, the Exalted, because the necessary is not established except by negating the impossible. For example, it is impossible that Allah be knowing and not-knowing, because if it were permissible, it would be permissible for two contradictories to coexist, and this is false. Therefore, either He is knowing or ignorant, and there is no other division. So when we proved that He is knowing, it is false that He is ignorant. And the same applies to the remaining attributes.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage provides a proof for the second category of judgments regarding Allah: impossibilities (al-mustaḥīlāt). The proof is that the same proofs that establish the necessary attributes (al-wājibāt) automatically prove the negation of their opposites (i.e., the impossibilities). Why? Because establishing a necessary attribute like knowledge implies that its opposite (ignorance) is impossible. The author gives the example: it is impossible for Allah to be both knowing and not-knowing, as that would require the coexistence of contradictories. Since contradictories cannot coexist, there is only a dichotomy: either He is knowing or ignorant. Once we prove He is knowing, the possibility of ignorance is negated, making it impossible. The same logic applies to all other attributes.

Key Terms
المستحيلات
البراهين
الواجبات
النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That impossibilities regarding Allah are proven by separate, independent proofs.
Correction: This passage shows that the same proofs that establish necessary attributes automatically prove the impossibilities, by negating the opposite.

Checkpoint

A) By separate proofs specifically for impossibilities.
B) By the same proofs that establish the necessary attributes.
C) By transmitted texts only.
D) By philosophical speculation without evidence.

Key Takeaway
The proofs that establish what Allah must be are the same proofs that establish what He cannot be.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Is there any attribute of Allah that you feel uncomfortable affirming, and if so, what does that reveal about the state of your heart?
?
If there is no third option between Allah being knowing and ignorant, and you affirm He is knowing, then how can you live as though He does not see your actions or hear your words? What would it take for you to let the reality of His all-encompassing knowledge transform your daily choices?

Allah's Freedom Regarding Contingent Things
Rational proof that no contingent thing obligates or prohibits Allah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why no contingent action is rationally necessary or impossible for Allah, and how the argument preserves divine freedom.

Core Idea
Allah is absolutely free; no contingent thing compels Him.

This lesson presents a key rational argument in Islamic theology: Allah's actions regarding contingent things are purely voluntary. The author, Imam Sanusi, provides a proof that it is impossible for any contingent action to be obligatory or prohibited for Allah by reason. By the end, you will see how this preserves divine freedom and avoid contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
Contingent things (al-mumkināt) are those whose existence and non-existence are both rationally conceivable. Grasp this definition before proceeding.
A contingent thing is neither necessary nor impossible by itself.
2
Follow the reductio argument
Assume a particular contingent action were rationally necessary or impossible for Allah. The author shows this leads to a contradiction: the contingent thing would cease to be contingent, and all contingents would become the same.
The contradiction arises because one thing cannot simultaneously accept both possibility and necessity.
3
See the conclusion and transmitted support
Since the assumption is false, no contingent action is necessary or impossible for Allah. This is also supported by Quran and Sunnah. Reflect on the implications for divine freedom.
The verse and hadith affirm that Allah does as He wills.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
برهان جواز فعل الممكنات وتركها : لو وجب عليه تعالى فعل شيء من الممكنات عقلاً أو استحال عقلاً لانقلب الممكن الذي صح في العقل وجوده وعدمه واجباً لا يتصور في العقل عدمه، فلو وجب عليه بعض الممكنات عقلاً لزم أن تنقلب كلها واجبة أو استحال بعضها عقلاً لزم أن تنقلب كلها مستحيلة وذلك باطل لا يتصور في العقل وجوده لأنه يؤدي إلى الجمع بين النقيضين لأنه بالنظر لكونه ممكناً يقبل الوجود والعدم وبالنظر لكونه واجباً لا يقبل إلا الوجود فقط، وبالنظر لكونه مستحيلا لا يقبل إلا العدم فقط، وكون الشيء الواحد يقبل الأمرين ولا يقبل إلا واحداً تناقض وهو باطل. ملاحظة : إذا وجب عليه البعض وجب الكل، وإذا استحال البعض استحال الكل لأنه لا فرق بين ممكن وممكن لأنه ما ثبت للأمر ثبت لمثيله. وحاصل الدليل أنه إذا بطل انقلاب الممكن واجباً أو مستحيلاً بطل وجوب البعض أو استحالته وإذا بطل وجوب البعض أو استحالته وجب أن الممكنات جائزة وهو المطلوب. إذن فجميع الممكنات لا يجب منها على الله فعل شيء ولا تركه لا شرعاً ولا عقلاً. ودليل النقل على جواز فعل الممكنات أو تركها من الكتاب قوله تعالى ﴿ وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَاءُ وَيَخْتَارُ ﴾ [القصص : ٦٨] ، ومن السنة قوله صلى الله عليه وسلم " ما شاء الله كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن " . (1) مرَّ تخريجه ، سابقاً انظر ص (٥٠). وبهذا نكون قد انتهينا من بيان ما يجب على المسلم معرفته مما يتعلق بالذات الإلهية، ونشرع بعون الله فيما يلي في بيان ما يتعلق بالرسل من أحكام.
Proof of the permissibility (jawāz) of doing contingent things (al-mumkināt) and leaving them: If it were necessary (wajaba) on Him, the Exalted, to do something of the contingent things rationally, or it were impossible (istaḥāla) rationally, then the contingent thing, which is rationally sound in its existence and non-existence, would turn into something necessary whose non-existence is not conceivable rationally. So if some contingent things were necessary for Him rationally, it would entail that all of them become necessary; or if some were impossible rationally, it would entail that all become impossible. And that is false, not conceivable rationally in its existence, because it leads to the conjunction of two contradictories. Because, with respect to its being contingent, it accepts existence and non-existence; with respect to its being necessary, it accepts only existence; and with respect to its being impossible, it accepts only non-existence. And one thing accepting two matters while only accepting one is a contradiction, and it is false. Note: If some are necessary for Him, then all are necessary; and if some are impossible, then all are impossible, because there is no difference between one contingent and another; for what is established for a thing is established for its like. The summary of the proof: if the change of the contingent into necessary or impossible is false, then the necessity or impossibility of some is false; and if the necessity or impossibility of some is false, then it is necessary that contingent things are permissible (jā’izah), and that is the desired conclusion. Therefore, of all contingent things, nothing is necessary for Allah to do or leave, neither by law nor by reason. The transmitted proof (dalīl al-naql) for the permissibility of doing or leaving contingent things from the Book is His saying, the Exalted: {And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses} [Qasas: 68]; and from the Sunnah is his saying, peace be upon him: "What Allah wills happens, and what He does not will does not happen." (1) Its chain was given previously; see p. 50. And with this we have finished explaining what is obligatory for the Muslim to know concerning the divine essence, and we begin, with Allah's help, in what follows to explain the rulings concerning the messengers.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage provides a proof that Allah is not compelled to do or leave any particular contingent thing. It is a classification of the second kind of proofs: proof of permissibility (jawāz). The author begins by stating the proof: If any contingent thing were rationally necessary or impossible for Allah, then that contingent would cease to be contingent, becoming necessary (if necessary) or impossible (if impossible). This would lead to a contradiction because a contingent thing by definition accepts both existence and non-existence, but if it becomes necessary it accepts only existence, and if impossible only non-existence. One and the same thing cannot accept both options while only accepting one—that is contradictory. Furthermore, if some contingent things were necessary, then all would be necessary because there is no distinction between them; similarly for impossibility. The summary: since turning a contingent into necessary or impossible is false, then no particular contingent is necessary or impossible, so all contingents remain permissible. The conclusion: Allah is not obligated to do or leave any contingent thing, neither by law nor by reason. The author then cites transmitted evidence: a Quranic verse emphasizing Allah's freedom to create and choose, and a hadith about His will. Finally, the author concludes the section on the divine essence and transitions to the next topic: the messengers.

Key Terms
الممكنات
الجواز
الوجوب
الاستحالة
النقيضين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that Allah must create certain things or cannot create others due to inherent necessities.
Correction: The proof shows that no contingent thing imposes necessity or impossibility on Allah; all contingents are equally within His power and choice.

Checkpoint
What would happen if a particular contingent thing were rationally necessary for Allah?
A) It would become impossible for Allah to do it.
B) All contingent things would become necessary.
C) Only that one thing would be necessary.
D) It would remain contingent.

Key Takeaway
No contingent action is rationally necessary or impossible for Allah; all contingents are expressions of His free will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you make du‘ā’, do you feel a sense of entitlement that Allah should answer because you have been faithful? If so, how does this lesson challenge that assumption, and how can you cultivate hope without expectation?
?
Is there any area of your life where you are acting as if a certain outcome is 'impossible' for Allah—something you've given up on? Reflect: if no contingent thing is impossible for Allah, what are you settling for that you should not?

Deriving Permanence from Self-Sufficiency
The author proves that God's self-sufficiency entails permanence (baqa'), the negation of subsequent non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's self-sufficiency logically necessitates permanence, and the precise definition of al-baqa' as the negation of subsequent non-existence.

Core Idea
God's self-sufficiency logically requires permanence, because any possibility of non-existence would imply need.

This passage presents a concise proof linking two divine attributes: self-sufficiency (al-ghina) and permanence (al-baqa'). The student will see how the impossibility of need in God entails that non-existence cannot overtake Him. The reasoning builds on the categories of contingent existence introduced earlier in the study of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the argument's structure become clear.
Notice that the passage is a single, tightly reasoned sentence.
2
Identify the definition of al-baqa'
Find where the author defines al-baqa' as 'the negation of subsequent non-existence.' This is the technical meaning in kalam.
It is not simply 'eternal,' but specifically the impossibility of ceasing to exist.
3
Trace the conditional proof
Follow the logic: if non-existence were possible → God would be contingent (ja'iz al-wujud) → contingent existents are originated (hadith) → they need an originator → need negates self-sufficiency. Since self-sufficiency is established, the initial possibility is impossible.
4
Connect to the broader kalam framework
Understand that this proof relies on the earlier classification of existence into necessary, contingent, and impossible. Permanence is a necessary attribute of the Necessary Being.
This lesson solidifies the relationship between self-sufficiency and permanence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و استغناؤه يوجب له تعالى البقاءً وهو عبارة عن انتفاء العدم اللاحق للوجود لأنه لو أمكن أن يلحقه العدم لكان جائز الوجود والجائز لا يكون وجوده إلا حادثاً، فيفتقر إلى محدث وينتفي عنه الغنى، ونفي الغنى عن الله تعالى محال لثبوته له عقلا ونقلا.
And His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him, exalted, permanence (al-baqa'), which is an expression of the negation of subsequent non-existence to existence. Because if it were possible for non-existence to overtake Him, He would be contingent in existence (ja'iz al-wujud), and that which is contingent cannot have existence except as originated, so He would need an originator, and need would negate His self-sufficiency. And the negation of self-sufficiency from Allah is impossible because it is established for Him by reason and transmission.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage derives the attribute of permanence (al-baqa') from God's self-sufficiency. Permanence is defined as the negation of non-existence after existence. The proof is that if it were possible for God to cease to exist, then He would be contingent (ja'iz al-wujud), meaning His existence is not necessary. But contingent beings are only existent as originated (hadith), and originated beings require an originator. This would imply need, contradicting self-sufficiency. Since self-sufficiency is established by reason and revelation, permanence is necessary.

Key Terms
البقاء
العدم اللاحق
جائز الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-baqa' (permanence) means simply 'eternal,' implying no beginning and no end.
Correction: In kalam, al-baqa' specifically refers to the negation of subsequent non-existence (after existence). The attribute of no beginning is called al-qidam (eternity). They are distinct concepts.

Checkpoint
What does 'al-baqa'' refer to in this passage?
A) The necessity of existence
B) The negation of prior non-existence
C) The negation of subsequent non-existence
D) The absence of all non-existence

Key Takeaway
God's self-sufficiency logically necessitates permanence, i.e., the impossibility of non-existence after existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's self-sufficiency means He cannot cease to exist, what does that reveal about your own tendency to seek security in things that can vanish? Are you trusting in anything that might 'cease' to be, while ignoring the One who is permanently self-sufficient?
?
The passage shows that any possibility of non-existence would imply need. In your life, do you ever act as if God's help might run out or His kindness might end? How does this truth challenge your fear and anxiety about the future?
?
Since self-sufficiency is proven by both reason and revelation, what is the practical implication for you when you feel Allah is not enough? Does your heart's reliance match what your mind knows about His permanence?

Dissimilarity Derived from Self-Sufficiency
Sanusi proves that self-sufficiency entails dissimilarity to all originated things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the attribute of self-sufficiency (al-ghina) necessarily entails dissimilarity to all created things (al-mukhalafah li al-hawadith) in essence, attributes, and actions.

Core Idea
God's self-sufficiency logically excludes any resemblance to originated things.

This lesson presents a crisp rational proof from the Sanusi matn. It directly connects the attribute of self-sufficiency to the doctrine of divine transcendence. The reasoning is concise: if God resembled any created thing, He would be like it—needing a creator—which contradicts His self-sufficiency. Understanding this logical chain is essential for grasping the Islamic concept of tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed logical style here.
2
Identify the proof structure
Break down the argument: self-sufficiency → if resemblance → then origination → then need → need contradicts self-sufficiency → therefore dissimilarity is necessary.
This is a conditional proof (qiyas sharti).
3
Reflect on the scope of dissimilarity
Note that the passage specifies dissimilarity in essence (dhat), attributes (sifat), and actions (af'al). Ask yourself why all three are mentioned.
This guards against limiting dissimilarity to only one aspect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستغناؤه يوجب له تعالى المخالفة للحوادث أي عدم المماثلة للحوادث في الذات والصفات والأفعال، لأنه لو ماثل شيئاً منها لكان حادثاً مثلها فيفتقر إلى محدث والافتقار ينافي الاستغناء ونفي الاستغناء عن الله تعالى محال لثبوته له عقلاً ونقلاً.
And His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him, exalted, dissimilarity to originated things (al-mukhalafah li al-hawadith), which is the absence of resemblance to originated things in essence, attributes, and actions. Because if He resembled any of them, He would be originated like them, and thus would need an originator, and need contradicts self-sufficiency. And the negation of self-sufficiency from Allah is impossible because it is established for Him by reason and transmission.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage derives the attribute of dissimilarity to originated things (al-mukhalafah li al-hawadith) from self-sufficiency. Dissimilarity is defined as the absence of resemblance to any created entity in essence, attributes, and actions. The proof is that if God resembled any originated thing, He would Himself be originated (hadith), since resemblance implies sharing in the property of being originated. An originated being needs an originator, which would entail need, contradicting self-sufficiency. Therefore, God must be unlike all created things.

Key Terms
المخالفة للحوادث
المماثلة
الذات
الصفات
الأفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think dissimilarity applies only to God's essence, not to His attributes or actions.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that dissimilarity covers essence (dhat), attributes (sifat), and actions (af'al).

Checkpoint
What does 'al-mukhalafah li al-hawadith' mean according to the passage?
A) God's independence from creation
B) God's lack of resemblance to created things in essence, attributes, and actions
C) God's transcendence above all deficiencies
D) God's self-subsistence

Key Takeaway
God's self-sufficiency logically entails His complete dissimilarity to all created things in essence, attributes, and actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining God in human-like terms or assigning Him limits? How does this proof challenge that habit, and what concrete step will you take this week to purify your conception of God?
?
If God is utterly self-sufficient and needs nothing from you, how does that reshape your understanding of worship? Do you treat prayer as if you are doing God a favor, or as an act of utter need from you?
?
When you feel distant from God, do you subconsciously think of Him as being 'far away' in a spatial sense? How does the doctrine of dissimilarity correct that notion? Write one specific reminder you can use during your next prayer to internalize His transcendence.

Deriving Self-Subsistence: Proof and Answer to Objection
Sanusi derives self-subsistence from self-sufficiency and answers a circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine attribute of self-subsistence is derived from self-sufficiency, and how the objection of circular reasoning is resolved.

Core Idea
Proving the specific from the general is valid, not circular.

This passage from Sanusi's work demonstrates a key inference in Islamic theology: from God's self-sufficiency we derive His self-subsistence. The author anticipates and resolves a potential objection about circular reasoning, clarifying the relationship between the two concepts. We will examine the proof, the objection, and the precise answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the flow of the argument become familiar.
2
Identify the proof that self-sufficiency implies self-subsistence
Notice how the author argues: if God needed a place or specifier, He would not be self-sufficient. Since self-sufficiency is established, self-subsistence follows.
3
Understand the objection: why would someone think this is circular?
The objector claims that self-subsistence itself means independence, so proving it by self-sufficiency seems to prove something by itself.
This is a common logical concern.
4
Learn the answer: how self-subsistence is a specific form of independence
The author explains that self-subsistence is independence from place and specifier only, while self-sufficiency is independence from everything else. The specific is proven by the general.
This distinction resolves the circularity.
5
Confirm the logical principle: proving the specific by the general is valid
Understand that in logic, if a general property holds, then any specific instance of that property also holds. Here, self-sufficiency is general, self-subsistence is specific.
This principle is sound and widely used.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و استغناؤه يوجب له تعالى القيام بالنفس الذي هو الاستغناء عن المحل والمخصص لأنه لو افتقر إلى محل أو مخصص لم يكن غنيا ونفي الغنى عن الله تعالى محال لثبوته له عقلاً ونقلاً. لا يقال القيام بالنفس هو الاستغناء ، فكيف يستدل على الشيء بنفسه؟ لأنا نقول القيام بالنفس خاص بالاستغناء عن المحل والمخصص فقط، والاستغناء عن كل ما سواه أعم، والاستدلال بالأعم على الأخص صحيح هنا .
And His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him, exalted, self-subsistence (al-qiyam bi al-nafs), which is independence from place (al-mahall) and specifier (al-mukhassis). Because if He were in need of a place or a specifier, He would not be self-sufficient. And the negation of self-sufficiency from Allah is impossible because it is established for Him by reason and transmission. It cannot be said: Self-subsistence is self-sufficiency, so how can something be proven by itself? Because we say: Self-subsistence is specific to independence from place and specifier only, while self-sufficiency from everything else is more general, and proving the specific by the general is valid here.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage derives the attribute of self-subsistence (al-qiyam bi al-nafs) from self-sufficiency. Self-subsistence is defined as independence from a place (al-mahall) and a specifier (al-mukhassis). The proof is that if God needed a place or a specifier, He would not be self-sufficient. Then the author anticipates an objection: Self-subsistence itself means independence, so proving it by self-sufficiency would be circular. The answer is that self-subsistence is a specific form of independence (independence from place and specifier), while self-sufficiency is general independence from everything else. Proving the specific by the general is valid, so the argument is sound.

Key Terms
القيام بالنفس
المحل
المخصص
استدلال الأعم على الأخص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-subsistence and self-sufficiency are identical, making the proof circular.
Correction: Self-subsistence is a specific type of independence (from place and specifier) while self-sufficiency is general independence from all else. Proving the specific by the general is valid.

Checkpoint
How does the author defend against the objection that deriving self-subsistence from self-sufficiency is circular?
A) He says self-subsistence is more general than self-sufficiency
B) He says self-subsistence is specific to independence from place and specifier, while self-sufficiency is general
C) He says the argument is not logical
D) He says self-sufficiency is not proven

Key Takeaway
Self-subsistence is proven from self-sufficiency by the valid logical principle of proving the specific from the general.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to believe that Allah is self-sufficient and independent of all things. But search your heart: is there a 'place' (job, status, relationship, home) that you secretly believe you cannot live without? What would your life look like if you truly internalized that Allah needs no place—and that your security lies in Him alone?
?
The objection in this passage accuses the proof of circularity. In your own life, do you sometimes use God's attributes as empty labels without letting them transform your trust? Have you ever truly relied on Allah's self-subsistence when the 'specifiers' of your life—health, wealth, approval—are stripped away?
?
The argument distinguishes general self-sufficiency from specific self-subsistence. Are you content with a general belief in Allah's independence, or are you willing to trust Him specifically for what you think you need most? What specific thing do you need to surrender to Him today?

Self-Sufficiency Necessitates Transcendence
The author proves that self-sufficiency entails transcendence and includes hearing, sight, speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how self-sufficiency logically entails transcendence from imperfections and why hearing, sight, and speech are included.

Core Idea
Self-sufficiency means no need; imperfection implies need, so God must be transcendent.

This passage demonstrates a key logical move in kalam: from God's self-sufficiency, we deduce His transcendence from all imperfections. The author also shows that certain attributes like hearing and sight are necessarily part of this transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the logical structure: if God were not transcendent, He would be described by imperfections and need someone to remove them, contradicting self-sufficiency.
2
Understand the proof
Identify the conditional reasoning: if not transcendent, then need; but self-sufficiency is established, so transcendence is necessary.
3
Connect to attributes of meanings
Notice that the author includes hearing, sight, and speech under transcendence. Why? Because these are perfections, and their absence would be imperfections.
The attributes of meanings necessitate the meaning-related states.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و استغناؤه يوجب له تعالى التنزه عن النقائص وهي الخصال الدنيئة، لأنه لو لم يكن منزهاً عنها ، لا تصف بها، فيحتاج إلى من يدفع عنه النقائص، والاحتياج ينافي الاستغناء ونفي الاستغناء عن الله محال لثبوته له عقلا ونقلا . ويدخل في وجوب التنزه عن النقائص وجوب السمع له تعالى والبصر والكلام ، ووجوب كونه سميعا وبصيراً ومتكلماً لأن وجود المعاني يستلزم وجود المعنوية. لاحظ أن ما يندرج من الصفات الواجبة تحت الاستغناء إحدى عشرة صفة ، واحدة نفسية وهي الوجود وأربعة سلبية وهي القدم والبقاء والمخالفة للحوادث والقيام بالنفس، وثلاثة معاني وهي السمع والبصر والكلام ، وثلاثة معنوية وهي كونه سميعاً وبصيراً ومتكلماً، وإذا ثبتت هذه الصفات انتفت أضدادها وهي إحدى عشرة أيضاً ، إذ لو لم تجب له هذه الصفات الإحدى عشرة وانتفت أضدادها لكان محتاجاً إلى المحدث وهذا استدلال على وجوب الوجود والقدم والبقاء والمخالفة للحوادث وأحد
And His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him, exalted, transcendence from imperfections (al-tanzih `an al-naqa'is), which are base characteristics. Because if He were not transcendent from them, He would be described by them, and would need someone to repel imperfections from Him, and need contradicts self-sufficiency. And the negation of self-sufficiency from Allah is impossible because it is established for Him by reason and transmission. And included in the necessity of transcendence from imperfections is the necessity of hearing, sight, and speech for Him, exalted, and the necessity of His being Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking, because the existence of the attributes (ma`ani) necessitates the existence of the attribute-related (ma`nawiyyah). Note that the necessary attributes that fall under self-sufficiency are eleven: one essential (nafsiyyah) attribute, which is existence; four negative (salbiyyah) attributes, which are eternity, permanence, dissimilarity to originated things, and self-subsistence; three attributes of meanings (ma`ani), which are hearing, sight, and speech; and three attribute-related (ma`nawiyyah) attributes, which are His being Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking. And when these attributes are established, their opposites are negated, which are also eleven. For if these eleven attributes were not necessary for Him and their opposites were not negated, He would be in need of an originator, and this is a proof for the necessity of existence, eternity, permanence, dissimilarity to originated things, and one...
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This final passage derives transcendence from imperfections (al-tanzih `an al-naqa'is) from self-sufficiency. Imperfections are defined as base characteristics. The proof is that if God were not free from defects, He would be described by them and would need someone to remove them, which implies need, contradicting self-sufficiency. The author then notes that included in this transcendence is the necessity of the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech, and their corresponding attribute-related states (being Hearing, Seeing, Speaking). The reasoning is that the existence of the meanings (ma`ani) necessitates the existence of the meaning-related (ma`nawiyyah). Finally, the author summarizes the total necessary attributes derived from self-sufficiency: eleven attributes — one essential (existence), four negative (eternity, permanence, dissimilarity, self-subsistence), three attributes of meaning (hearing, sight, speech), and three attribute-related (being Hearing, Seeing, Speaking). The negation of their opposites is also eleven. The argument concludes that if these attributes were not necessary, God would be in need of an originator. The text ends abruptly while listing the proof for existence, eternity, etc.

Key Terms
التنزه عن النقائص
السمع
البصر
الكلام
المعاني
المعنوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why must God be transcendent from imperfections?
A) Because imperfection is impossible for God
B) Because imperfection would imply need, contradicting self-sufficiency
C) Because scripture says so
D) Because transcendence is a philosophical concept

Key Takeaway
God's self-sufficiency logically demands complete transcendence from all imperfections, including the necessity of perfect attributes like hearing, sight, and speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does realizing He needs nothing challenge your tendency to ask only for worldly things? Commit to one dua today that acknowledges His independence above your own needs.

The Eleven Necessary Attributes
The author enumerates the eleven attributes and provides a proof by negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of the eleven necessary attributes derived from self-sufficiency and the proof that their negation would imply need.

Core Idea
The eleven attributes are essential for God's self-sufficiency; without them, God would need an originator.

This passage provides a neat summary of the attributes that necessarily follow from God's self-sufficiency. The author categorizes them into essential, negative, attributes of meanings, and meaning-related states, and then presents a proof that their opposites must be negated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classification
The attributes are grouped into four types: one essential, four negative, three of meanings, and three meaning-related. Find each in the text.

The proof is incomplete in the text but the logic is clear.

Verify that the total is eleven. The text lists them: existence, eternity, permanence, dissimilarity, self-subsistence, hearing, sight, speech, being Hearing, being Seeing, being Speaking.
The enumeration cuts off at 'وأحد' in Arabic but the translation fills it.

القيام بالنفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How many negative attributes are listed?
A) One
B) Four
C) Three
D) Eleven

Key Takeaway
The eleven necessary attributes are derived from self-sufficiency and their negation proves that God is not in need of an originator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were lacking any of these eleven attributes, He would need an originator. How does this realization change the way you view your own dependence on God for every moment of your existence?
?
The attribute of self-subsistence (al-qiyāmu bin-nafs) means God does not rely on anything. In your own life, what have you been relying on more than God, and how can you shift that trust starting today?
?
You affirm that God is unlike His creation, but do your thoughts about God sometimes resemble human limitations? What specific mental image or assumption about God do you need to repent from and replace with the truth of these attributes?
