Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
Eternity and Dissimilarity from Self-Sufficiency
Derivation of eternity and dissimilarity from self-sufficiency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's self-sufficiency rationally necessitates two attributes: eternity (al-baqā') and dissimilarity to originated things (al-mukhālafat li-l-ḥawādith).

Core Idea
Self-sufficiency demands that God be eternal and unlike anything created.

The author begins by showing that God's absolute self-sufficiency logically leads to specific attributes. If God were not eternal, He would be possible and need a creator, contradicting self-sufficiency. Similarly, if He resembled creation, He would be originated. This lesson explains these two derivations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice how each attribute is derived from the same premise.
2
Trace the proof for eternity
Identify the conditional chain: if non-being were possible → God would be possible → originated → need a creator → contradict self-sufficiency.
3
Trace the proof for dissimilarity
Identify the similar chain: if He resembled any originated thing → He would be originated → need a creator → contradict self-sufficiency.
4
Reflect on the shared structure
Both arguments use the same logical form: the opposite leads to contingency, which contradicts self-sufficiency. Notice how the author repeats the conclusion for emphasis.
This repetitive structure is pedagogical.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و استغناؤه يوجب له تعالى البقاءً وهو عبارة عن انتفاء العدم اللاحق للوجود لأنه لو أمكن أن يلحقه العدم لكان جائز الوجود والجائز لا يكون وجوده إلا حادثاً، فيفتقر إلى محدث وينتفي عنه الغنى، ونفي الغنى عن الله تعالى محال لثبوته له عقلا ونقلا. واستغناؤه يوجب له تعالى المخالفة للحوادث أي عدم المماثلة للحوادث في الذات والصفات والأفعال، لأنه لو ماثل شيئاً منها لكان حادثاً مثلها فيفتقر إلى محدث والافتقار ينافي الاستغناء ونفي الاستغناء عن الله تعالى محال لثبوته له عقلاً ونقلاً. و استغناؤه يوجب له تعالى القيام بالنفس الذي هو الاستغناء عن المحل والمخصص لأنه لو افتقر إلى محل أو مخصص لم يكن غنيا ونفي الغنى عن الله تعالى محال لثبوته له عقلاً ونقلاً. لا يقال القيام بالنفس هو الاستغناء ، فكيف يستدل على الشيء بنفسه؟ لأنا نقول القيام بالنفس خاص بالاستغناء عن المحل والمخصص فقط، والاستغناء عن كل ما سواه أعم، والاستدلال بالأعم على الأخص صحيح هنا . و استغناؤه يوجب له تعالى التنزه عن النقائص وهي الخصال الدنيئة، لأنه لو لم يكن منزهاً عنها ، لا تصف بها، فيحتاج إلى من يدفع عنه النقائص، والاحتياج ينافي الاستغناء ونفي الاستغناء عن الله محال لثبوته له عقلا ونقلا . ويدخل في وجوب التنزه عن النقائص وجوب السمع له تعالى والبصر والكلام ، ووجوب كونه سميعا وبصيراً ومتكلماً لأن وجود المعاني يستلزم وجود المعنوية. لاحظ أن ما يندرج من الصفات الواجبة تحت الاستغناء إحدى عشرة صفة ، واحدة نفسية وهي الوجود وأربعة سلبية وهي القدم والبقاء والمخالفة للحوادث والقيام بالنفس، وثلاثة معاني وهي السمع والبصر والكلام ، وثلاثة معنوية وهي كونه سميعاً وبصيراً ومتكلماً، وإذا ثبتت هذه الصفات انتفت أضدادها وهي إحدى عشرة أيضاً ، إذ لو لم تجب له هذه الصفات الإحدى عشرة وانتفت أضدادها لكان محتاجاً إلى المحدث وهذا استدلال على وجوب الوجود والقدم والبقاء والمخالفة للحوادث وأحد جزأي معنى القيام بالنفس الذي هو الاستغناء عن المخصص يعني لو انتفت هذه الصفات لكان محتاجاً إلى المحدث، واحتياجه تعالى محال لثبوت الغنى له تعالى عقلاً ونقلاً. أو لكان محتاجاً إلى المحل وهذا استدلال على وجوب الجزء الثاني من معنى القيام بالنفس وهو الاستغناء عن المحل، فلو لم يكن مستغنياً عن المحل لكان محتاجاً له والاحتياج ينافي الاستغناء، ونفي الاستغناء عنه تعالى محال، لوجوبه عقلاً ونقلاً. أو لكان محتاجاً إلى من يدفع عنه النقائص، وهذا استدلال على وجوب التنزه عن النقائص ومن جملة النقائص أضداد السمع والبصر والكلام، لأنه لو لم يكن منزهاً عن النقائص لكان محتاجا إلى من يدفع عنه النقائص والاحتياج ينافي الاستغناء ونفي الاستغناء عن الله تعالى محال لثبوته له عقلاً ونقلاً.
And His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him, exalted, eternity (al-baqā'), which is the negation of non-being (al-‘adam) that could follow existence, because if it were possible for non-being to follow Him, He would be possible of existence (jā’iz al-wujūd), and the possible only has its existence as originated (ḥādith), so He would need a creator and self-sufficiency would be negated from Him, and negation of self-sufficiency from God is impossible due to its establishment for Him by reason and revelation. And His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him, exalted, dissimilarity to originated things (al-mukhālafat li-l-ḥawādith), i.e., the absence of resemblance to originated things in essence, attributes, and actions, because if He resembled any of them, He would be originated like them, so He would need a creator, and need contradicts self-sufficiency, and negation of self-sufficiency from God is impossible due to its establishment for Him by reason and revelation. And His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him, exalted, self-subsistence (al-qiyām bi al-nafs), which is independence from a locus (maḥall) and a particularizer (mukhaṣṣiṣ), because if He were in need of a locus or particularizer, He would not be self-sufficient, and negation of self-sufficiency from God is impossible due to its establishment for Him by reason and revelation. It cannot be said: self-subsistence is self-sufficiency, so how can a thing be used to prove itself? Because we say: self-subsistence is specific to independence from a locus and a particularizer only, while self-sufficiency from everything else is more general, and using the general to prove the specific is valid here. And His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him, exalted, transcendence of defects (al-tanzīh ‘an al-naqā’iṣ), which are base traits, because if He were not transcendent of them, He would be described with them, and thus need someone to repel defects from Him, and need contradicts self-sufficiency, and negation of self-sufficiency from God is impossible due to its establishment for Him by reason and revelation. And included in the necessity of transcendence of defects is the necessity of hearing (al-sam‘), sight (al-baṣar), and speech (al-kalām) for Him, and the necessity of His being hearing, seeing, and speaking, because the existence of meanings necessitates the existence of meaning-bearing attributes. Note that what is included of the necessary attributes under self-sufficiency is eleven attributes: one essential (nafsī) which is existence (al-wujūd), four privative (salbiyya) which are pre-eternity (al-qidam), eternity, dissimilarity to originated things, and self-subsistence, three attributes of meaning (ma‘ānī) which are hearing, sight, and speech, and three meaning-bearing attributes (ma‘nawiyya) which are His being hearing, seeing, and speaking. And if these attributes are established, their opposites are negated, which are also eleven. For if these eleven attributes were not necessary for Him and their opposites were not negated, He would be in need of a creator, and this is an argument for the necessity of existence, pre-eternity, eternity, dissimilarity to originated things, and one part of the meaning of self-subsistence which is independence from a particularizer, meaning: if these attributes were negated, He would be in need of a creator, and His need is impossible due to the establishment of self-sufficiency for Him by reason and revelation. Or He would be in need of a locus, and this is an argument for the necessity of the second part of the meaning of self-subsistence, which is independence from a locus, for if He were not independent of a locus, He would be in need of it, and need contradicts self-sufficiency, and negation of self-sufficiency from Him is impossible due to its necessity by reason and revelation. Or He would be in need of someone to repel defects from Him, and this is an argument for the necessity of transcendence of defects, and among the defects are the opposites of hearing, sight, and speech, for if He were not transcendent of defects, He would need someone to repel defects from Him, and need contradicts self-sufficiency, and negation of self-sufficiency from God is impossible due to its establishment for Him by reason and revelation.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition and proof. It begins by deriving three necessary attributes from God's self-sufficiency (al-istighnā'): first, that He is eternal (al-baqā'), which is the negation of non-being that could follow existence. The argument: if non-being were possible for God, He would be a possible existent, and all possible existents are originated, thus needing a creator, contradicting self-sufficiency. Second, dissimilarity to originated things (al-mukhālafat li-l-ḥawādith): if He resembled any originated thing, He would be originated, again needing a creator. Third, self-subsistence (al-qiyām bi al-nafs): if He needed a locus or particularizer, He would not be self-sufficient. The author then addresses an objection: self-subsistence seems identical to self-sufficiency, so how can one prove the other? His answer is that self-subsistence is the specific form of independence from a locus and particularizer, while self-sufficiency is general independence from everything else; using the general to prove the specific is logically valid. Fourth, transcendence of defects (al-tanzīh ‘an al-naqā’iṣ): if He had defects, He would need someone to remove them, contradicting self-sufficiency. The passage then notes that under the necessity of transcendence of defects falls the necessity of the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech, and their corresponding meaning-bearing attributes (being hearing, etc.), because the existence of a meaning (ma‘nā) necessitates the existence of its bearer (ma‘nawiyya). Next comes an enumeration: the attributes included under self-sufficiency are eleven in total: one essential (existence), four privative (pre-eternity, eternity, dissimilarity to origins, self-subsistence), three attributes of meaning (hearing, sight, speech), and three meaning-bearing attributes. The passage concludes with a nested argument: if these eleven attributes are established, their opposites (eleven in number) are negated. The opposite situation would imply need: if these attributes were not necessary, God would be contingent upon a creator (establishing the first part of self-subsistence), or dependent on a locus (establishing the second part), or in need of a defect-remover (establishing transcendence). This logical chain is repeated to reinforce that impossibility.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of eternity (al-baqā') in this passage?
A) The continuation of existence without end
B) The negation of non-being that could follow existence
C) The absence of a beginning
D) The necessity of existence

Key Takeaway
God's self-sufficiency logically entails that He is eternal and unlike any created thing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's self-sufficiency means He is nothing like anything you have ever experienced, how does that challenge the way you imagine Him when you pray? What specific image or thought will you replace with a firm belief in His dissimilarity?
?
Consider a time you felt 'self-sufficient' in your own strength. According to this lesson, your sufficiency is always borrowed; only God's is absolute. How does that realization change how you view your own abilities and failures?
?
This proof uses reason to establish that self-sufficiency is impossible for any being other than God. Are you fully convinced that you are entirely dependent on Him every moment? What step can you take today to live out that dependency?

Divine Attributes

Self-Subsistence and Transcendence from Self-Sufficiency
Derivation of self-subsistence and transcendence, with an objection and inclusion of sensory attributes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how God's self-sufficiency necessitates self-subsistence (al-qiyām bi al-nafs) and transcendence of defects (al-tanzīh 'an al-naqā'iṣ), including the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech.

Core Idea
Self-sufficiency means God depends on nothing and is free from all defects.

The author continues deriving attributes from self-sufficiency. He argues that since God is self-sufficient, He cannot need a locus or particularizer (self-subsistence). Also, He cannot have defects; if He did, He would need someone to remove them. This lesson also covers an important objection about self-subsistence and shows how the necessity of transcendence implies the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation carefully, noting the objection and answer.
2
Understand the proof for self-subsistence
Follow the argument: need for a locus or particularizer would contradict self-sufficiency. Then see how the author answers the objection that self-subsistence seems identical to self-sufficiency.
The answer distinguishes specific vs. general independence.
3
Understand the proof for transcendence
If God had defects, He would need a remover. Also see how hearing, sight, speech are included under transcendence.
4
Reflect on the inclusion of sensory attributes
Note that transcendence includes these positive attributes because their opposites (deafness, blindness, muteness) are defects.

Source Text

ما يندرج من الصفات الواجبة تحت الاستغناء إحدى عشرة صفة ، واحدة نفسية وهي الوجود وأربعة سلبية وهي القدم والبقاء والمخالفة للحوادث والقيام بالنفس، وثلاثة معاني وهي السمع والبصر والكلام ، وثلاثة معنوية وهي كونه سميعاً وبصيراً ومتكلماً، وإذا ثبتت هذه الصفات انتفت أضدادها وهي إحدى عشرة أيضاً ، إذ لو لم تجب له هذه الصفات الإحدى عشرة وانتفت أضدادها لكان محتاجاً إلى المحدث وهذا استدلال على وجوب الوجود والقدم والبقاء والمخالفة للحوادث وأحد جزأي معنى القيام بالنفس الذي هو الاستغناء عن المخصص يعني لو انتفت هذه الصفات لكان محتاجاً إلى المحدث، واحتياجه تعالى محال لثبوت الغنى له تعالى عقلاً ونقلاً. أو لكان محتاجاً إلى المحل وهذا استدلال على وجوب الجزء الثاني من معنى القيام بالنفس وهو الاستغناء عن المحل، فلو لم يكن مستغنياً عن المحل لكان محتاجاً له والاحتياج ينافي الاستغناء، ونفي الاستغناء عنه تعالى محال، لوجوبه عقلاً ونقلاً. أو لكان محتاجاً إلى من يدفع عنه النقائص، وهذا استدلال على وجوب التنزه عن النقائص ومن جملة النقائص أضداد السمع والبصر والكلام، لأنه لو لم يكن منزهاً عن النقائص لكان محتاجا إلى من يدفع عنه النقائص والاحتياج ينافي الاستغناء ونفي الاستغناء عن الله تعالى محال لثبوته له عقلاً ونقلاً.
And His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him, exalted, eternity (al-baqā'), which is the negation of non-being (al-‘adam) that could follow existence, because if it were possible for non-being to follow Him, He would be possible of existence (jā’iz al-wujūd), and the possible only has its existence as originated (ḥādith), so He would need a creator and self-sufficiency would be negated from Him, and negation of self-sufficiency from God is impossible due to its establishment for Him by reason and revelation. And His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him, exalted, dissimilarity to originated things (al-mukhālafat li-l-ḥawādith), i.e., the absence of resemblance to originated things in essence, attributes, and actions, because if He resembled any of them, He would be originated like them, so He would need a creator, and need contradicts self-sufficiency, and negation of self-sufficiency from God is impossible due to its establishment for Him by reason and revelation. And His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him, exalted, self-subsistence (al-qiyām bi al-nafs), which is independence from a locus (maḥall) and a particularizer (mukhaṣṣiṣ), because if He were in need of a locus or particularizer, He would not be self-sufficient, and negation of self-sufficiency from God is impossible due to its establishment for Him by reason and revelation. It cannot be said: self-subsistence is self-sufficiency, so how can a thing be used to prove itself? Because we say: self-subsistence is specific to independence from a locus and a particularizer only, while self-sufficiency from everything else is more general, and using the general to prove the specific is valid here. And His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him, exalted, transcendence of defects (al-tanzīh ‘an al-naqā’iṣ), which are base traits, because if He were not transcendent of them, He would be described with them, and thus need someone to repel defects from Him, and need contradicts self-sufficiency, and negation of self-sufficiency from God is impossible due to its establishment for Him by reason and revelation. And included in the necessity of transcendence of defects is the necessity of hearing (al-sam‘), sight (al-baṣar), and speech (al-kalām) for Him, and the necessity of His being hearing, seeing, and speaking, because the existence of meanings necessitates the existence of meaning-bearing attributes. And if these attributes are established, their opposites are negated, which are also eleven. For if these eleven attributes were not necessary for Him and their opposites were not negated, He would be in need of a creator, and this is an argument for the necessity of existence, pre-eternity, eternity, dissimilarity to originated things, and one part of the meaning of self-subsistence which is independence from a particularizer, meaning: if these attributes were negated, He would be in need of a creator, and His need is impossible due to the establishment of self-sufficiency for Him by reason and revelation. Or He would be in need of a locus, and this is an argument for the necessity of the second part of the meaning of self-subsistence, which is independence from a locus, for if He were not independent of a locus, He would be in need of it, and need contradicts self-sufficiency, and negation of self-sufficiency from Him is impossible due to its necessity by reason and revelation. Or He would be in need of someone to repel defects from Him, and this is an argument for the necessity of transcendence of defects, and among the defects are the opposites of hearing, sight, and speech, for if He were not transcendent of defects, He would need someone to repel defects from Him, and need contradicts self-sufficiency, and negation of self-sufficiency from God is impossible due to its establishment for Him by reason and revelation.

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition and proof. It begins by deriving three necessary attributes from God's self-sufficiency (al-istighnā'): first, that He is eternal (al-baqā'), which is the negation of non-being that could follow existence. The argument: if non-being were possible for God, He would be a possible existent, and all possible existents are originated, thus needing a creator, contradicting self-sufficiency. Second, dissimilarity to originated things (al-mukhālafat li-l-ḥawādith): if He resembled any originated thing, He would be originated, again needing a creator. Third, self-subsistence (al-qiyām bi al-nafs): if He needed a locus or particularizer, He would not be self-sufficient. The author then addresses an objection: self-subsistence seems identical to self-sufficiency, so how can one prove the other? His answer is that self-subsistence is the specific form of independence from a locus and particularizer, while self-sufficiency is general independence from everything else; using the general to prove the specific is logically valid. Fourth, transcendence of defects (al-tanzīh ‘an al-naqā’iṣ): if He had defects, He would need someone to remove them, contradicting self-sufficiency. The passage then notes that under the necessity of transcendence of defects falls the necessity of the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech, and their corresponding meaning-bearing attributes (being hearing, etc.), because the existence of a meaning (ma‘nā) necessitates the existence of its bearer (ma‘nawiyya). Next comes an enumeration: the attributes included under self-sufficiency are eleven in total: one essential (existence), four privative (pre-eternity, eternity, dissimilarity to origins, self-subsistence), three attributes of meaning (hearing, sight, speech), and three meaning-bearing attributes. The passage concludes with a nested argument: if these eleven attributes are established, their opposites (eleven in number) are negated. The opposite situation would imply need: if these attributes were not necessary, God would be contingent upon a creator (establishing the first part of self-subsistence), or dependent on a locus (establishing the second part), or in need of a defect-remover (establishing transcendence). This logical chain is repeated to reinforce that impossibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Self-subsistence and self-sufficiency are the same thing, making the proof circular.
Correction: Self-subsistence is a specific kind of self-sufficiency (independence from a locus and particularizer), not identical. Using the general to prove the specific is logically valid.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that self-subsistence is the same as self-sufficiency?
A) He denies that self-subsistence is a kind of self-sufficiency
B) He says self-subsistence is specific to independence from a locus and particularizer, while self-sufficiency is more general
C) He argues that the proof is not circular because it uses reason and revelation
D) He says the objection is invalid because the premise is false

Key Takeaway
God's self-sufficiency logically requires that He be independent of all things and free from every defect, which includes positive attributes like hearing, sight, and speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You speak of God's self-sufficiency, but do you secretly believe that He needs your worship or obedience? How would your relationship with Him change if you truly lived knowing you are the only one in need?
?
If God is free from all defects, what does that say about your own complaints against His decree? Are you not, in essence, implying a defect in His plan? How can you repent from that subtle ingratitude?
?
Your heart craves independence from people and circumstances, yet you are utterly dependent on God for every breath. Does this lesson expose your pride? What would it look like to embrace your neediness before Him today?

Eleven Attributes Under Self-Sufficiency
Enumeration of eleven attributes and deduction of their opposites

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the complete enumeration of eleven necessary attributes derived from God's self-sufficiency and how their opposites are negated.

Core Idea
Self-sufficiency yields eleven attributes: one essential, four privative, three meanings, and three meaning-bearing.

After deriving individual attributes, the author steps back to list all attributes that fall under self-sufficiency. He counts eleven: existence (essential), four privative attributes (pre-eternity, eternity, dissimilarity, self-subsistence), three attributes of meaning (hearing, sight, speech), and three meaning-bearing attributes. He then shows that if these are established, their opposites (also eleven) are negated, reinforcing the original argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the enumeration and the categories.
2
Memorize the eleven attributes and their categories
Identify the one nafsī (essential), four salbiyya (privative), three ma‘ānī (meanings), and three ma‘nawiyya (meaning-bearing).
This classification is foundational in kalam.
3
Understand the negation of opposites
The author argues that if these eleven are established, their eleven opposites are negated. He then re-proves the same derivations by showing that the absence of these attributes would imply need.
4
Reflect on the logical pattern
Notice that the argument for opposites mirrors the earlier proofs: need contradicts self-sufficiency.

ونفي الاستغناء عن الله محال لثبوته له عقلا ونقلا . ويدخل في وجوب التنزه عن النقائص وجوب السمع له تعالى والبصر والكلام ، ووجوب كونه سميعا وبصيراً ومتكلماً لأن وجود المعاني يستلزم وجود المعنوية. لاحظ أن ما يندرج من الصفات الواجبة تحت الاستغناء إحدى عشرة صفة ، واحدة نفسية وهي الوجود وأربعة سلبية وهي القدم والبقاء والمخالفة للحوادث والقيام بالنفس، وثلاثة معاني وهي السمع والبصر والكلام ، وثلاثة معنوية وهي كونه سميعاً وبصيراً ومتكلماً، وإذا ثبتت هذه الصفات انتفت أضدادها وهي إحدى عشرة أيضاً ، إذ لو لم تجب له هذه الصفات الإحدى عشرة وانتفت أضدادها لكان محتاجاً إلى المحدث وهذا استدلال على وجوب الوجود والقدم والبقاء والمخالفة للحوادث وأحد جزأي معنى القيام بالنفس الذي هو الاستغناء عن المخصص يعني لو انتفت هذه الصفات لكان محتاجاً إلى المحدث، واحتياجه تعالى محال لثبوت الغنى له تعالى عقلاً ونقلاً. أو لكان محتاجاً إلى المحل وهذا استدلال على وجوب الجزء الثاني من معنى القيام بالنفس وهو الاستغناء عن المحل، فلو لم يكن مستغنياً عن المحل لكان محتاجاً له والاحتياج ينافي الاستغناء، ونفي الاستغناء عنه تعالى محال، لوجوبه عقلاً ونقلاً. أو لكان محتاجاً إلى من يدفع عنه النقائص، وهذا استدلال على وجوب التنزه عن النقائص ومن جملة النقائص أضداد السمع والبصر والكلام، لأنه لو لم يكن منزهاً عن النقائص لكان محتاجا إلى من يدفع عنه النقائص والاحتياج ينافي الاستغناء ونفي الاستغناء عن الله تعالى محال لثبوته له عقلاً ونقلاً.
And His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him, exalted, eternity (al-baqā'), which is the negation of non-being (al-‘adam) that could follow existence, because if it were possible for non-being to follow Him, He would be possible of existence (jā’iz al-wujūd), and the possible only has its existence as originated (ḥādith), so He would need a creator and self-sufficiency would be negated from Him, and negation of self-sufficiency from God is impossible due to its establishment for Him by reason and revelation. And His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him, exalted, dissimilarity to originated things (al-mukhālafat li-l-ḥawādith), i.e., the absence of resemblance to originated things in essence, attributes, and actions, because if He resembled any of them, He would be originated like them, so He would need a creator, and need contradicts self-sufficiency, and negation of self-sufficiency from God is impossible due to its establishment for Him by reason and revelation. And His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him, exalted, self-subsistence (al-qiyām bi al-nafs), which is independence from a locus (maḥall) and a particularizer (mukhaṣṣiṣ), because if He were in need of a locus or particularizer, He would not be self-sufficient, and negation of self-sufficiency from God is impossible due to its establishment for Him by reason and revelation. It cannot be said: self-subsistence is self-sufficiency, so how can a thing be used to prove itself? Because we say: self-subsistence is specific to independence from a locus and a particularizer only, while self-sufficiency from everything else is more general, and using the general to prove the specific is valid here. And His self-sufficiency necessitates for Him, exalted, transcendence of defects (al-tanzīh ‘an al-naqā’iṣ), which are base traits, because if He were not transcendent of them, He would be described with them, and thus need someone to repel defects from Him, and need contradicts self-sufficiency, and negation of self-sufficiency from God is impossible due to its establishment for Him by reason and revelation. And included in the necessity of transcendence of defects is the necessity of hearing (al-sam‘), sight (al-baṣar), and speech (al-kalām) for Him, and the necessity of His being hearing, seeing, and speaking, because the existence of meanings necessitates the existence of meaning-bearing attributes. Note that what is included of the necessary attributes under self-sufficiency is eleven attributes: one essential (nafsī) which is existence (al-wujūd), four privative (salbiyya) which are pre-eternity (al-qidam), eternity, dissimilarity to originated things, and self-subsistence, three attributes of meaning (ma‘ānī) which are hearing, sight, and speech, and three meaning-bearing attributes (ma‘nawiyya) which are His being hearing, seeing, and speaking. And if these attributes are established, their opposites are negated, which are also eleven. For if these eleven attributes were not necessary for Him and their opposites were not negated, He would be in need of a creator, and this is an argument for the necessity of existence, pre-eternity, eternity, dissimilarity to originated things, and one part of the meaning of self-subsistence which is independence from a particularizer, meaning: if these attributes were negated, He would be in need of a creator, and His need is impossible due to the establishment of self-sufficiency for Him by reason and revelation. Or He would be in need of a locus, and this is an argument for the necessity of the second part of the meaning of self-subsistence, which is independence from a locus, for if He were not independent of a locus, He would be in need of it, and need contradicts self-sufficiency, and negation of self-sufficiency from Him is impossible due to its necessity by reason and revelation. Or He would be in need of someone to repel defects from Him, and this is an argument for the necessity of transcendence of defects, and among the defects are the opposites of hearing, sight, and speech, for if He were not transcendent of defects, He would need someone to repel defects from Him, and need contradicts self-sufficiency, and negation of self-sufficiency from God is impossible due to its establishment for Him by reason and revelation.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition and proof. It begins by deriving three necessary attributes from God's self-sufficiency (al-istighnā'): first, that He is eternal (al-baqā'), which is the negation of non-being that could follow existence. The argument: if non-being were possible for God, He would be a possible existent, and all possible existents are originated, thus needing a creator, contradicting self-sufficiency. Second, dissimilarity to originated things (al-mukhālafat li-l-ḥawādith): if He resembled any originated thing, He would be originated, again needing a creator. Third, self-subsistence (al-qiyām bi al-nafs): if He needed a locus or particularizer, He would not be self-sufficient. The author then addresses an objection: self-subsistence seems identical to self-sufficiency, so how can one prove the other? His answer is that self-subsistence is the specific form of independence from a locus and particularizer, while self-sufficiency is general independence from everything else; using the general to prove the specific is logically valid. Fourth, transcendence of defects (al-tanzīh ‘an al-naqā’iṣ): if He had defects, He would need someone to remove them, contradicting self-sufficiency. The passage then notes that under the necessity of transcendence of defects falls the necessity of the attributes of hearing, sight, and speech, and their corresponding meaning-bearing attributes (being hearing, etc.), because the existence of a meaning (ma‘nā) necessitates the existence of its bearer (ma‘nawiyya). Next comes an enumeration: the attributes included under self-sufficiency are eleven in total: one essential (existence), four privative (pre-eternity, eternity, dissimilarity to origins, self-subsistence), three attributes of meaning (hearing, sight, speech), and three meaning-bearing attributes. The passage concludes with a nested argument: if these eleven attributes are established, their opposites (eleven in number) are negated. The opposite situation would imply need: if these attributes were not necessary, God would be contingent upon a creator (establishing the first part of self-subsistence), or dependent on a locus (establishing the second part), or in need of a defect-remover (establishing transcendence). This logical chain is repeated to reinforce that impossibility.

Divine Life Proven by Creation's Need
Proof of God's life from creation's neediness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dependence of everything on God logically requires that God be living (hayy).

Core Idea
Everything's need for God proves He is alive, not dead.

The author begins by establishing the foundational attribute of life. He argues that if God were not living, He could not cause existence, and nothing would depend on Him. But since all creation is in need of God, He must possess life. This sets the pattern for proving the other attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about life
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the proof of life. Notice the structure: the claim, the conditional reasoning, and the evidence from revelation and reason.
The Quranic verse and the argument from oneness are both used.
2
Understand the logic
The proof is based on the impossibility of the opposite (life vs. death). If God were dead, He could not be the source of existence, contradicting creation's neediness.
3
Connect to the larger argument
This is the first of four attributes proven by the same method. Recognize how the pattern repeats for power, will, and knowledge.
The conclusion of the passage ties all attributes together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما افتقار كل ما سواه إليه فهو يوجب له تعالى عقلاً الحياة أي كونه حياً، إذ لو لم يتصف بالحياة لما افتقر إليه شيء لأن الميت لا يُوجد شيئا ولا يفتقر إليه شيء، ونفي الافتقار إليه تعالى محال لثبوته له عقلاً ونقلاً، أما النقل فقوله تعالى ( يَتَأَيُّها النَّاسُ أَنتُمُ الْفُقَرَاءُ إِلَى اللَّهِ ﴾ [فاطر: ١٥)، وأما العقل فبرهان الوحدانية لأنه دل على أنه لا إله غيره. وافتقار كل ما سواه إليه يُوجب له تعالى عموم تعلق القدرة وعموم تعلقها بجميع الممكنات فلو لم تكن له قدرة عامة التعلق لاتصف بالعجز ولو كان عاجزا لما افتقر إليه كل ما سواه. وافتقار كل ما سواه إليه يوجب له الإرادة وعموم تعلقها بجميع الممكنات إذ لو لم تكن له إرادة عامة التعلق لاتصف بالكراهة ولو كان كارها لما افتقر إليه شيء ونفي الافتقار إليه تعالى محال لثبوته عقلاً ونقلاً. وافتقار كل ما سواه إليه يوجب له تعالى العلم وعموم تعلقه بجميع المعلومات ، لأنه لو اتصف بالجهل لما افتقر إليه شيء ونفي الافتقار إليه تعالى محال لثبوته له عقلاً .ونقلاً. وثبوت هذه الصفات يستلزم المعنوية وهي كونه قادراً وكونه مريداً وكونه عالماً.
As for the neediness (iftiqar) of everything other than Him for Him, it necessitates for Him, by reason, life (al-hayah), i.e., that He is living (hayy). For if He were not described with life, nothing would be in need of Him, because the dead does not bring anything into existence and nothing is in need of it. And the negation of the neediness of all things for Him is impossible, due to its establishment for Him by reason and tradition. As for tradition, His saying (may He be exalted): 'O mankind, you are the poor in need of Allah' [Fatir: 15]. As for reason, it is the proof of oneness (burhan al-wahdaniyyah), because it indicates that there is no god but Him. And the neediness of everything other than Him for Him necessitates for Him the comprehensive attachment of power (al-qudrah) and its comprehensive attachment to all possible things. For if He did not have power with comprehensive attachment, He would be described with incapacity ('ajz), and if He were incapable, not everything other than Him would be in need of Him. And the neediness of everything other than Him for Him necessitates for Him will (al-iradah) and its comprehensive attachment to all possible things. For if He did not have will with comprehensive attachment, He would be described with aversion (karahah), and if He were averse, nothing would be in need of Him. And the negation of the neediness of all things for Him is impossible, due to its establishment for Him by reason and tradition. And the neediness of everything other than Him for Him necessitates for Him knowledge (al-ilm) and its comprehensive attachment to all known things. For if He were described with ignorance (jahl), nothing would be in need of Him. And the negation of the neediness of all things for Him is impossible, due to its establishment for Him by reason and tradition. And the establishment of these attributes entails the state of being described by them (al-ma'aniyyah), namely that He is powerful (qadir), willing (murid), and knowing (alim).
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the observed fact that all creation is in need of God (iftiqar) to establish that God must possess certain attributes. The author begins with life: if God were not living, He could not cause things to exist and nothing would depend on Him, but dependence is undeniable both by revelation (Quran 35:15) and reason (the argument from oneness). Next, from universal neediness, God must have power (qudrah) that comprehensively attaches to all possible things; otherwise He would be incapable ('ajiz), and nothing would need Him. Similarly, He must have will (iradah) attaching to all possibles; otherwise He would be averse (karih), and nothing would need Him. He must have knowledge (ilm) of all known things; otherwise He would be ignorant (jahil), and nothing would need Him. The conclusion: these attributes (hayat, qudrah, iradah, ilm) are established, and they logically entail that God is described as qadir, murid, alim (the 'meaning' attributes or ma'ani sifat).

الافتقار
الحياة
حي

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument says if God were dead, nothing would depend on Him. Yet you often act as if your life depends on your own efforts or on others. When was the last time you realized you were relying on something other than the Living God? How will you redirect that reliance now?
?
The proof of oneness shows that only God deserves your dependence. Is there any person, possession, or plan that you have placed your ultimate trust in, other than Allah? Name it silently and resolve to return your dependence to Him alone today.

Divine Power Proven by Creation's Need
Proof of God's power from creation's neediness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dependence of everything on God necessitates that God has all-encompassing power (qudrah).

Core Idea
Universal need proves God's power is all-encompassing.

Building on the establishment of life, the author now proves that God must have power that extends to all possible things. The same method is used: if God lacked comprehensive power, He would be incapable, and then nothing would depend on Him. But everything does depend on Him, so He must be powerful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about power
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the proof of power. Notice the phrase 'عموم تعلق القدرة' meaning comprehensive attachment of power.
The argument mirrors that for life.
2
Analyze the conditional
The proof uses the opposite of power, which is incapacity ('ajz). If God were incapable, He could not be the object of need for all things.
3
Connect to the larger argument
This is the second attribute. Recognize the pattern: each attribute is proven by the impossibility of its opposite given universal need.
The Quranic and rational evidence for neediness is already established from the previous lesson.

Source Text

As for the neediness (iftiqar) of everything other than Him for Him, it necessitates for Him, by reason, life (al-hayah), i.e., that He is living (hayy). For if He were not described with life, nothing would be in need of Him, because the dead does not bring anything into existence and nothing is in need of it. And the negation of the neediness of all things for Him is impossible, due to its establishment for Him by reason and tradition. As for tradition, His saying (may He be exalted): 'O mankind, you are the poor in need of Allah' [Fatir: 15]. As for reason, it is the proof of oneness (burhan al-wahdaniyyah), because it indicates that there is no god but Him. And the neediness of everything other than Him for Him necessitates for Him the comprehensive attachment of power (al-qudrah) and its comprehensive attachment to all possible things. For if He did not have power with comprehensive attachment, He would be described with incapacity ('ajz), and if He were incapable, not everything other than Him would be in need of Him. And the neediness of everything other than Him for Him necessitates for Him will (al-iradah) and its comprehensive attachment to all possible things. For if He did not have will with comprehensive attachment, He would be described with aversion (karahah), and if He were averse, nothing would be in need of Him. And the negation of the neediness of all things for Him is impossible, due to its establishment for Him by reason and tradition. And the neediness of everything other than Him for Him necessitates for Him knowledge (al-ilm) and its comprehensive attachment to all known things. For if He were described with ignorance (jahl), nothing would be in need of Him. And the negation of the neediness of all things for Him is impossible, due to its establishment for Him by reason and tradition. And the establishment of these attributes entails the state of being described by them (al-ma'aniyyah), namely that He is powerful (qadir), willing (murid), and knowing (alim).

This passage is a proof from the observed fact that all creation is in need of God (iftiqar) to establish that God must possess certain attributes. Divine Will Proven by Creation's Need
Proof of God's will from creation's neediness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dependence of everything on God requires that God has all-encompassing will (iradah).

Core Idea
Universal need proves God's will is all-encompassing.

The author now applies the same logical pattern to prove that God has will. If God lacked comprehensive will, He would be averse (karih), and then nothing would depend on Him. But everything does depend on Him, so He must have will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about will
Read the Arabic passage focusing on the proof of will. Note the term 'الإرادة' and its opposite 'الكراهة'.
The argument is parallel to the previous ones.
2
Understand the opposite
The opposite of will is aversion (karahah). The author argues that if God were averse (i.e., unwilling), nothing would need Him.
3
Recognize the cumulative effect
Each attribute adds to the understanding of God's perfection. The universal need proves will just as it proved life and power.
The Quranic and rational evidence for need is consistent.

Divine Knowledge and Ma'ani Attributes
Proof of God's knowledge and conclusion on ma'ani attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dependence of everything on God necessitates that God has all-encompassing knowledge (ilm) and that these attributes imply the ma'ani attributes of being powerful, willing, and knowing.

Core Idea
Universal need proves God's knowledge and His essential descriptions.

The author completes the set of four attributes by proving knowledge in the same manner. He then concludes that the establishment of these attributes (life, power, will, knowledge) entails the 'meaning attributes' (al-ma'aniyyah): that God is described as powerful, willing, and knowing. This final step shows how the rational proofs lead to the attributes of essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about knowledge and conclusion
Read the Arabic passage covering knowledge and the final sentence about al-ma'aniyyah. Note the structure: proof of knowledge similar to previous, then summary.
The proof for knowledge uses ignorance (jahl) as the opposite.
2
Understand the proof for knowledge
If God were ignorant, He would not be the object of universal need. Since He is, He must know all things.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The four attributes together necessitate that God is qadir, murid, alim. This connects the attributes of the essence to the being of God.
Al-ma'aniyyah refers to the state of being described by these attributes.

Proof that God is Living from Universal Dependence
Sanusi proves God's life from universal dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for God's attribute of life based on the universal dependence of all creation upon Him.

Core Idea
Everything depends on God; therefore, God must be living.

This lesson examines one of the classical kalam proofs for the divine attribute of life. Imam Sanusi argues that the universal dependence of all contingent beings upon God necessarily implies that God is alive. The proof is both rational and scriptural. Let's walk through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own flow come through.
Notice the concise style of kalam arguments.
2
Identify the key conditional argument
The core argument is: If God were not living (i.e., dead), then nothing could depend on Him. But everything does depend on Him. Therefore, He must be living.
This is a modus tollens or contrapositive reasoning.
3
Examine the two supports for the premise
Sanusi gives two proofs that everything depends on God: rational (burhan al-wahdaniyyah) and revealed (Qur'an 35:15). Reflect on how each support reinforces the premise.
The rational proof of God's unity implies that all else is dependent on the one true God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وافتقار كل ما سواه إليه يوجب له تعالى عقلاً الحياة أي كونه حياً ، إذ لو لم يتصف بالحياة لما افتقر إليه شيء لأن الميت لا يُوجد شيئا ولا يفتقر إليه شيء، ونفي الافتقار إليه تعالى محال لثبوته له عقلاً ونقلاً، أما النقل فقوله تعالى ( يَتَأَيُّها النَّاسُ أَنتُمُ الْفُقَرَاءُ إِلَى اللَّهِ ﴾ [فاطر: ١٥)، وأما العقل فبرهان الوحدانية لأنه دل على أنه لا إله غيره.
And the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him necessitates for Him, may He be exalted, rationally life (al-hayah), that is, His being alive. For if He were not described with life, nothing would depend on Him, because the dead does not bring anything into existence and nothing depends on it. And the negation of dependence upon Him, may He be exalted, is impossible due to its being established for Him rationally and by transmission. As for transmission, it is His saying, Exalted is He: 'O mankind, you are the poor toward Allah' [Fatir: 15]. As for reason, it is the proof of oneness (burhan al-wahdaniyyah) because it indicates that there is no god but Him.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the attribute of life (al-hayah) for God, derived from the universal dependence of all contingent beings upon Him. The argument proceeds as follows: The fact that everything other than God depends on Him necessarily implies that God is living, because if He were not alive—specifically, if He were dead—then nothing would depend on Him, since a dead entity cannot cause anything to exist and thus nothing would be dependent upon it. But we know that everything does depend on God; this dependence is established both by revelation (Qur'an 35:15) and by reason (the proof of oneness, which shows that no god other than Him exists). Therefore, the negation of dependence on Him is impossible, and consequently, God must be living. The passage also hints that the rational proof for this dependence is the proof of God's unity, which itself demonstrates that all things are in need of the one true God.

Key Terms
الحياة
الافتقار
الوحدانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that God's life is not rationally necessary and is only known from revelation.
Correction: This passage shows that life is rationally necessary because universal dependence cannot be on a non-living being.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that God must be living?
A) Because all living things need a creator
B) Because if God were not living, nothing would depend on Him, but everything does depend on Him
C) Because life is a necessary attribute for any agent
D) Because the Qur'an explicitly says God is living

Key Takeaway
God's life is a necessary implication of the universal dependence of all creation upon Him.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything in your life, including your next breath, depends on God, why do you often live as though you are self-sufficient? What would change if you truly internalized this dependence moment by moment?
?
This passage shows that a dead thing cannot be depended upon. Are there any 'dead' things in your life—people, possessions, or achievements—that you are relying on for ultimate security? How can you shift your reliance to the Living God today?
?
The rational proof for God's life is built on the undeniable fact that you need Him. When was the last time you felt that deep poverty before Allah? If it has been a while, does that indifference accuse your faith? Will you let this lesson awaken a real sense of need for the Alive?

Proof of God's Power, Will, and Knowledge from Universal Dependence
Sanusi deduces God's power, will, and knowledge from universal dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the universal dependence of all things upon God necessitates His attributes of power, will, and knowledge.

Core Idea
Everything depends on Allah, so Allah must be powerful, willing, and knowing.

Sanusi presents an elegant proof: because everything other than Allah depends on Him, He must possess power, will, and knowledge in the most absolute sense. Each attribute is established by showing that its opposite would make universal dependence impossible. This passage demonstrates the intimate connection between the world's contingency and the perfection of its Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the parallel structure of the three proofs.
2
Identify the three attributes
Find the three attributes being proven: power, will, and knowledge. Observe how each proof follows the same pattern.
The pattern is: dependence necessitates attribute; without attribute, opposite would hold; opposite contradicts dependence.
3
Understand the logical necessity
Reflect on why the negation of dependence is impossible. The passage states it is established both rationally and by revelation.
4
Note the conclusion
The final sentence introduces the 'meaning-based states' (al-ma'nawiyyah) which are the actual descriptions of God resulting from these attributes.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to confirm your grasp of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وافتقار كل ما سواه إليه يُوجب له تعالى عموم تعلق القدرة وعموم تعلقها بجميع الممكنات فلو لم تكن له قدرة عامة التعلق لاتصف بالعجز ولو كان عاجزا لما افتقر إليه كل ما سواه.
وافتقار كل ما سواه إليه يوجب له الإرادة وعموم تعلقها بجميع الممكنات إذ لو لم تكن له إرادة عامة التعلق لاتصف بالكراهة ولو كان كارها لما افتقر إليه شيء ونفي الافتقار إليه تعالى محال لثبوته عقلاً ونقلاً.
وافتقار كل ما سواه إليه يوجب له تعالى العلم وعموم تعلقه بجميع المعلومات ، لأنه لو اتصف بالجهل لما افتقر إليه شيء ونفي الافتقار إليه تعالى محال لثبوته له عقلاً ونقلاً.
وثبوت هذه الصفات يستلزم المعنوية وهي كونه قادراً وكونه مريداً وكونه عالماً.
And the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him necessitates for Him, may He be exalted, the generality of the attachment of power (qudrah) and the generality of its attachment to all possible things. For if He did not have power with general attachment, He would be described with incapacity, and if He were incapacitated, everything other than Him would not depend on Him. And the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him necessitates for Him will (iradah) and the generality of its attachment to all possible things. For if He did not have will with general attachment, He would be described with aversion (karahah), and if He were averse, nothing would depend on Him. And the negation of dependence upon Him, may He be exalted, is impossible due to its being established for Him rationally and by transmission. And the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him necessitates for Him, may He be exalted, knowledge ('ilm) and the generality of its attachment to all known things. For if He were described with ignorance, nothing would depend on Him. And the negation of dependence upon Him, may He be exalted, is impossible due to its being established for Him rationally and by transmission. And the establishment of these attributes entails the attributive states (al-ma'nawiyyah), which are His being powerful (qadir), His being willing (murid), and His being knowing ('alim).
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a triple proof establishing three essential divine attributes—power, will, and knowledge—from the universal dependence of all contingent beings upon God. Each attribute is proven by a parallel argument: The dependence of everything on God requires that God have power with general attachment to all possible things; otherwise, He would be incapable (al-'ajz), and an incapable being cannot be the object of universal dependence. Similarly, universal dependence requires that God have will with general attachment; otherwise, He would be characterized by aversion (al-karahah), and an averse being would not be depended upon. Likewise, universal dependence requires that God have knowledge of all things; otherwise, He would be ignorant, and an ignorant being cannot be depended upon. In each case, the negation of dependence on God is impossible because it is proven by reason and revelation. The final sentence indicates that these three attributes imply the 'meaning-based' states (al-ma'nawiyyah) of being powerful, willing, and knowing, which are the actual descriptions of God.

Key Terms
القدرة
الإرادة
العلم
المعنوية
الكراهة
العجز
الجهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that power, will, and knowledge are not universally necessary for God.
Correction: The argument shows that their opposites (incapacity, aversion, ignorance) would make universal dependence impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would be the consequence if God did not have power with general attachment?
A) He would be ignorant
B) He would be described with incapacity, and nothing would depend on Him
C) He would be averse
D) He would still be depended upon

Key Takeaway
The universal dependence of all contingent beings on Allah directly proves that He possesses power, will, and knowledge without restriction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
This passage proves that Allah's will is general to all things. Have you ever acted as if your own effort, not His will, determines outcomes — for example, by worrying excessively or by taking credit? What concrete step can you take today to align your actions with the belief that His will is always supreme?

The Necessity of All Divine Attributes for Contingent Existence
Sanusi explains that if any divine attribute were missing, nothing contingent could exist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why every divine attribute is necessary for the existence of any contingent being.

Core Idea
Every contingent thing depends on all of Allah's attributes without exception.

Sanusi now builds on the listing of attributes to demonstrate their collective necessity. He presents a compelling argument that if even one attribute were missing, nothing contingent could exist. This lesson unpacks that argument and its foundation in the very nature of contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the conditional structure and the rhetorical question at the end.
2
Identify the main claim
Find the key claim: if any attribute or its implication is absent, then no contingent thing could exist. This is the conclusion Sanusi aims to prove.
3
Understand the reason given
Sanusi provides the reason: it is impossible for something dependent to exist without that upon which it depends. The cause of dependence is contingency (imkan) and temporal origination (huduth).
Reflect on why these two characteristics make a being inherently dependent.
4
Reflect on the closing rhetorical question
Sanusi asks: how could one negate dependence on God when everything else depends on Him for its initial existence and continued existence? This emphasizes the absurdity of the contrary view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو انتفى شيء من هذه الصفات أو مطلب من مطالبها وأحرى لو انتفى جميعها لما أمكن أن يوجد شيء من الحوادث، والعلة في ذلك استحالة وجود المتوقف بدون المتوقف عليه ، فلا يفتقر إليه شيء ، لأن علة الافتقار الإمكان والحدوث فلو انتفى شيء منها لما افتقر إليه شيء، كيف يتصور نفي الافتقار إليه تعالى وهو عز وجل يفتقر إليه كل ما سواه ابتداء بالإيجاد ودواماً بالإمداد.
And if any of these attributes or any of their implications were absent, and more so if all of them were absent, then it would not be possible for any of the contingent things to exist. The reason for that is the impossibility of the existence of that which is dependent without that upon which it depends. So nothing would depend on Him, because the cause of dependence is contingency (al-imkan) and temporal origination (al-huduth). So if any of them were absent, nothing would depend on Him. How can one conceive the negation of dependence upon Him, may He be exalted, while He, mighty and majestic, everything other than Him depends upon Him, initially by bringing into existence and perpetually by supplying?
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the indispensable necessity of all divine attributes for the existence of any contingent (hâdith) being. The argument is that the dependence of everything upon God is not arbitrary; it is grounded in the very nature of contingent beings—their 'imkan' (possibility) and 'huduth' (temporal origination). Therefore, if even a single attribute were missing, the entire structure of dependence would collapse, and nothing could exist. The author emphasizes the absurdity of denying this dependence, given that everything clearly depends on God for its initial creation and continued existence.

Key Terms
الصفات
مطالب
الحوادث
المتوقف
الإمكان
الحدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that not all divine attributes are essential for creation; perhaps only power and will are needed.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly argues that if any attribute were missing, no contingent thing could exist at all, because dependence is grounded in contingency and temporal origination, which require all attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the cause of dependence according to this passage?
A) The need for a creator
B) Contingency and temporal origination
C) The will of God
D) The power of God

Key Takeaway
All divine attributes are absolutely necessary; without any one, no contingent being could exist, because dependence arises from contingency itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Everything other than Allah depends on Him 'initially by bringing into existence and perpetually by supplying.' In what area of your life have you started acting as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting that even your continued existence requires every single attribute? What will you change today to acknowledge that dependence?

Proof of God's Oneness from Universal Dependence
Sanusi proves God's oneness from universal dependence via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's argument that universal dependence necessitates God's oneness in divinity, because a second god would be incapable and thus nothing would depend on Him.

Core Idea
If there were two gods, nothing would depend on either—so there can only be one.

Sanusi now builds a rigorous proof for tawhid (God's oneness) using a premise already established: everything other than God depends on Him. He shows that positing a second god leads to a logical contradiction—that nothing would depend on either—which is false. This lesson walks through that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage aloud, then read the English translation. Try to follow the logical flow of the argument before diving into analysis.
Notice how the argument uses a conditional: 'if there were a second...then nothing would depend...'
2
Identify the premise and the conditional
The passage starts with the premise of universal dependence. Then it introduces a hypothetical: if there were two gods, they would be incapable, so nothing would depend on them. Identify each part.
The incapacity arises whether they agree, disagree, or divide creation.
3
Understand why a second god leads to incapacity
If two gods exist, each would be limited—neither could be the sole source. Whether they cooperate, conflict, or divide tasks, each would depend on the other in some way. Thus both would be incapable of being the independent source of everything.
This is a key part of the argument: any form of duality entails limitation.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Since the assumption of a second god leads to the false conclusion that nothing depends on God, the assumption itself is false. Therefore God must be one in divinity (al-uluhiyyah).
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: from the false consequence, we reject the premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وافتقار كل ما سواه إليه جل وعلا يوجب له تعالى الوحدانية في الألوهية إذ لو كان معه ثان في الألوهية لما افتقر إليه شيء للزوم عجزهما حينئذ أي حين فرض وجودهما معا ويثبت العجز لهما سواء اتفقا أو اختلفا أو اقتسما ، لكن كونه لا يفتقر إليه شيء باطل لما سبق من وجوب افتقار كل ما سواه إليه ، كيف وهو جل وعلا الذي يفتقر إليه كل ما سواه ابتداء ودواما.
And the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him, glorious and exalted, necessitates for Him, may He be exalted, oneness (al-wahdaniyyah) in divinity (al-uluhiyyah). For if there were with Him a second in divinity, nothing would depend on Him due to the necessary existence of incapacity in both at that time—that is, when their existence together is assumed—and incapacity is established for both, whether they agree or differ or divide (the creation). But the notion that nothing depends on Him is false because of what preceded: the necessity of the dependence of everything other than Him upon Him. How (could it be otherwise) while He, glorious and exalted, is the One upon whom everything other than Him depends initially and perpetually?
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a proof for God's oneness (tawhid) from the universal dependence of all contingent beings. The argument is: If there were two gods sharing in divinity, then either they would cooperate, conflict, or divide the creation. In any case, both would be limited and thus incapable of being the sole source of dependence for everything. Consequently, nothing would depend on either of them. But we already know that everything does depend on God. Therefore, the assumption of a second god is false. The passage reaffirms that everything depends on God initially and continually, so He must be the one and only God.

Key Terms
الوحدانية
الألوهية
العجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Polytheism is possible; multiple gods can coexist.
Correction: This argument shows that multiple gods would be mutually limiting, making none of them worthy of absolute dependence, which contradicts the observed universal dependence.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that there cannot be two gods?
A) Because they would disagree
B) Because they would both be incapable, so nothing would depend on them
C) Because they would divide creation
D) Because the Qur'an says so

Key Takeaway
The universal dependence of all creation on God logically entails that there can be only one God, because a second would create incapacity and destroy the basis of dependence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life are you still acting as if someone or something other than God has ultimate control over your outcomes? How will you consciously transfer that trust to Him alone starting today?
?
The proof shows that true dependence on anything besides God is logically impossible. Is there a person, possession, or status you are relying on for your security or identity? What one concrete step will you take this week to detach from that false dependency?
?
If you fully internalized that nothing and no one can be depended upon except God, how would your daily anxieties change? Write down one specific worry right now and commit it to God, asking Him to replace your fear with reliance on Him.

Belief in Angels: Affirmation and Attributes
Definition and attributes of belief in angels with two proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what it means to believe in angels according to Sunni theology and the key attributes that this belief entails.

Core Idea
Belief in angels is integral to faith and requires affirming their obedience, emissary role, and countless numbers.

The declaration of Muhammad as Allah's Messenger implicitly requires belief in all the angels he was sent with. This passage from the Sanusiyyah explains exactly what that belief entails, from their existence to their obedience and their role as intermediaries. It also provides scriptural evidence for their vast numbers.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the literal translation. Notice the structure: the author lists several attributes before giving proofs.
2
Identify the seven attributes of angels
The passage enumerates attributes such as existence, honor, obedience, emissary role, management of creation, truthfulness, and countless numbers. List them as you find them.
These attributes define what belief in angels requires.
3
Examine the two proofs for their number
The author cites a Qur'anic verse and a hadith to demonstrate that only Allah knows the vast number of angels. Reflect on why this point is emphasized.
The proofs underscore the limit of human knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويدخل في قولنا محمد رسول الله ، الإيمان بسائر الملائكة ومعنى الإيمان بهم التصديق بوجودهم وأنهم مكرمون لا يعصون الله ما أمرهم ويفعلون ما يؤمرون وأنهم سفراء بين الله وبين خلقه متصرفين فيهم كما أذن لهم صادقون فيما أخبروا به، وأنهم بالغون من الكثرة ما لا يعلمه إلا الله ، قال تعالى ( وَمَا يَعْلَمُ جُنُودَ رَبِّكَ إِلَّا هُوَ ) المدثر : ۳۱) ، وقال : "أنت السماء وحق لها أن تنط، ما فيها موضع أربع أصابع إلا وملك واضع جبهته ساجدا الله (۲)."
And included in our statement 'Muhammad is the Messenger of Allah' is belief in all the angels. The meaning of belief in them is affirming their existence, that they are honored, they do not disobey Allah in what He commands them and they do what they are commanded, and that they are emissaries between Allah and His creation, managing them as He has permitted, truthful in what they convey, and that they are of a multitude known only to Allah. He, exalted, said: 'And none knows the hosts of your Lord except Him' (al-Muddaththir: 31), and he (the Prophet) said: 'The heaven groans and it has the right to groan; there is no space of four fingers except that an angel is placing his forehead prostrating to Allah' (2).
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a definition of belief in angels as part of the broader declaration of faith in Muhammad as the Messenger of Allah. The author explains that believing in angels requires affirming several attributes: (1) their existence; (2) they are honored (mukramun); (3) they never disobey Allah's commands; (4) they act as emissaries (sufara') between Allah and His creation; (5) they manage affairs as Allah permits; (6) they are truthful in what they convey; and (7) their vast numbers are known only to Allah. The author then provides two proofs: a Qur'anic verse from Surah al-Muddaththir stating that no one knows the hosts of the Lord except Him, and a hadith describing the heaven filled with angels prostrating, indicating their immense number and worship. The footnote marker (2) refers to the source of the hadith.

Key Terms
الملائكة (angels)
التصديق (affirmation)
سفراء (emissaries)
جنود ربك (hosts of your Lord)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think belief in angels is optional or independent of belief in the Prophet.
Correction: The passage shows that belief in angels is integral to affirming Muhammad as Messenger; it is included in the declaration.

Checkpoint

A) They can disobey Allah if they choose
B) They are honored and never disobey Allah's commands
C) They are equal to humans in status

Key Takeaway
Belief in angels requires affirming their existence, honor, perfect obedience, role as emissaries, truthfulness, and countless numbers known only to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Story of Harut and Marut: Refutation and Correct Belief
Refutation of Harut and Marut and correct interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the story of Harut and Marut does not contradict angelic infallibility and what the correct Islamic belief about them is.

Core Idea
The story of Harut and Marut is inauthentic; their teaching of magic was a warning, not a sin.

We have established that angels are infallible. However, a story about two angels named Harut and Marut seems to suggest otherwise. In this passage, the author addresses this apparent contradiction. He shows that the story is unreliable and explains the true purpose of their teaching of magic.

Guided Path
1
Reaffirm angelic infallibility
The author begins by restating that angels always obey Allah. Keep this principle in mind as you read.
2
Examine the authenticity of the story
Notice how the author dismisses the story as coming from Isra'iliyyat (Jewish/Christian sources) and lacking authentic hadith.
This is a key method in Islamic theology: rejecting unverified reports.
3
Understand the correct interpretation
The author explains that Harut and Marut taught magic not to practice it but to warn people and to distinguish magic from miracles. Reflect on why this interpretation preserves angelic integrity.
There is also a minority view that they were righteous humans, but the main point is the purpose of their teaching.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يعصون الله ما أمرهم ويفعلون ما يؤمرون ولا ينافي ذلك ما ينقل عن هاروت وماروت لأنه إنما ينقله المؤرخون عن الإسرائيليات أي كتب اليهود والنصارى، ولم يصح فيه خبر كما قاله المفسرون ، وما يذكره كذبة المؤرخين من أنهم عوقبا ومسخا كذب وزور لا يجوز اعتقاده، بل الذي يجب اعتقاده أن تعليمهما السحر لم يكن لأجل العمل به بل للتحذير منه ، وليظهر الفرق بينه وبين المعجزة، فإنه قد وقع أن السحرة كثروا بسبب استراق السمع وتعليمهم إياهم، فظن الجهلة أن معجزات الأنبياء، سحر، فأنزلهما الله تعالى ليعلما الناس كيفية السحر، ليظهر الفرق بينه وبينها، هذا كله بناء على أنهما كانا، ملكين، وقيل إنهما كانا رجلين صالحين وسميا ملكين لصلاحهما.(ج)
They do not disobey Allah in what He commands them and they do what they are commanded. And that does not contradict what is narrated about Harut and Marut, because it is only narrated by historians from the Isra'iliyyat, i.e., the books of the Jews and Christians, and no authentic report came concerning it as the exegetes said. And what the lying historians mention about their being punished and transformed is falsehood and lie, not permissible to believe. Rather, what must be believed is that their teaching of magic was not to practice it but to warn against it, and to manifest the difference between it and the miracle. For it happened that sorcerers increased due to eavesdropping and their teaching them, so the ignorant thought that the miracles of the prophets were magic. So Allah sent them down to teach people the nature of magic, to manifest the difference between it and miracles. All this is based on the view that they were two angels. And it is said that they were two righteous men and were called angels for their righteousness. (J)
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer defending the infallibility of angels against the story of Harut and Marut. The author begins by reasserting the general principle that angels do not disobey Allah. He then addresses the potential objection from the narrative about Harut and Marut, which suggests that two angels taught magic and were punished. The answer is that this story is not authentic; it comes from Isra'iliyyat (Jewish and Christian sources) and is not confirmed by any reliable hadith. The exegetes have stated this. The author goes further to condemn as falsehood the claims that they were punished and transformed into something. Instead, the correct belief is that their teaching of magic was intended as a warning and to distinguish miracles from magic. The historical context: because of eavesdropping on the heavenly conversation, sorcerers increased, and people confused miracles with magic. Therefore, Allah sent Harut and Marut to teach magic so that people could see the difference. The author notes that this interpretation assumes they were actually angels, though a minority view says they were righteous humans called angels metaphorically. The footnote initial (ج) indicates a source or author.

Key Terms
هاروت وماروت (Harut and Marut)
الإسرائيليات (Isra'iliyyat)
السحر (magic)
المعجزة (miracle)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The story of Harut and Marut proves that angels can sin and be punished.
Correction: The story is inauthentic (from Isra'iliyyat) and the correct understanding is that they taught magic as a warning, not as a sin.

Checkpoint
What is the correct view about Harut and Marut according to the author?
A) They were two angels who sinned and were punished
B) They taught magic to warn against it and to distinguish magic from miracles
C) They were idols worshipped by the polytheists
D) They were prophets

Key Takeaway
The story of Harut and Marut does not contradict angelic infallibility because it lacks authentication and the correct interpretation makes their teaching a warning, not a sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Iblis Is Not from the Angels
The author refutes the claim that Iblis was an angel using Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Iblis's disobedience does not contradict angelic infallibility and how the Quran clarifies his nature as jinn, not angel.

Core Idea
Iblis was jinn, not angel — angelic infallibility remains intact.

Some argue that because Iblis disobeyed Allah, angels are capable of sin, implying Iblis was an angel. The author refutes this by citing a Quranic verse that explicitly identifies Iblis as from the jinn, a separate species. This lesson examines that argument and the evidence that preserves the doctrine of angelic infallibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the author's tone as he declares the opposing view 'very weak, rather false.'
The verse quoted is from Surah al-Kahf (18:50).
2
Identify the claim and the counter-evidence
Find the claim that angels can sin based on Iblis, and then the Quranic verse the author uses to refute it. Understand why the verse is decisive.
The verse explicitly says 'he was of the jinn,' not angels.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why it matters that Iblis was jinn. How does this protect the doctrine of angelic infallibility? Also note the author's dismissal of the weak counter-argument that some angels could be called jinn.
The author emphasizes that there is no evidence for that claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن البعض قال إن الملائكة يجوز عليهم العصيان الله تعالى واستندوا في قولهم هذا إلى أن إبليس من الملائكة، وهو قول ضعيف جداً بل باطل، لأنه يخالف نص الآية الكريمة ( إلَّا إِبْلِيسَ كَانَ مِنَ الْجِنِّ فَفَسَقَ ) فهذا نص في أن إبليس لم يكن من الملائكة بل كان من الجن ونحن نعلم أن الجن هم جنس غير الملائكة ولا يندرجون تحتهم ولا وجه لمن قال إن بعض الملائكة يقال له جن، لأن هذا لا دليل عليه ، وهو خلاف الظاهر فتنبه (س).
And know that some said that angels are capable of disobedience to Allah, and they based this saying on the fact that Iblis is from the angels. This is a very weak, rather false, statement, because it contradicts the text of the noble verse: 'Except Iblis, he was of the jinn, so he transgressed' (Quran 18:50). So this is a clear text that Iblis was not from the angels but was from the jinn. And we know that the jinn are a species other than the angels and are not included under them. There is no justification for those who say that some angels are called jinn, because there is no evidence for that, and it is contrary to the apparent meaning. So be aware. (S)
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that angels can sin, based on the example of Iblis. Some people argue that because Iblis disobeyed Allah, angels are capable of sin, implying that Iblis was an angel. The author declares this view very weak and false. He provides the Quranic verse (Surah al-Kahf, 18:50) which explicitly states that Iblis was from the jinn, not angels. Therefore, his disobedience is not an example of angelic sin. The author then notes that jinn are a distinct species from angels, and there is no evidence to claim that some angels are called jinn. The conclusion is that the infallibility of angels stands, and the argument from Iblis is invalid. The footnote initial (س) indicates the source.

Key Terms
إبليس (Iblis)
الجن (jinn)
الملائكة (angels)
العصيان (disobedience)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume Iblis was an angel, so his disobedience proves angels can sin.
Correction: The Quran clearly states Iblis was from the jinn, a separate creation, thus angelic infallibility is preserved.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the author use to argue that Iblis was not an angel?
A) Consensus of scholars
B) The hadith about Harut and Marut
C) The Quranic verse 'إلَّا إِبْلِيسَ كَانَ مِنَ الْجِنِّ'
D) Logical deduction

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verse explicitly states Iblis was of the jinn, preserving the doctrine of angelic infallibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Quranic verse 'Except Iblis, he was of the jinn,' do you accept it as clear and decisive, or do you find yourself trying to reinterpret it to fit your own assumptions? What does that reveal about your heart's relationship with divine revelation?
?
The author ends with 'so be aware'—what specific awareness about accepting Allah's words as they are, without twisting, do you need to cultivate in your daily life, especially when the truth challenges popular or comfortable beliefs?
?
Consider that Allah protected angels from sin. Does this certainty increase your awe and love for them? How does it affect the way you seek their role in your protection and guidance, knowing they never disobey?

Divine Attributes
