The Obligation and the First Six Attributes
Sanusi states the obligation and lists the first six necessary attributes with classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the legal obligation to know God's attributes and the first six necessary attributes, classified as nafsiyya and salbiyya.

Core Idea
Knowledge of God's attributes begins with six that establish His existence and transcendence.

Sanusi begins by making it a legal obligation for every responsible person to learn what is necessary, impossible, and possible regarding God. He then lists the first six of the twenty necessary attributes, explaining the first as essential (nafsiyya) and the next five as privative (salbiyya). This classification shows how we affirm God's existence and remove imperfections from Him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the obligation
Read the Arabic and translation, noticing that Sanusi starts with the duty to know the necessary, impossible, and possible for God and messengers.
This sets the importance of the knowledge.
2
Identify the six attributes
List the six attributes: Existence, Pre-eternity, Permanence, Being unlike creation, Self-subsistence, Oneness.
3
Understand the classification
Learn that the first, Existence, is essential (nafsiyya), and the other five are privative (salbiyya) – they negate imperfections.
Privative attributes clarify what God is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيَجِبُ عَلَى كُلِّ مُكَلَّفٍ شَرْعًا أَنْ يَعْرِفَ مَا يَجِبُ فِي حَقِّ مَوْلَانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ وَمَا يَسْتَحِيلُ وَمَا يَجُوزُ، وَكَذَا يَجِبُ عَلَيْهِ أَنْ يَعْرِفَ مِثْلَ ذَلِكَ فِي حَقِّ الرُّسُلِ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ. فِيمَا يَجِبُ لِمَوْلَانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ عِشْرُونَ صِفَةً، وَهِيَ: الْوُجُودُ، وَالْقِدَمُ، وَالْبَقَاءُ، وَمُخَالَفَتُهُ تَعَالَى لِلْحَوَادِثِ، وَقِيَامُهُ تَعَالَى بِنَفْسِهِ: أَيْ لَا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى مَحَلٍّ وَلَا مُخَصِّصٍ، وَالْوَحْدَانِيَةُ: أَيْ لَا شَرِيكَ لَهُ فِي ذَاتِهِ وَلَا فِي صِفَاتِهِ وَلَا فِي أَفْعَالِهِ. فَالْأُولَى نَفْسِيَّةٌ وَهِيَ الْوُجُودُ، وَالْخَمْسَةُ بَعْدَهَا سَلْبِيَّةٌ. ثُمَّ يَجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى سَبْعُ صِفَاتٍ مُسَمَّاةٌ بِصِفَاتِ الْمَعَانِي، وَهِيَ: الْقُدْرَةُ، وَالْإِرَادَةُ - الْمُتَعَلِّقَانِ بِجَمِيعِ الْمُمْكِنَاتِ - وَالْعِلْمُ الْمُتَعَلِّقُ بِجَمِيعِ الْوَاجِبَاتِ وَالْجَائِزَاتِ وَالْمُسْتَحِيلَاتِ، وَالْحَيَاةُ وَهِيَ لَا تَتَعَلَّقُ بِشَيْءٍ، وَالسَّمْعُ وَالْبَصَرُ الْمُتَعَلِّقَانِ بِجَمِيعِ الْمَوْجُودَاتِ، وَالْكَلَامُ الَّذِي لَيْسَ بِحَرْفٍ وَلَا صَوْتٍ، وَيَتَعَلَّقُ بِمَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ الْعِلْمُ مِنَ الْمُتَعَلِّقَاتِ. ثُمَّ سَبْعُ صِفَاتٍ مُسَمَّاةٌ صِفَاتٍ مَعْنَوِيَّةً، وَهِيَ مُلَازِمَةٌ لِلسَّبْعِ الْأُولَى، وَهِيَ: كَوْنُهُ تَعَالَى قَادِرًا، وَمُرِيدًا، وَعَالِمًا، وَحَيًّا، وَسَمِيعًا، وَبَصِيرًا، وَمُتَكَلِّمًا.
It is legally obligatory upon every legally responsible person to know what is necessary in respect of our Lord, glory and majesty, and what is impossible and what is possible. Likewise it is obligatory upon him to know the like of that in respect of the messengers, peace and blessings be upon them. What is necessary for our Lord, glory and majesty, are twenty attributes: Existence, Pre-eternity, Permanence, His being unlike originated things, His being self‑subsistent – i.e., He does not need a substratum nor a specifier –, and Oneness – i.e., He has no partner in His essence, nor in His attributes, nor in His actions. The first is essential (nafsiyyah) and it is Existence, and the five after it are privative (salbiyyah). Then for Him, majestic, are necessary seven attributes called attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al‑maʿānī): Power and Will – both relate to all possibles –, Knowledge which relates to all necessaries, possibles and impossibles, Life which does not relate to anything, Hearing and Sight which relate to all existents, and Speech which is not a letter nor a sound and relates to whatever Knowledge relates to of things. Then seven attributes called moral attributes (ṣifāt maʿnawiyyah), which are concomitant with the first seven: that He is Powerful, Willing, Knowing, Living, Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage first states the obligation on every accountable person (mukallaf) to know what is necessary, impossible, and possible regarding God and the prophets. It then enumerates the twenty attributes necessary for God. The first attribute, Existence, is essential (nafsiyyah). The next five (pre‑eternity, permanence, otherness, self‑subsistence, oneness) are privative, meaning they negate imperfections. Then seven attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al‑maʿānī) are listed: Power, Will, Knowledge, Life, Hearing, Sight, Speech – each with its respective objects (e.g., Power and Will relate to all possibles, Knowledge relates to all necessaries, possibles and impossibles, etc.). Finally, seven moral attributes (ṣifāt maʿnawiyyah) are derived from the attributes of meanings: being Powerful, Willing, etc. These are called 'moral' because they describe the state of the essence as qualified by those attributes.

Key Terms
الوجود
القدم
البقاء
مخالفة الحوادث
قيام بالنفس
الوحدانية
الصفات النفسية
الصفات السلبية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'self-subsistence' means God exists in a place.
Correction: Self-subsistence means He does not need a substratum (place) nor a specifier.

Checkpoint
What is the classification of the first six attributes?
A) All are essential
B) First is essential, five are privative
C) First is privative, five are essential
D) All are privative

Key Takeaway
The first six attributes of God consist of one essential attribute (Existence) and five privative attributes that negate imperfections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that God's existence is essential and that His other attributes are negations of imperfections. Reflect on your own perceived imperfections: what do you need to negate from your understanding of God? When you think of God, do you ever picture Him having a location or resembling creation? How does this lesson confront those thoughts? What concrete step can you take today to purify your concept of God from such ideas?
?
The lesson states that it is obligatory to know these attributes. Are you content with simply memorizing them, or will you strive to internalize their meaning? Consider how you can actively use this knowledge in your worship—by affirming His existence and negating any likeness to creation when you say 'Allahu Akbar' in prayer. Will you make it a habit to consciously reflect on one of these attributes during each prayer?
?
Sanusi mentions that knowing the necessary, impossible, and possible for God is a religious duty. Does your life reflect this priority? Are you as concerned with knowing your Lord correctly as you are with daily routines? What would change if you took this duty as seriously as you take your other responsibilities? Write one practical change starting today.

The Seven Attributes of Meanings
Sanusi enumerates the seven attributes of meanings and specifies their objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al‑maʿānī) and how each relates to its specific objects.

Core Idea
God's attributes of meanings are real qualities that exist eternally and relate to specific categories of things.

After explaining the first six attributes, Sanusi moves to the seven attributes of meanings. These are real, eternal attributes that subsist in God's essence and each has a specific scope of relation. This lesson details them: Power and Will relate to all possibles, Knowledge to all categories, Life to nothing, Hearing and Sight to all existents, and Speech to all that Knowledge knows.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of seven attributes
Identify each attribute: Power, Will, Knowledge, Life, Hearing, Sight, Speech.
2
Note the objects of each
Notice which categories each attribute relates to. For example, Power and Will relate to all possible things.
3
Understand the uniqueness of each
Some attributes have universal relation, some specific. Life has no relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيَجِبُ عَلَى كُلِّ مُكَلَّفٍ شَرْعًا أَنْ يَعْرِفَ مَا يَجِبُ فِي حَقِّ مَوْلَانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ وَمَا يَسْتَحِيلُ وَمَا يَجُوزُ، وَكَذَا يَجِبُ عَلَيْهِ أَنْ يَعْرِفَ مِثْلَ ذَلِكَ فِي حَقِّ الرُّسُلِ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ. فِيمَا يَجِبُ لِمَوْلَانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ عِشْرُونَ صِفَةً، وَهِيَ: الْوُجُودُ، وَالْقِدَمُ، وَالْبَقَاءُ، وَمُخَالَفَتُهُ تَعَالَى لِلْحَوَادِثِ، وَقِيَامُهُ تَعَالَى بِنَفْسِهِ: أَيْ لَا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى مَحَلٍّ وَلَا مُخَصِّصٍ، وَالْوَحْدَانِيَةُ: أَيْ لَا شَرِيكَ لَهُ فِي ذَاتِهِ وَلَا فِي صِفَاتِهِ وَلَا فِي أَفْعَالِهِ. فَالْأُولَى نَفْسِيَّةٌ وَهِيَ الْوُجُودُ، وَالْخَمْسَةُ بَعْدَهَا سَلْبِيَّةٌ. ثُمَّ يَجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى سَبْعُ صِفَاتٍ مُسَمَّاةٌ بِصِفَاتِ الْمَعَانِي، وَهِيَ: الْقُدْرَةُ، وَالْإِرَادَةُ - الْمُتَعَلِّقَانِ بِجَمِيعِ الْمُمْكِنَاتِ - وَالْعِلْمُ الْمُتَعَلِّقُ بِجَمِيعِ الْوَاجِبَاتِ وَالْجَائِزَاتِ وَالْمُسْتَحِيلَاتِ، وَالْحَيَاةُ وَهِيَ لَا تَتَعَلَّقُ بِشَيْءٍ، وَالسَّمْعُ وَالْبَصَرُ الْمُتَعَلِّقَانِ بِجَمِيعِ الْمَوْجُودَاتِ، وَالْكَلَامُ الَّذِي لَيْسَ بِحَرْفٍ وَلَا صَوْتٍ، وَيَتَعَلَّقُ بِمَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ الْعِلْمُ مِنَ الْمُتَعَلِّقَاتِ. ثُمَّ سَبْعُ صِفَاتٍ مُسَمَّاةٌ صِفَاتٍ مَعْنَوِيَّةً، وَهِيَ مُلَازِمَةٌ لِلسَّبْعِ الْأُولَى، وَهِيَ: كَوْنُهُ تَعَالَى قَادِرًا، وَمُرِيدًا، وَعَالِمًا، وَحَيًّا، وَسَمِيعًا، وَبَصِيرًا، وَمُتَكَلِّمًا.
It is legally obligatory upon every legally responsible person to know what is necessary in respect of our Lord, glory and majesty, and what is impossible and what is possible. Likewise it is obligatory upon him to know the like of that in respect of the messengers, peace and blessings be upon them. What is necessary for our Lord, glory and majesty, are twenty attributes: Existence, Pre-eternity, Permanence, His being unlike originated things, His being self‑subsistent – i.e., He does not need a substratum nor a specifier –, and Oneness – i.e., He has no partner in His essence, nor in His attributes, nor in His actions. Then seven attributes called moral attributes (ṣifāt maʿnawiyyah), which are concomitant with the first seven: that He is Powerful, Willing, Knowing, Living, Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's speech is like human speech with letters and sounds.
Correction: God's speech is not a letter nor a sound, as stated in the text.

Checkpoint
Which attribute of meaning relates to all necessaries, possibles, and impossibles?
A) Power
B) Knowledge
C) Life
D) Speech

Key Takeaway
The seven attributes of meanings are real eternal qualities, each with a defined scope: Power and Will over possibles, Knowledge over all categories, Life without relation, Hearing and Sight over existents, and Speech over all that Knowledge knows.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God's Knowledge covers even impossibles (like a second god). Is there any situation you consider 'impossible' in your life that you have stopped trusting God for? How does this truth challenge your view of His power? Commit to praying about one such 'impossible' matter this week.
?
Will and Power relate only to possibles, meaning God does not will or do the impossible. When you ask Allah for something, do you really believe it is within the realm of possibility for Him? Or do you limit His power based on your own experience? Write down one request you have been hesitant to make and ask it tonight.
?
God's Speech is not like human speech—no letters, no sounds. Does your prayer reflect that you are addressing a Being whose 'speech' is beyond your comprehension, yet who speaks to you through the Qur'an? Do you listen to Him as a real communication, or just as a ritual? Resolve to read one verse today as if He is speaking directly to you.

The Seven Moral Attributes
Sanusi enumerates the seven moral attributes as concomitant with the attributes of meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seven moral attributes (ṣifāt maʿnawiyya) and their relationship to the attributes of meanings.

Core Idea
Moral attributes are the necessary consequences of God being qualified by the attributes of meanings.

Sanusi completes the twenty attributes with the seven moral attributes. These are not additional real qualities but rather statements that God is characterized by the attributes of meanings. They are called 'moral' because they describe God's state as being Powerful, Willing, etc., necessarily following from the existence of the attributes of meanings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the seven moral attributes
They are: being Powerful, Willing, Knowing, Living, Hearing, Seeing, Speaking.
2
Understand their nature
They are not separate attributes but descriptions necessarily consequent upon the attributes of meanings.
3
See the classification
They are the fourth category, following nafsiyya, salbiyya, and ma'ani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَيَجِبُ عَلَى كُلِّ مُكَلَّفٍ شَرْعًا أَنْ يَعْرِفَ مَا يَجِبُ فِي حَقِّ مَوْلَانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ وَمَا يَسْتَحِيلُ وَمَا يَجُوزُ، وَكَذَا يَجِبُ عَلَيْهِ أَنْ يَعْرِفَ مِثْلَ ذَلِكَ فِي حَقِّ الرُّسُلِ عَلَيْهِمُ الصَّلَاةُ وَالسَّلَامُ. فِيمَا يَجِبُ لِمَوْلَانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ عِشْرُونَ صِفَةً، وَهِيَ: الْوُجُودُ، وَالْقِدَمُ، وَالْبَقَاءُ، وَمُخَالَفَتُهُ تَعَالَى لِلْحَوَادِثِ، وَقِيَامُهُ تَعَالَى بِنَفْسِهِ: أَيْ لَا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى مَحَلٍّ وَلَا مُخَصِّصٍ، وَالْوَحْدَانِيَةُ: أَيْ لَا شَرِيكَ لَهُ فِي ذَاتِهِ وَلَا فِي صِفَاتِهِ وَلَا فِي أَفْعَالِهِ. فَالْأُولَى نَفْسِيَّةٌ وَهِيَ الْوُجُودُ، وَالْخَمْسَةُ بَعْدَهَا سَلْبِيَّةٌ. ثُمَّ يَجِبُ لَهُ تَعَالَى سَبْعُ صِفَاتٍ مُسَمَّاةٌ بِصِفَاتِ الْمَعَانِي، وَهِيَ: الْقُدْرَةُ، وَالْإِرَادَةُ - الْمُتَعَلِّقَانِ بِجَمِيعِ الْمُمْكِنَاتِ - وَالْعِلْمُ الْمُتَعَلِّقُ بِجَمِيعِ الْوَاجِبَاتِ وَالْجَائِزَاتِ وَالْمُسْتَحِيلَاتِ، وَالْحَيَاةُ وَهِيَ لَا تَتَعَلَّقُ بِشَيْءٍ، وَالسَّمْعُ وَالْبَصَرُ الْمُتَعَلِّقَانِ بِجَمِيعِ الْمَوْجُودَاتِ، وَالْكَلَامُ الَّذِي لَيْسَ بِحَرْفٍ وَلَا صَوْتٍ، وَيَتَعَلَّقُ بِمَا يَتَعَلَّقُ بِهِ الْعِلْمُ مِنَ الْمُتَعَلِّقَاتِ. ثُمَّ سَبْعُ صِفَاتٍ مُسَمَّاةٌ صِفَاتٍ مَعْنَوِيَّةً، وَهِيَ مُلَازِمَةٌ لِلسَّبْعِ الْأُولَى، وَهِيَ: كَوْنُهُ تَعَالَى قَادِرًا، وَمُرِيدًا، وَعَالِمًا، وَحَيًّا، وَسَمِيعًا، وَبَصِيرًا، وَمُتَكَلِّمًا.

Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage first states the obligation on every accountable person (mukallaf) to know what is necessary, impossible, and possible regarding God and the prophets. It then enumerates the twenty attributes necessary for God. The first attribute, Existence, is essential (nafsiyyah). The next five (pre‑eternity, permanence, otherness, self‑subsistence, oneness) are privative, meaning they negate imperfections. Then seven attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al‑maʿānī) are listed: Power, Will, Knowledge, Life, Hearing, Sight, Speech – each with its respective objects (e.g., Power and Will relate to all possibles, Knowledge relates to all necessaries, possibles and impossibles, etc.). Finally, seven moral attributes (ṣifāt maʿnawiyyah) are derived from the attributes of meanings: being Powerful, Willing, etc. These are called 'moral' because they describe the state of the essence as qualified by those attributes.

بصير
متكلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the moral attributes are additional real qualities independent of the attributes of meanings.
Correction: They are concomitant with (necessarily follow from) the attributes of meanings.

Checkpoint

B) 7
C) 20
D) 1

Key Takeaway
The seven moral attributes are necessary descriptions of God as Powerful, Willing, Knowing, Living, Hearing, Seeing, and Speaking, and they follow from the attributes of meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You recite 'Allah hears the one who praises Him' daily. But do you truly believe that the Hearing described here is necessary to His essence — that He cannot cease to hear your whispered du'a at 3 AM? If He must hear, why do you sometimes pray as if no one is listening?
?
The moral attributes are not optional extra features; they are necessary consequences of who Allah is. If He is necessarily Knowing, then He knows the excuses you make to skip Fajr. What does that knowledge demand of you tonight?
?
You claim to believe Allah is Qadir (Powerful) — a necessary attribute. Then why do you act as if your problems are too big for Him? Identify one fear you have been carrying alone, and commit to placing it before the One who is necessarily Powerful.

The Twenty Impossible Attributes: Opposites of the Necessary
Sanusi enumerates the twenty impossible attributes as opposites of the necessary ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the twenty impossible attributes are the exact opposites of the necessary attributes, and you will be able to identify them as safeguards against limiting God's perfection.

Core Idea
What is impossible for God is simply the opposite of what is necessary.

Sanusi now completes the picture of divine attributes by listing their opposites. Just as we affirm twenty necessary attributes, we must deny twenty impossible ones. This passage systematically enumerates them, showing that denying imperfections is part of affirming God's absolute perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the full list of opposites
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to see all twenty impossible attributes. Notice how they are grouped.
The list starts with non-existence, origination, and passing away.
2
Observe the structure of opposites
Each impossible attribute is the direct opposite of a necessary one from the previous lesson. For example, non-existence is opposite to existence.
3
Examine the detailed examples
For attributes like 'likeness to created things', Sanusi provides specific forms such as being a body or accident. Understand why each form is impossible for God.
These examples help avoid misunderstanding what 'likeness' means.
4
Connect to tawheed
Reflect on how denying these attributes strengthens tawheed. Affirming God's perfection requires negating all imperfections.
This completes the picture of God's uniqueness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَمِمَّا يَسْتَحِيلُ فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى عِشْرُونَ صِفَةً، وَهِيَ أَضْدَادُ الْعِشْرِينَ الْأُولَى، وَهِيَ: الْعَدَمُ، وَالْحُدُوثُ، وَطُرُوُّ الْعَدَمِ، وَالْمُمَاثَلَةُ لِلْحَوَادِثِ بِأَنْ يَكُونَ جِسْمًا أَوْ عَرَضًا يَقُومُ بِالْجِسْمِ، أَوْ يَكُونَ فِي جِهَةٍ لِلْجِسْمِ، أَوْ هُوَ جِهَةٌ، أَوْ يَتَقَيَّدَ بِمَكَانٍ أَوْ زَمَانٍ، أَوْ تَتَّصِفَ ذَاتُهُ الْعَلِيَّةُ بِالْحَوَادِثِ، أَوْ يُوصَفَ بِالصِّغَرِ أَوِ الْكِبَرِ، أَوْ يَتَّصِفَ بِالْأَغْرَاضِ فِي الْأَفْعَالِ أَوِ الْأَحْكَامِ. وَكَذَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ قَائِمًا بِنَفْسِهِ، بِأَنْ يَكُونَ صِفَةً يَقُومُ بِمَحَلٍّ، أَوْ يَحْتَاجَ إِلَى مُخَصِّصٍ. وَكَذَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى أَنْ لَا يَكُونَ وَاحِدًا، بِأَنْ يَكُونَ مُرَكَّبًا فِي ذَاتِهِ، أَوْ يَكُونَ لَهُ مُمَاثِلٌ فِي ذَاتِهِ أَوْ صِفَاتِهِ، أَوْ يَكُونَ مَعَهُ فِي الْوُجُودِ مُؤَثِّرٌ فِي فِعْلٍ مِنَ الْأَفْعَالِ. وَكَذَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى الْعَجْزُ عَنْ مُمْكِنٍ مَا، وَإِيجَادُ شَيْءٍ مِنَ الْعَالَمِ مَعَ كَرَاهَتِهِ لِوُجُودِهِ – أَيْ عَدَمِ إِرَادَتِهِ لَهُ – أَوْ مَعَ الدُّهُولِ أَوِ الْغَفْلَةِ، أَوْ بِالتَّعْلِيلِ، أَوْ بِالطَّبْعِ. وَكَذَا يَسْتَحِيلُ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى الْجَهْلُ وَمَا فِي مَعْنَاهُ بِمَعْلُومٍ، وَالْمَوْتُ، وَالصَّمَمُ، وَالْعَمَى، وَالْبَكَمُ. وَأَضْدَادُ الصِّفَاتِ الْمَعْنَوِيَّةِ وَاضِحَةٌ مِنْ هَذِهِ.
And from what is impossible in respect of Him, exalted, are twenty attributes, and they are the opposites of the first twenty. They are: non‑existence, origination, passing away of existence, likeness to originated things such as being a body or an accident subsisting in a body, or being in a direction of a body, or being a direction itself, or being restricted by place or time, or His exalted essence being described by originated attributes, or being described as small or big, or being described by purposes in actions or rulings. Likewise it is impossible for Him, exalted, that He should not be self‑subsistent, by being an attribute subsisting in a substratum, or needing a specifier. Likewise it is impossible for Him, exalted, that He should not be one, by being composite in His essence, or having a likeness in His essence or attributes, or there being with Him in existence an effecter in any action. Likewise it is impossible for Him, exalted, powerlessness from any possible, and bringing into existence any thing from the world while disliking its existence – i.e., not willing it –, or with forgetfulness or heedlessness, or by final cause, or by nature. Likewise it is impossible for Him, exalted, ignorance and what is in its meaning, death, deafness, blindness, and muteness. And the opposites of the moral attributes are clear from these.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage enumerates the twenty impossible attributes for God, which are the exact opposites of the twenty necessary ones. It first lists the opposites of the privative attributes: non‑existence (opposite of existence), origination (opposite of pre‑eternity), cessation (opposite of permanence), likeness to originated things (opposite of otherness), not being self‑subsistent (opposite of qiyām bi‑nafsihi), not being one (opposite of oneness). It elaborates on likeness to created things by specifying forms: being a body, an accident, occupying direction, being located in space/time, being subject to change, having size, or acting for purposes. For the opposites of the attributes of meanings and moral attributes, it lists inability (opposite of power), unwillingness, ignorance, death, deafness, blindness, muteness, and their equivalents. The passage concludes that the opposites of the moral attributes (e.g., not being powerful, not being willing) are clear from the ones already mentioned.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossible attributes are merely accidental defects that could be overlooked.
Correction: These attributes are logically impossible for God; denying them is essential to affirming His absolute perfection and uniqueness.

This lesson lists twenty impossibilities. Which of these, if you are honest, do you find hardest to fully accept in your heart? Is there a subtle belief that God might be indifferent or limited? Confront that and resolve this week to actively affirm His absolute perfection in your prayers.

Proof of God's Pre‑eternity
Sanusi proves that God must be pre‑eternal by ruling out temporal origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's proof that God must be pre‑eternal, and why the alternative (temporal origination) leads to absurdities.

Core Idea
If God had a beginning, He would need a creator — leading to an impossible chain.

Sanusi now turns to proving two attributes that follow directly from God's necessary existence. The first is pre‑eternity (qidam): that God has no beginning. He shows that if God were temporal, the very logic of causation would unravel into circularity or infinite regress — both impossible. This lesson explores that proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Sanusi structures the argument as a conditional chain: if not pre‑eternal, then temporal, then needing a creator, then leading to an absurdity.
The passage is concise; each step follows logically.
2
Identify the two impossible outcomes
Sanusi says that if God were temporal, it would lead to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). Understand what each means: circularity is where the creator is itself, and infinite regress is an endless chain of creators.
Both are impossible in Islamic theology.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Since the only alternative to pre‑eternity leads to absurdity, God must be pre‑eternal. Ask yourself: why is infinite regress impossible? This connects to the earlier proof of God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ الْقِدَمِ لَهُ تَعَالَى، فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ قَدِيمًا لَكَانَ حَادِثًا فَيَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى مُحْدِثٍ فَيَلْزَمُ الدَّوْرُ أَوِ التَّسَلْسُلُ. وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ الْبَقَاءِ لَهُ تَعَالَى، فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ أَمْكَنَ أَنْ يَلْحَقَهُ الْعَدَمُ انْتَفَى عَنْهُ الْقِدَمُ، لِكَوْنِ وُجُودِهِ حِينَئِذٍ جَائِزًا لَا وَاجِبًا، وَالْجَائِزُ لَا يَكُونُ وُجُودُهُ إِلَّا حَادِثًا، كَيْفَ وَقَدْ سَبَقَ قَرِيبًا وُجُوبُ قِدَمِهِ تَعَالَى وَبَقَائِهِ.
As for the proof of the necessity of pre‑eternity for Him, exalted, it is that if He were not pre‑eternal, He would be temporal and would need a creator, leading to circularity or infinite regress. As for the proof of the necessity of permanence for Him, exalted, it is that if non‑existence could attach to Him, pre‑eternity would be negated from Him, because His existence would then be possible, not necessary, and the possible's existence is only temporal. Moreover, the necessity of His pre‑eternity and permanence was already mentioned.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage gives two proofs: for pre‑eternity (qidam) and for permanence (baqāʾ). For pre‑eternity: if God were not pre‑eternal, He would be temporal (ḥādith) and would require a creator. That creator would either be Himself (circularity) or another, leading to an infinite regress – both impossible. Therefore God must be without beginning. For permanence: if it were possible for God to cease to exist, then His existence would be merely possible (jāʾiz) rather than necessary (wājib). But the existence of a possible thing is always temporal (i.e., it comes into existence after non‑existence). Since God is proven necessary, He cannot be possible, hence He cannot cease to exist. The passage also notes that the necessity of pre‑eternity and permanence was already established in the earlier enumeration of necessary attributes.

Key Terms
القدم
الدور
التسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Pre‑eternity means God is simply very old but had a beginning.
Correction: Pre‑eternity (qidam) means existence without beginning — God never came into being.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's proof, if God were temporal, what would necessarily follow?
A) Circularity only
B) Infinite regress only
C) Either circularity or infinite regress
D) Immediate acceptance of creation

Key Takeaway
God's pre‑eternity is proven by the impossibility of circularity or infinite regress in the causal chain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God is pre‑eternal and has no beginning, how would that change the way you think about your own dependency on Him? Write down one area of your life where you act as if you are self‑sufficient, and commit to acknowledging your need for God before you sleep tonight.
?
The argument shows that circularity and infinite regress are impossible. In your own life, are there any cycles of sin or bad habits that you keep repeating in a circular way, never breaking out? What would it mean to let God's eternality break that cycle?
?
If God's pre‑eternity means He is the only uncreated being, how does that challenge your tendency to attribute ultimate control to anything else—your job, your relationships, your health? Take a moment to repent for any hidden shirk in reliance on created things.

Proof of God's Permanence
Sanusi proves that God must be permanent by showing that the alternative would make Him contingent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's proof that God cannot cease to exist, because that would make His existence contingent and temporal.

Core Idea
If God could cease to exist, He would be merely possible — contradicting His necessity.

After establishing God's pre‑eternity, Sanusi proves His permanence (baqāʾ): that God will never cease to exist. He shows that if non‑existence were possible for God, then His existence would be merely possible (jāʾiz) rather than necessary. But possible things are always temporal — they come into being after non‑existence. Since God is necessary, He cannot be possible, so He must be permanent. This lesson breaks down that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Sanusi uses the concept of possibility (jawāz) to connect non‑permanence to temporality.
The proof relies on the earlier establishment of God's necessity.
2
Understand the key implication
If God could cease to exist, then His existence would be merely possible (jāʾiz), not necessary (wājib). But what is possible is inherently temporal — it begins and ends. This would contradict the earlier proof of pre‑eternity.
Possible existence always requires a cause and is temporal.
3
Appreciate the overall conclusion
Since God's existence is necessary, it cannot be possible. Therefore, non‑existence cannot attach to Him. He must be permanent.
Permanence is inseparable from necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

That creator would either be Himself (circularity) or another, leading to an infinite regress – both impossible. Therefore God must be without beginning. But the existence of a possible thing is always temporal (i.e., it comes into existence after non‑existence). Since God is proven necessary, He cannot be possible, hence He cannot cease to exist. ?

Proof of God's Otherness from Creation
Sanusi proves God is unlike originated things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God must be absolutely unlike all created beings.

Core Idea
If God resembled creation, He would be created—so He is other.

This lesson unpacks Sanusi's concise proof that God cannot resemble any originated thing. The argument is a simple conditional: resemblance would imply temporality, which contradicts the already established attributes of pre-eternity and permanence. Understanding this proof solidifies the Islamic concept of tanzih (transcendence).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text for the first proof carefully. Notice the conditional structure starting with 'law' (if).
The text is concise; each word carries logical weight.
2
Identify the logical structure
The proof is a conditional argument: if resemblance (مماثلة) then temporality (حداثة), which is impossible (محال) because of pre-eternity (قدم) and permanence (بقاء).
The impossibility of the consequent proves the impossibility of the antecedent.
3
Connect to prior knowledge
Recall the earlier lesson where God's pre-eternity and permanence were established. This proof relies on those attributes as premises.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that God's otherness is not just a theological claim but a rational necessity. He cannot be like anything in creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ مُخَالَفَتِهِ تَعَالَى لِلْحَوَادِثِ، فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ مَاثَلَ شَيْئًا مِنْهَا لَكَانَ حَادِثًا مِثْلَهَا، وَذَلِكَ مُحَالٌ لِمَا عَرَفْتَ قَبْلُ مِنْ وُجُوبِ قِدَمِهِ وَبَقَائِهِ. وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ قِيَامِهِ تَعَالَى بِنَفْسِهِ، فَلِأَنَّهُ تَعَالَى لَوِ احْتَاجَ إِلَى مَحَلٍّ لَكَانَ صِفَةً، وَالصِّفَةُ لَا تُوصَفُ بِصِفَاتِ الْمَعَانِي وَلَا بِالْمَعْنَوِيَّةِ، وَمَوْلَانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ يَجِبُ اتِّصَافُهُ بِهِمَا، فَلَيْسَ بِصِفَةٍ. وَلَوِ احْتَاجَ إِلَى مُخَصِّصٍ لَكَانَ حَادِثًا، كَيْفَ وَقَدْ قَامَ
As for the proof of the necessity of His being unlike originated things, exalted, it is that if He resembled any of them, He would be temporal like them, and that is impossible because of what you already know of the necessity of His pre‑eternity and permanence. As for the proof of the necessity of His being self‑subsistent, exalted, it is that if He needed a substratum, He would be an attribute, and an attribute cannot be described with attributes of meanings nor with moral attributes, and our Lord, glory and majesty, must be described with both, so He is not an attribute. And if He needed a specifier, He would be temporal, [especially] since [the proof] has established
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage provides two proofs. First, for God being unlike originated things (mukhālafatuhu lil‑ḥawādith): if God resembled any created thing, He would be temporal like them, contradicting His proven pre‑eternity and permanence. Therefore God is absolutely unlike His creation. Second, for God being self‑subsistent (qiyāmuhu bi‑nafsihi): if He needed a substratum (maḥall), He would be an attribute (ṣifa) subsisting in something else. But an attribute cannot itself be described with attributes of meanings (e.g., power, knowledge) or moral attributes (e.g., being powerful, knowing). Since God must be described with these, He cannot be an attribute; thus He does not need a substratum. Moreover, if He needed a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to determine His existence, He would be temporal, which contradicts His pre‑eternity. The text ends abruptly, but the argument is that the proofs already establish His pre‑eternity, so He cannot need a specifier.

Key Terms

Proof of God's Self‑Subsistence
Sanusi proves God is self‑subsistent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must be self‑subsistent—not needing a substratum or a specifier.

Core Idea
If God needed a locus, He would be an attribute—but attributes cannot have the attributes God must have.

This lesson presents Sanusi's proof that God exists independently, requiring no substrate (maḥall) and no external determiner (mukhaṣṣiṣ). The argument uses the nature of attributes: if God depended on a substratum, He would be a mere accident, which cannot be described with the essential attributes that God necessarily possesses.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the second proof from the source. Notice it contains two sub‑arguments: one about needing a substratum, and one about needing a specifier.
The text is incomplete at the end, but the core reasoning is clear.
2
Analyze the substratum argument
The author argues: if God needed a substratum, He would be an attribute. But an attribute cannot be described with attributes of meanings (like power, knowledge) or moral attributes (like being powerful, knowing). Since God must be described with these, He cannot be an attribute; therefore He does not need a substratum.
This shows God is not an accident inhering in something else.
3
Analyze the specifier argument
The second sub‑argument: if God needed a specifier to determine His existence, He would be temporal. But the text says 'how, when [the proof] has established' (the proof of pre-eternity). This is an appeal to the already proven pre-eternity.
The argument is cut off but the intent is clear.
4
Synthesize the meaning of self‑subsistence
Self‑subsistence means God does not depend on anything else for His existence—neither a locus nor a determining cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ مُخَالَفَتِهِ تَعَالَى لِلْحَوَادِثِ، فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ مَاثَلَ شَيْئًا مِنْهَا لَكَانَ حَادِثًا مِثْلَهَا، وَذَلِكَ مُحَالٌ لِمَا عَرَفْتَ قَبْلُ مِنْ وُجُوبِ قِدَمِهِ وَبَقَائِهِ. وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ قِيَامِهِ تَعَالَى بِنَفْسِهِ، فَلِأَنَّهُ تَعَالَى لَوِ احْتَاجَ إِلَى مَحَلٍّ لَكَانَ صِفَةً، وَالصِّفَةُ لَا تُوصَفُ بِصِفَاتِ الْمَعَانِي وَلَا بِالْمَعْنَوِيَّةِ، وَمَوْلَانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ يَجِبُ اتِّصَافُهُ بِهِمَا، فَلَيْسَ بِصِفَةٍ. وَلَوِ احْتَاجَ إِلَى مُخَصِّصٍ لَكَانَ حَادِثًا، كَيْفَ وَقَدْ قَامَ
As for the proof of the necessity of His being unlike originated things, exalted, it is that if He resembled any of them, He would be temporal like them, and that is impossible because of what you already know of the necessity of His pre‑eternity and permanence. As for the proof of the necessity of His being self‑subsistent, exalted, it is that if He needed a substratum, He would be an attribute, and an attribute cannot be described with attributes of meanings nor with moral attributes, and our Lord, glory and majesty, must be described with both, so He is not an attribute. And if He needed a specifier, He would be temporal, [especially] since [the proof] has established
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage provides two proofs. First, for God being unlike originated things (mukhālafatuhu lil‑ḥawādith): if God resembled any created thing, He would be temporal like them, contradicting His proven pre‑eternity and permanence. Therefore God is absolutely unlike His creation. Second, for God being self‑subsistent (qiyāmuhu bi‑nafsihi): if He needed a substratum (maḥall), He would be an attribute (ṣifa) subsisting in something else. But an attribute cannot itself be described with attributes of meanings (e.g., power, knowledge) or moral attributes (e.g., being powerful, knowing). Since God must be described with these, He cannot be an attribute; thus He does not need a substratum. Moreover, if He needed a specifier (mukhaṣṣiṣ) to determine His existence, He would be temporal, which contradicts His pre‑eternity. The text ends abruptly, but the argument is that the proofs already establish His pre‑eternity, so He cannot need a specifier.

الصفة
المحل
المخصص
صفات المعاني

A) A body
B) An attribute
C) A possible
D) A creator

Key Takeaway
God's self‑subsistence means He does not inhere in anything nor is He determined by any external cause.

The Necessity of Power, Will, Knowledge, and Life
Sanusi proves the necessity of four essential attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for why Allah must possess the attributes of power, will, knowledge, and life.

Core Idea
If any essential attribute were missing, nothing contingent could exist.

Sanusi begins by establishing the most fundamental divine attributes—those without which no created thing could exist. This lesson unpacks his concise proof, showing how the reality of creation itself demands these attributes in the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic once to get the rhythm, then the translation for meaning. Notice how Sanusi states the conditional: 'if any were absent, none would exist'.
2
Identify the conditional structure
The proof is a conditional: if any of these four attributes were missing, then no contingent thing would exist. Since contingent things do exist, the antecedent must be false—the attributes are present.
This is a modus tollens argument.
3
Reflect on each attribute's necessity
Consider why each attribute (power, will, knowledge, life) is needed for creation. Power to bring into existence, will to choose, knowledge to design, life as a condition.
These attributes are essential for any agent that creates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ اتِّصَافِهِ تَعَالَى بِالْقُدْرَةِ وَالْإِرَادَةِ وَالْعِلْمِ وَالْحَيَاةِ فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوِ انْتَفَى شَيْءٌ مِنْهَا لَمَا وُجِدَ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْحَوَادِثِ. وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ السَّمْعِ لَهُ تَعَالَى وَالْبَصَرِ وَالْكَلَامِ فَالْكِتَابُ وَالسُّنَّةُ وَالْإِجْمَاعُ، وَأَيْضًا لَمَّا اتَّصَفَ بِهَا لَزِمَ أَنْ يُوصَفَ بِأَضْدَادِهَا، وَهِيَ النَّقَائِصُ، وَالنَّقْصُ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى مُحَالٌ. وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ كَوْنِ فِعْلِ الْمُمْكِنَاتِ أَوْ تَرْكِهَا جَائِزًا فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ وَجَبَ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى شَيْءٌ مِنْهَا عَقْلًا أَوِ اسْتَحَالَ عَقْلًا لَانْقَلَبَ الْمُمْكِنُ وَاجِبًا أَوْ مُسْتَحِيلًا. وَذَلِكَ لَا يُعْقَلُ.
As for the proof of the necessity of His being described with power, will, knowledge, and life, exalted, because if any of them were absent, nothing of the originated things would exist. As for the proof of the necessity of hearing, sight, and speech for Him, exalted, it is the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus. Also, if He were not described with them, He would necessarily be described with their opposites, which are deficiencies, and deficiency upon Him, exalted, is impossible. As for the proof that doing or leaving possible things is permissible in His right, exalted, because if any of them were intellectually obligatory or impossible for Him, then the possible would turn into obligatory or impossible, and that is inconceivable.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents proofs for necessary attributes and the permissibility of divine acts. First, the proof for the essential attributes of power (qudra), will (irāda), knowledge (ʿilm), and life (ḥayāh): the existence of originated things (ḥawādith) requires these attributes. For instance, power is needed to create, will to choose, knowledge to direct, and life as a condition for these attributes. If any were absent, nothing could exist. Second, for the attributes of hearing (samʿ), sight (baṣar), and speech (kalām), the proof is from revelation: the Qur’an, Sunnah, and consensus establish them. Additionally, if God lacked these, He would possess their opposites, which are deficiencies, and deficiency is impossible for God. Third, the proof that God's actions (doing or leaving any possible thing) are permissible (jā’iz) rather than obligatory or impossible: if any possible act were mandatory or impossible for God, then the possible would be transformed into necessary or impossible, which is absurd. Hence, God is free to act or not act regarding any contingent matter.

Key Terms
القدرة
الإرادة
العلم
الحياة
الحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These attributes are only known through revelation, not reason.
Correction: Sanusi provides a rational proof from the existence of contingent things, showing that these attributes are rationally necessary.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

The Necessity of Hearing, Sight, and Speech
Sanusi proves the necessity of three attributes via revelation and the impossibility of their opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand both the revealed and rational proofs for Allah's attributes of hearing, sight, and speech.

Core Idea
God must have hearing, sight, and speech, or else He would be deficient.

While some attributes can be proven solely by reason, others require revelation as well. Sanusi demonstrates that hearing, sight, and speech are proven both by the Qur'an and Sunna and by the logical impossibility of their opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully. Notice that Sanusi gives two distinct proofs: first from revelation, then from rational argument.
2
Identify the two types of proof
The first proof is scriptural: 'the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus.' The second is rational: if Allah lacked these attributes, He would possess their opposites (deafness, blindness, muteness), which are deficiencies.
This shows how transmitted and rational proofs complement each other.
3
Understand the rational conditional
The conditional is: if Allah did not have hearing, sight, and speech, then He would have their opposites, which are imperfections. Since imperfection is impossible for Allah, He must have these attributes.
This argument relies on the axiom that God is perfect.

Source Text

The Permissibility of Divine Actions
Sanusi proves that divine actions are neither obligatory nor impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for why Allah's actions regarding possible things are purely permissible, not obligatory or impossible.

Core Idea
If any divine action were necessary or impossible, the possible would cease to be possible.

A common misconception is that God is compelled to create or act in certain ways. Sanusi refutes this with a precise rational argument, showing that if any divine act were necessary or impossible, that would transform the very nature of possibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the conditional statement: 'if any of them were intellectually obligatory or impossible...'
2
Understand the concept of 'possible' (mumkin)
'Mumkin' refers to contingent things that can exist or not exist. The proof presupposes that the objects of divine action (created things) are possible in themselves.
The possibility is intrinsic to the thing, not to God's action.
3
Analyze the reductio
Assume a possible thing is obligatory for God: then it would be necessary, contradicting its possibility. Assume it is impossible: then it would be impossible for God to do, also contradicting its possibility. Therefore, divine actions regarding possible things are neither obligatory nor impossible—they are permissible (jā'iz).
This argument safeguards divine freedom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ اتِّصَافِهِ تَعَالَى بِالْقُدْرَةِ وَالْإِرَادَةِ وَالْعِلْمِ وَالْحَيَاةِ فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوِ انْتَفَى شَيْءٌ مِنْهَا لَمَا وُجِدَ شَيْءٌ مِنَ الْحَوَادِثِ. وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ السَّمْعِ لَهُ تَعَالَى وَالْبَصَرِ وَالْكَلَامِ فَالْكِتَابُ وَالسُّنَّةُ وَالْإِجْمَاعُ، وَأَيْضًا لَمَّا اتَّصَفَ بِهَا لَزِمَ أَنْ يُوصَفَ بِأَضْدَادِهَا، وَهِيَ النَّقَائِصُ، وَالنَّقْصُ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى مُحَالٌ. وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ كَوْنِ فِعْلِ الْمُمْكِنَاتِ أَوْ تَرْكِهَا جَائِزًا فِي حَقِّهِ تَعَالَى فَلِأَنَّهُ لَوْ وَجَبَ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى شَيْءٌ مِنْهَا عَقْلًا أَوِ اسْتَحَالَ عَقْلًا لَانْقَلَبَ الْمُمْكِنُ وَاجِبًا أَوْ مُسْتَحِيلًا. وَذَلِكَ لَا يُعْقَلُ.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Divine Attributes
beginner
5 min
Proof for Hearing, Sight, and Speech
Sanusi provides scriptural and rational proofs for three divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine attributes of hearing, sight, and speech are proven by both revelation (Qur'an, Sunnah, consensus) and reason (denial entails deficiency).

Core Idea
Denying God's hearing, sight, or speech is rationally impossible because it implies deficiency.

After establishing that God possesses necessary attributes, Sanusi addresses three specific attributes: hearing, sight, and speech. The proof combines scriptural authority with a rational argument: if God lacked these, He would have their opposites, which are deficiencies, and deficiency is impossible for God.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice the two parts: first the scriptural evidence, then the rational argument.
The passage is dense but contains a complete argument.
2
Identify the two proofs
The first proof is from the Book, Sunnah, and consensus. The second is a rational proof based on the impossibility of deficiency. Notice how Sanusi connects them with 'and also' (wa aydan).
3
Understand the rational argument
The rational proof is a conditional: if God lacked hearing, sight, and speech, He would have their opposites (deafness, blindness, muteness), which are deficiencies. Since deficiency is impossible for God, the attributes must exist.
This uses the principle that attributes and their opposites are mutually exclusive; absence of one entails presence of the other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا بُرْهَانُ وُجُوبِ السَّمْعِ لَهُ تَعَالَى وَالْبَصَرِ وَالْكَلَامِ فَالْكِتَابُ وَالسُّنَّةُ وَالْإِجْمَاعُ، وَأَيْضاً لَوْ لَمْ يَتَّصِفْ بِهَا لَزِمَ أَنْ يَتَّصِفَ بِأَضْدَادِهَا، وَهِيَ نَقَائِصُ، وَالنَّقْصُ عَلَيْهِ تَعَالَى مُحَالٌ.
And as for the proof for the necessity of hearing, seeing, and speech for Him, exalted is He, it is the Book, the Sunnah, and consensus; and also if He were not characterized by them, He would necessarily be characterized by their opposites, which are deficiencies, and deficiency upon Him, exalted is He, is impossible.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage addresses the proof for the divine attributes of hearing (sam'), sight (basar), and speech (kalam). Sanusi first cites the primary sources of Islamic knowledge: the Qur'an, the Sunnah, and scholarly consensus (ijma') as evidence. He then provides a rational argument: if God were not characterized by these attributes, He would necessarily be characterized by their opposites (e.g., deafness, blindness, muteness). These opposites are deficiencies (naqa'is), and deficiency is impossible for God. Thus, the three attributes are necessary.

Key Terms
سَمْع
بَصَر
كَلَام
كِتَاب

نَقَائِص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think these attributes are only known from scripture and have no rational basis.
Correction: Sanusi provides a rational proof: denial of these attributes implies deficiency, which is impossible for God, so reason also requires these attributes.

Checkpoint
What would necessarily follow if God were not characterized by hearing, sight, and speech?
A) He would be characterized by their opposites, which are deficiencies.
B) He would be non-existent.
C) He would be unable to create.
D) Nothing, because these attributes are not essential.

Key Takeaway
God must have hearing, sight, and speech; denying them entails deficiency, which is impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think of God's hearing and sight, do you truly live as if He sees your every action and hears every word? What specific area of your life would change if you internalized this truth today?
?
The proof shows that denying these attributes implies deficiency in God. Have you ever, even unconsciously, assumed God is indifferent or unaware of your struggles? How does this rational argument call you to repent of that assumption?
?
If you were asked to defend the belief that God speaks with real speech, using both scripture and reason from this lesson, could you do it? What would it cost you to learn this proof well enough to teach it to someone else this week?
